## Page 5

ABSTRACT On behalf of the Texas Department of Transportation (TxDOT), SWCA conducted staged mitigation at site 41DN612, within the area of potential effects (APE) of the proposed Farm-to-Market (FM) 407 Project in Denton County, Texas (CSJ: 1310-01-027). As the project will involve federal funds from the Federal Highway Administration (FHWA) and involves state land that will be controlled by the Dallas District of TxDOT, investigations were conducted in compliance with the Antiquities Code of Texas; the National Historic Preservation Act; the Programmatic Agreement between the FHWA, the Advisory Council on Historic Preservation (ACHP), TxDOT, and the Texas Historical Commission (THC); and the Memorandum of Understanding between TxDOT and the THC. Kevin Hanselka served as Principal Investigator (PI) under Antiquities Permit No. 8872. TxDOT Dallas District is proposing to replace the bridge and approaches on Denton Creek, and replace the culvert at Denton Creek Slough, along FM 407 east of Justin in Denton County, Texas. The total project length is approximately 0.6 mile. A total of 3.047 acres of new right-of-way (ROW) have been acquired for the project and no easements are required. Typical depth of impacts for roadway construction would be about 2.2 feet; maximum depth of impacts would be approximately 20 feet for bridge supports. Roadside ditches would have impacts up to 5 feet below existing ground surface. Based largely on safety concerns related to the existing dangerous curve at this location (to be resolved by realignment during the proposed construction), the project is on an accelerated schedule. The site is a stratified Transitional Archaic occupation situated on a broad flat floodplain of Denton Creek and Denton Creek Slough, an abandoned channel of Denton Creek. SWCA and its subcontractor conducted the data recovery investigations of 41DN612 from April 14 to June 22, 2019; geomorphological assessments were conducted during the last week of fieldwork. Field investigations consisted of a combination of mechanical and hand excavation of all site deposits within the APE. Excavations terminated at 5 feet, as depths of project impacts would not exceed 5 feet. Overall, SWCA excavated 310.1 m3 of sediment from the site. Based on the results of the various analyses, particularly the chronometric analyses, the prehistoric deposits at 41DN612 appear to be primarily associated with the period commonly recognized as the Transitional Archaic, although there is a slight overlap with the Late Prehistoric I period. During these excavations, seven cultural features were exposed, including a large earth oven facility (Feature A) and six isolated burned rock features (Features 1–6), interpreted to be single- or limited-use cooking features. A total of 519 artifacts were collected during the investigations, including 209 historic artifacts and 310 prehistoric artifacts. The historic artifacts were primarily within a large refuse pit that truncated portions of the Feature A. The prehistoric assemblage consisted of faunal remains (155 mussel shells and 99 faunal bone) and burned clay (n=21). No lithic tools or debitage were recovered during the field investigations or during flotation or fine-screening of the feature matrix. In addition, SWCA documented 6,429 pieces of burned rock during the investigations and collected 176 special samples. The excavations were limited to the APE and the exposure afforded only a partial glimpse of the overall site. Based on the assemblage, the site is interpreted as a seasonal (likely spring and summer), ephemeral campsite utilized primarily for hunting/foraging and cooking activities, as indicated by the flora and faunal i NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 6

assemblage and associated site furniture. Small groups exploited the riparian zone resources, making forays into the landscape to hunt and forage. Over time, there was an increase in land use intensification as evidenced by the single- or limited-use hearths being replaced by earth oven hot rock cooking technology to process lower-ranked resources (e.g., geophytes and starches). Overall, previous studies and literature reviews show a paucity of burned rock features within the Trinity River basin (e.g., Black et al. 1997; Lintz et al. 2008; Prikryl 1990; Yates and Ferring 1986). In a report presented by Geo-Marine, a literature review of the Texas Archeological Sites Atlas Database was conducted to compare the occurrence of thermal features reported within the Trinity River and Brazos River basins (Lintz et al. 2008). Lintz et al. (2008) reported that thermal features are rarely reported within the Trinity River basin and twice as likely to be reported within the Brazos River basin. This suggests that the occurrence of seven distinct features at 41DN612 is significant and may provide valuable information on the regional history of the Trinity River basin during the Transitional Archaic period. The smaller hearth features identified at 41DN612 are typical of the small burned rock features encountered within the Trinity River basin during the Late Archaic to early Late Prehistoric (Black et al. 1997; Lintz et al. 2008; Prikryl 1990; Tinsley et al. 2011). Interestingly, larger burned rock features and earth ovens are particularly rare, making the occurrence of Feature A rather unique. In concurrence with the previous survey recommendations, the Hardeman Midden site is considered eligible for designation as a State Antiquities Landmark and for the National Register of Historic Places. The early and late Transitional Archaic components revealed intact, well-preserved archeological deposits that significantly contributed to our understanding of Transitional Archaic patterns. These investigations mitigated the project- specific effects, not the entire site. Any future project that could impact deeper deposits (i.e., deeper than 5 feet), or those beyond the current ROW, warrant further consideration to assess the possibilities for additional contributing components. With these considerations, no further work is recommended. ii NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 13

Chapter 1. Introduction On behalf of the Texas Department of Transportation (TxDOT), SWCA conducted staged mitigation at site 41DN612, within the area of potential effects (APE) of the proposed Farm-to-Market (FM) 407 Project in Denton County, Texas (CSJ: 1310-01-027) (Figures 1.1 and 1.2). As the project will involve federal funds from the Federal Highway Administration (FHWA) and involves state land that will be controlled by the Dallas District of TxDOT, investigations were conducted in compliance with the Antiquities Code of Texas; the National Historic Preservation Act (NHPA); the Programmatic Agreement between the FHWA, the Advisory Council on Historic Preservation (ACHP), TxDOT, and the Texas Historical Commission (THC); and the Memorandum of Understanding between TxDOT and the THC. Kevin Hanselka served as Principal Investigator (PI) under Antiquities Permit No. 8872. Project Background TxDOT Dallas District is proposing to replace the bridge and approaches on Denton Creek, and replace the culvert at Denton Creek Slough, along FM 407 east of Justin in Denton County, Texas (see Figure 1.1). The proposed project would replace and widen the existing structurally deficient bridge at Denton Creek, bridge class culvert at Denton Creek Slough, and roadway approaches including the addition of a culvert extension just north of the bridge (see Figure 1.2). The existing radius of the curve between the culvert and the bridge would be increased in order to provide a safer approach to the two structures. The roadway would be shifted by as much as 80 feet from the center of the existing curve to the center of the proposed curve. Stone riprap would be added for erosion control. The vertical alignment of the roadway would be raised by 3 to 5 feet and that of the bridge would be raised by 5 to 8 feet to meet hydraulic standards. The total project length is approximately 0.6 mile. A total of 3.047 acres of new right-of-way (ROW) have been acquired for the project and no easements are required. Typical depth of impacts for roadway construction would be about 2.2 feet; maximum depth of impacts would be about 20 feet for bridge supports. Roadside ditches would have impacts up to 5 feet below existing ground surface. Based largely on safety concerns related to the existing dangerous curve at this location (to be resolved by realignment during the proposed construction), the project is on an accelerated schedule. Previous Investigations at 41DN612 On behalf of TxDOT, Raba Kistner performed an intensive archeological survey from January to March 2019 within the project APE, employing pedestrian survey, 10 shovel tests, and 15 backhoe trenches (BHTs) (Neel and Meléndez 2019). Buried prehistoric cultural materials were encountered in a BHT on the far northern end of the project APE, and four more BHTs were excavated nearby to delineate the span of these remains. The cultural remains were ultimately exposed in three BHTs (i.e., BHTs 10, 11, and 12), all within existing ROW of FM 407. Of note, modern and historic debris (e.g., glass, metal, brick) was encountered within a 72-centimeter (cm)-thick fill layer within BHT 10; however, the fill was approximately 22–36 cm above the prehistoric cultural features and did not appear to have impacted the integrity of the cultural features. All other subsurface investigations throughout the APE were negative for cultural remains. 1 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 16

Site 41DN612 was initially recorded as a prehistoric site consisting of four small scatters of fire-cracked rock (FCR), burned and unburned faunal bone, and unburned mussel shell fragments exposed within the three BHTs mentioned above between 90 and 108+ cmbs. The four FCR scatters were located approximately 50– 100 cm apart and were interpreted to be remnants of hearth clean-out and/or dumping activities as no in situ charcoal or burned earth was identified in proximity to the FCR. The BHT excavations were halted at the contact of the cultural materials and limited hand excavations were conducted to expose and document the size, content, and function of each of the cultural features. No temporally diagnostic artifacts or datable materials were observed during this initial survey. Additional work including mechanical scraping and hand excavations were recommended to expose, thoroughly document, and sample the features and determine if any underlying cultural deposits were present within the APE. Due to its buried context, potential integrity, and preservation of faunal materials, TxDOT determined site 41DN612 as eligible for the National Register of Historic Places (NRHP) and for designation as a State Antiquities Landmark (SAL). To accommodate the accelerated project schedule, TxDOT proposed the project for staged data recovery, where an initial phase of work would be used to further explore the site’s data potential and, if warranted, a second phase of work would address that potential with additional excavation. The THC concurred with this plan in a letter dated March 7, 2019. Overview of SWCA Investigations From April 14 to June 22, 2019, SWCA and its subcontractor completed the data recovery investigations of 41DN612 utilizing a combination of mechanical and hand excavation of all site deposits within the APE. Excavations terminated at 5 feet as depths of project impacts would not exceed that depth in this location. The initial excavations consisted of mechanical (i.e., backhoe) scraping of an 8×8-meter (m) block surrounding the features identified during Raba Kistner’s previous survey (Neel and Meléndez 2019) (Figure 1.3). During the course of investigations, TxDOT and SWCA modified the excavation methodology to address unanticipated findings, specifically once it became apparent that the four discrete burned rock clusters identified during the previous survey were part of a larger and more complex burned rock midden (BRM). To delineate the larger feature, mechanical and hand scraping was first used to determine the outer boundary of the portion of the BRM present within the initial 8×8- m excavation block. The initial excavation block was then expanded to the north approximately 2 m and to the east 3 m and an exploratory BHT (BHT 1) was excavated to the south of the block to determine the extent of the BRM in those directions (Figure 1.4). Hand excavations (i.e., 1×1-m units, 50×50-cm column sample) were utilized to: 1) identify, record, and sample any internal feature(s) within the BRM; 2) systematically quantify and characterize the rock material types within the BRM; 3) identify and collect in situ datable materials; and 4) document the internal stratigraphy of the BRM and any underlying cultural deposits. A total of 3.21 m3 of soils were hand excavated across the site, including three 1×1-m excavation units (i.e., Units 1–3) and one 50×50-cm column sample (i.e., CS 1). One feature-focused unit (Unit 4) was also excavated to further expose and document the burned rock layers of the BRM (see Figure 1.4). The matrix from this unit was not screened and the unit was terminated at the base of the feature; it is not included in the total volume of excavated deposits above. 4 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 17

Feature 4 Feature 2 Feature 3 Feature 1 Figure 1.3. Overview of re-excavation of Raba Kistner’s BHTs 10 and 12, view southeast. Gray tarps in BHTs are covering Raba Kistner’s features. Mechanical stripping and BHT “cuts” were then excavated to conduct the geoarcheological investigations at the site and to look for additional cultural features within the site deposits. A total of 16 cuts (BHT A–P) were excavated during this phase (Figure 1.5). A total of approximately 307.7 m3 of soils were excavated during the initial mechanical excavations and subsequent stripping. Once all mechanical and hand excavations and stripping were complete, final photographs were taken and the site was backfilled. Project Personnel Kevin Hanselka, Ph.D. (TxDOT) served as the PI for the project. Steve Carpenter, M.A., was the Project Manager (PM) for the overall contract and Christina Nielsen, M.A., served as the Project Archeologist (PA) for the 41DN612 field investigations, overseeing overall logistics and organization, managing reporting, and agency consultation. The data recovery was completed by Christina Nielsen; SWCA archeologists Jennifer Anderson, Jessica Ulmer, Michael Golden, Kim Wile, Ken Lawrence, Janaka Greene, and Brittney Ducre; and Integrated Environmental Solutions, LLC (IES) archeologists Thomas Chapman and Anne Gibson, between April 14 and June 22, 2019, under Permit No. 8872. Jim Abbott and Eric Oksanen from TxDOT also visited the site and assisted with the geoarcheological investigations. Christina Nielsen, Ashley Eyeington, Ken Lawrence, Chris Shelton, Jessica Ulmer, and Michael Golden undertook the reporting effort, while Carole Carpenter and Christian Hartnett expertly produced all field and report maps for the project, and Lauri Logan provided technical editing and document preparation. 5 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 20

Structure of Report The following report presents the environmental setting of site 41DN612 (Chapter 2) followed by a cultural history of the site and region (Chapter 3). Chapter 4 provides the research design developed for the site and a discussion of methods utilized to accomplish the data recovery. Chapter 5 offers a general overview of the data recovery as it relates to hand excavation units, mechanical excavations, chronology, and general level of effort. Chapter 6 present the results of the various geoarcheological analyses conducted at the site and the analytical unit definition. Chapter 7 presents a detailed examination of excavated cultural features, recovered artifacts, faunal remains, and special samples obtained during excavations. Chapter 8 addresses and answers the research questions posited for the data recovery. The report ends with Chapter 9, which provides a synthesis and overall interpretations of the site. Supporting data are presented in eight appendices to this report. The majority of the appendices are special study results and the various analytical data. 8 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 21

Chapter 2. Environmental Context This chapter provides an overview of the present-day environmental setting of 41DN612 and a review of the regional paleoenvironmental record. The discussion is based on the results of field investigations performed by SWCA archeologists and a review of relevant literature. Physiographic Ecoregion Site 41DN612 is within the Western Cross Timbers physiographic ecoregion of Texas, which covers the Grand Prairie (U.S. Department of the Interior 2020; U.S. Environmental Protection Agency [EPA] 2020). This ecoregion represents a cuesta topography with sandstone ridges, with terrain ranging from gentle slopes to steep scarps. Dominant vegetation includes post oak (Quercus stellata) and blackjack oak (Quercus stellate) with laurel greenbriar (Smilax laurifolia), little bluestem (Schizachyrium scoparium), and purple top grasses (Tridens flavus). The soils in this region are mainly fine, sandy loams overlying clay soils, which retain water better. The Grand Prairie ecoregion is characterized by the Lower Cretaceous limestone interbedded with marl and clay. In this region, meandering streams cut into the limestone. The original vegetated species include tall grass prairie in the upper region and elm (Ulmus spp.), pecan (Carya illinoensis), and hackberry (Celtis spp.) in areas along floodplains. Invasive species of Ashe juniper (Juniperus ashei) and honey mesquite (Prosopis glandulosa) have become more abundant in the region since settlement (EPA 2020). Soils Soils at site 41DN612 are mapped as frequently flooded Frio silty clay (Natural Resources Conservation Service [NRCS] 2020). These deposits are characterized as very deep, slowly permeable soils that formed in calcareous loamy and clayey alluvium that occupy floodplains and river valleys (NRCS 2020). Typical horizons for this series include an A1 horizon (0 to 20 cm), dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2) silty clay when dry, very dark grayish brown (10YR 3/2) moist, moderate fine and medium granular structure, hard and firm consistency, moderately sticky, moderately plastic, roots, few shell fragments, moderately alkaline with a clear smooth lower contact boundary. Below this is an A2 horizon, which typically is described as a dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2) clay loam when dry, very dark grayish brown (10YR 3/2) when moist, with a moderate and coarse subangular blocky structure giving way to moderate fine subangular blocky structure, few roots inclusions, few shells fragments, moderately alkaline, with a clear smooth boundary. Following the A2 horizon is usually an A3 horizon with characteristics including a dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2) silty clay loam when dry, very dark grayish brown (10YR 3/2) when moist, with a moderate fine and medium prismatic structure towards moderate fine and medium subangular blocky structure, with a hard and firm consistency, few roots, films and threads of calcium carbonate, few shell fragment inclusions, moderately alkaline, with a gradual and smooth lower boundary. Below this, an A4 horizon may occur and is typically described as a dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2) silty clay loam when dry, very dark grayish brown (10YR 3/2) when moist, with a moderate fine and medium prismatic structure towards moderate medium and coarse subangular blocky structure, with a hard and firm consistency, few roots, films and threads of calcium carbonate present, moderately alkaline, with a gradual smooth lower contact boundary. Following the A horizons is a Bk horizon consisting of a grayish brown (10YR 5/2) silty clay when dry, dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2) when moist, with a moderate medium and 9 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 22

coarse prismatic structure towards a weak coarse blocky structure, with a hard and firm consistency, few roots, with many fine films and threads with mottling of calcium carbonates. All soils are well drained and slope ranges from 0 to 2 percent. Flooding occurs one to three times a year (NRCS 2020). Topography The topography of the project APE consists of the broad flat floodplain of Denton Creek and Denton Creek Slough, an abandoned channel of Denton Creek located approximately 1,050 feet west of the current channel that functions as an overflow channel during flood events. Another old channel is evident as a remnant oxbow located in woodland northeast of the current channel. A total of 918 feet of the western portion of the APE extends onto a low terrace landform of Denton Creek, measured from the edge of the water to the topographic upland break. The current channel of Denton Creek is entrenched approximately 13–16 feet into its floodplain. The project APE topographically extends from a low of approximately 585 feet above mean sea level (amsl) at the channel of Denton Creek to approximately 605 feet amsl on the western valley terrace, a rise in elevation of only 20 feet (Figure 2.1). Figure 2.1. Overview of Denton Creek floodplain facing northeast toward 41DN612 (image from Google Earth Streetview). Geology and Hydrology The floodplain of Denton Creek is mapped as Holocene-age Quaternary (Qal) deposits composed of alluvium (U.S. Geological Survey [USGS] 2020). The minor terrace at the western end of the project extends onto Pleistocene–age Quaternary fluviatile terrace deposits (Qt). The Denton Creek valley has been cut into the underlying hard rock geology of the Fort Worth Limestone and Duck Creek formation, undivided (Kfd) and has subsequently filled with Holocene alluvium (Fisher 1991). The Fort Worth Limestone and Duck Creek formation, undivided, is an Early Cretaceous-age carbonate limestone with minor beds of unconsolidated marl 10 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


![Image 3 - Page 22](assets/Nielsen et al 2021 - Hardeman Midden Site (41DN612) Data Recovery/Nielsen et al 2021 - Hardeman Midden Site (41DN612) Data Recovery_p22_img3.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 22*

## Page 23

and clay that weathers to low rolling hills. Marine megafossils of oysters, echinoids, and ammonites are known from this unit. No outcrops of the unit were observed in the project area. Denton Creek is within the Upper Trinity River basin and is a tributary of the Elm Fork of the Trinity River. It joins the Elm Fork approximately 22 miles downstream from 41DN612 and eventually the Trinity River. Denton Creek at 41DN612, is located in southwestern Denton County, approximately 1.4 miles east of Justin, Texas (see Figure 1.1). The Denton Creek valley at this location is approximately 1 mile wide and appears to have a paired terrace system floodplain (Waters 1992:150–151). There is one alluvial landform (T0) on the floodplain near 41DN612. The T0 landform is generally 800 feet wide on both sides of the drainage. Notably, the T0 landform in this area is dissected in several locations across the floodplain by tributary channels. The tread of the T0 landform is positioned about 10–15 feet above the channel base and quickly rises to the nearby valley margins on the left bank (east side) and gradually rises to the valley margins on the right bank (west side). Site 41DN612 is situated on the tread of the T0 landform, roughly midway between Denton Creek and the upland valley margin. Abbott (2011:130) notes that the drainage gauge for Denton Creek near site 41DN612 has recorded that over 15 large overbank floods have occurred on the drainage within the last 60 years. Denton Creek at this crossing has a high sinuosity ratio (SR) of 1.4, indicating that it is a sinuous channel (Charlton 2008). This elevated SR value indicates that lateral erosion (exterior bends) and extensive sediment deposition (interior bends) are occurring within the channel, particularly at exterior and interior meander bends. Notably, site 41DN612 on the left bank (east side) is positioned on a slight interior meander bend, whereas the right bank (west side) is located on an exterior meander bend. Put simply, the west side of the channel at this crossing is being actively eroded away, while the east side is expanding through continual flood deposition. Furthermore, the high sinuosity of the drainage is also evident on aerial and topographic maps of this crossing (see Figures 1.1 and 1.2). These maps illustrate numerous remnant channels and oxbows that indicate the dynamic fluvial history of this drainage. Notably, site 41DN612 is adjacent to a remnant, oxbow channel about 115 feet to the southeast of the site. The channel base at this crossing is positioned at 585 feet amsl, while the surrounding floodplain in the project area is generally at 590 to 600 feet amsl. Site 41DN612 is positioned at 600 to 605 feet amsl. Flora Vegetation in the Grand Prairie region consists of mixed prairie grasses such as big bluestem (Andropogon gerardii), little bluestem, purpletop grasses, sideoats grama (B. curtipendula), yellow Indiangrass (Sorghastrum nutans), and Texas cupgrass (Eriochloa sericea) with riparian environments composed of pecan, hackberry, post oak, blackjack oak, laurel greenbriar, and elm (Griffith et al. 2007). Much of the region has been converted to pasture or is in use as rangeland. Due to intensive grazing in the region, there is an increase in buffalograss (Bouteloua dactyloides), Texas wintergrass (Nassella leucotricha), and grama grass (Bouteloua sp.) to the area (Bezanson 2000). 11 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 24

Fauna The project area falls within the far eastern section of the Kansan biotic province. There are 59 mammalian species, 46 reptilian species (turtle, snakes, and lizards), and 15 amphibian species identified by Blair (1950) within the Kansan biotic province. Important mammalian fauna encountered within the Kansan province include: Ord’s kangaroo rat (Dipodomys ordii), deer mouse (Peromyscus maniculatus), and eastern mole (Scalopus aquaticus), swift fox (Vulpes velox), plains pocket mouse (Perognathus flavescens), black-footed ferret (Mustela nigripes), striped skunk (Mephitis mephitis), badger (Taxidea taxus), coyote (Canis latrans), spotted ground squirrel (Citellus spilosoma), blacktail prairie dog (Cynomys ludovicianus), yellow-faced pocket gopher (Cratogeomys castonops), hispid pocket mouse (Perognathus hispidus), Merriam pocket mouse (Perognathus merriami), northern grasshopper mouse (Onychomys leucogaster), white-footed mouse (Peromyscus leucopus), whitethroat woodrat (Neotoma albigula), southern plains woodrat (Neotoma micropus), and blacktail jackrabbit (Lepus californicus). Bison once existed in this area but no longer remain in this area (Blair 1950; Burt and Grossenheider 1976; Davis and Schmidly 1994). Reptilian species encountered within the Kansan province include one turtle species, 14 lizard species, and 31 snake species. The land turtle species in this province is an ornate box turtle (Terrapene ornate). Some of the lizard species include eastern collared lizard (Crotaphytus collaris), Great Plains skink (Eumeces obsoletus), and prairie lizard (Sceloporus undulatus). Some of the snake species include: eastern glossy snake (Arizona elegans), western diamond-backed rattlesnake (Crotalus atrox), western rattlesnake (Crotalus viridis), plains blind snake (Leptotyphlops dulcis), gopher snake (Pituophis catenifer), long-nosed snake (Rhinocheilus lecontei), plains black-headed snake (Tantilla nigriceps), checkered garter snake (Thamnophis marcianus), and common garter snake (Thamnophis sirtalis) (Blair 1950; Conant and Collins 1998; Werler and Dixon 2004). Amphibian species encountered within the Kansan biotic province include one urodele, eastern tiger salamander (Ambystoma tigrinum). Anuran species encountered within the Kansan biotic province include: southern cricket frog (Acris gryllus), Great Plains toad (Bufo cognatus), eastern green toad (Bufo debilis), red- spotted toad (Bufo punctatus), and Woodhouse’s toad (Bufo woodhousii) (Blair 1950; Conant and Collins 1998). Paleoenvironment To date no detailed paleoenvironmental research has been conducted in the immediate vicinity of the project area. Investigations at site 41DL270 along Denton Creek by Brown (1998) compared isotopic variation in oxygen and carbon in mussel shell features to the paleoenvironmental studies conducted within the Central Texas region and found similarities in the paleoclimatic patterns in Denton Creek. Therefore, SWCA used data from regional studies south of the project area to provide a foundation for the paleoenvironment of the North- Central Texas region. Over the past 12,000 years, the environmental and climatic conditions of the Central Texas region have varied considerably. There is some debate about the paleoenvironmental conditions ca. 12,000–10,000 years before 12 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 25

present (B.P.), and two models are presently posited for the paleoenvironment of this early period. Bryant and Holloway (1985) have proposed that the Central Texas region was predominantly a deciduous forest 65,000– 10,000 years ago, with a diverse assortment of tree and plant species. In contrast, more recent research by Hall and Valastro (1995) and Toomey et al. (1993) suggests that Central Texas was dominated by grassland and mesic climatic conditions during the late Pleistocene. This interpretation fits well with the regional faunal record since megafaunal species from this period, such as mastodon, mammoth, giant bison, camel, and horse, would have required substantial areas of grassland for grazing (Dillehay 1974; Graham 1987; Graham and Lundelius 1984; Toomey et al. 1993). About 10,000 years ago, changes in paleoclimatic conditions led to mass extinctions of megafauna across the region (Graham 1987; Graham and Lundelius 1984). Faunal records from the Edward’s Plateau indicate the average seasonal temperature warmed, and climatic conditions became progressively drier (but still retained higher effective moisture than present day) throughout the early Holocene (Bement 1994; Toomey et al. 1993). As a result, vegetation cover diminished, and a degradation of the soil mantle ensued (Toomey et al. 1993:316). Due to the wind currents and temperature patterns of the nearby Gulf of Mexico, paleoclimatic conditions experienced east of the Edwards Plateau may have been relatively warm and moist during this time (Johnson and Goode 1994:20). Toomey et al. (1993:309) propose that the early Holocene warming trend continued into the middle and late Holocene and peaked ca. 5000–2500 B.P. “when climatic conditions were drier than any time during the last 20,000 years.” This warmer, drier climate is reflected in the shell isotope values from 3200–2600 B.P. at 41DL270 (Brown 1998). From 2500–1500 B.P. a wetter, cooler period followed in central and north-central Texas, which is supported by a rise in the Denton Creek shell isotope values (Brown 1998; Bryant and Holloway 1985; Hall 1988; Toomey et al. 1993). In addition, Humphrey and Ferring (1994) and Nordt et al. (1994) have argued for increased xeric vegetation during this time based on soil carbonates (Brown 1998). Finally, a rise in the Denton Creek isotope values after 1500 B.P. suggests a return to warmer and drier conditions (Brown 1998). From this time forward, present-day climatic and environmental conditions were firmly established for north-central Texas. Previous Regional Geoarcheological Investigations To provide a context for site 41DN612 and Denton Creek, SWCA reviewed previous geoarcheological investigations in the region relevant to the project area. The most extensive geoarcheological investigations within the Upper Trinity River basin occurred about 22 miles northeast of 41DN612 for the Ray Roberts- Lewisville Reservoir project in Cooke, Denton, and Grayson Counties (Prikryl and Yates 1987; Ferring and Yates 1997). The creation of these reservoirs from the impoundment of segments of the Elm Fork River generated archeological investigations extending over two decades by a variety of interdisciplinary researchers (e.g., Southern Methodist University [SMU], Environmental Consultants Inc., USACE - Fort Worth District, and University of North Texas). Among the many notable accomplishments from this research was the discovery and investigation of the Aubrey Clovis site (41DN479). The Aubrey Clovis site, situated on the Elm Fork River, was identified to have an intact stratigraphy extending back into the Pleistocene (Ferring 1990a, 1990b, 1991, 1992, 1994, 1995a, 1995b, 2000, 2001; Humphrey and Ferring 1994). Most significant, the site has a 13 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 26

Paleoindian occupation with a well-dated stratigraphy that suggests it is the oldest Clovis site currently known (Ferring 2001). In addition, the extensive investigations at Aubrey Clovis have provided a robust radiocarbon dataset and information from past environments. In summarizing the alluvial history of the Upper Trinity River basin, Ferring (1994) recognizes four morphostratigraphic units (i.e., landforms) composed of deposits from six alloformations. The morphostratigraphic units identified from oldest to youngest include the Stewart Creek Terrace, Hickory Street Terrace, Denton Creek Terrace, and the Floodplain that are interpreted to encompass the Middle Pleistocene up to the present. The Stewart Creek Terrace landform is composed of Irving alloformation deposits, while the Hickory Street Terrace is composed of Coppell alloformation deposits. The Irving alloformation deposits are interpreted to date to the middle Pleistocene approximately 75,000–125,000 years BP (Ferring 1994:35). The Coppell alloformation deposits are interpreted to date to the Pleistocene, sometime around 30,000 years ago (Ferring 1994:44–47; Ferring and Yates 1997). The more recent Denton Creek Terrace is composed of deposits from the Carrollton alloformation that contain Pleistocene faunal remains (e.g., bison [Bison antiquus]) and is interpreted to date to roughly 30,000–20,000 years ago (Ferring 1994:47–48). Most relevant to the archeology of the region is the landform identified as the Floodplain. The Floodplain contains deposits from three alloformations that, from oldest to youngest, include the Aubrey, Sanger, and Pilot Point alloformations. Ferring (1994) indicates that the Aubrey alloformation dates to the Late Pleistocene-Holocene transition. The terminus ante quem for this alloformation is from the Aubrey Clovis site (41DN479), which initially calibrated to 17,030 calendar years before present (cal yr BP) (SMU-2236) and terminates sometime after 14,410 cal yr BP (Ferring 1994; Ferring and Yates 1997). The initial calibration of the overlying Sanger alloformation begins in the Late Pleistocene sometime prior to 13,460 cal yr BP (AA-5274) and extends to sometime after 7,550 cal yr BP (SMU-2339) (Ferring 2001: Table 3.2). Ferring (1994:58–59) notes that the Sanger alloformation is capped by a moderately developed buried soil that in several locations has been eroded by the Pilot Point alloformation. The Pilot Point deposits began prior to 4,470 cal yr BP (SMU-2401) and terminated sometime after 1,676 cal yr BP (Beta-14963). A well-developed cumulic soil identified as the West Fork soil frequently caps the Pilot Point alloformation that is interpreted to encompass the last 4,000 years. Beginning in the Late Pleistocene, the alluvial history of the Upper Trinity River basin as defined by Ferring (1994:147–149) has a period of stasis with no discernable geomorphic activity occurring between 14,000– 11,000 years ago (17,000–13,000 cal yr B.P.). Specifically, Ferring (1994:147) notes that no alluvial or colluvial deposition or evidence of a disconformity is associated with this period. Subsequent to 11,000 years ago (13,000 cal yr B.P.), a period of rapid alluviation occurs in the basin, initiating a phase of valley filling. Interestingly, this period of rapid alluviation occurred shortly after the Clovis occupation at the Aubrey Clovis site and continued until 7,500 years ago (7,550 cal yr B.P.) (Ferring 1994:148). This event is inferred to be associated with a shift to a moister (i.e., mesic) climate. By the middle of the Holocene, a shift to a drier (xeric) climate occurred as evidenced by soil development in the Sanger alloformation and eolian deposits in some of the uplands of the upper basin of the Trinity River (Ferring 1994:148). These xeric conditions seemingly continued until approximately 4,500 years ago when a period of rapid alluviation occurred and the deposition 14 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 27

of the Pilot Point alloformation began (Ferring 1994:148–149). The rapid alluviation is particularly apparent between 3,000 and 2,000 years ago (Ferring 1994:149). In contrast to other researchers, Ferring (1994) notes that alluviation in the Upper Trinity River basin does not correlate with arid conditions. Instead, Ferring (1994:150–153) noted in this basin that the fluvial response to moist conditions was rapid alluviation and/or erosion while drier conditions led to stability and soil development and that the primary internal influence on a landscape’s evolution is the underlying bedrock, which affects the vegetation. Specifically, the calcareous loams and clay loams derived from weathered limestone support a prairie environment that is more resistant to erosion. Conversely, the non-calcareous soils derived from sandstone and shale support a mixed forest environment that has a lower threshold for erosion. Two primary external influences identified are the climate and changes in sea level. Although no examples are provided, eustatic influence is proposed as a possible influence to the upper extent of the basin. Regarding overall archeological potential within the upper basin, Ferring and Yates (1997) indicate that archeological sites occur in two principal contexts (i.e., terraces and upon or below floodplains). For the terraces, Ferring and Yates (1997) propose that the Trinity River terraces between Dallas and Valley View are Late Pleistocene in age. As such, any archeological sites less than 11,000 years old (~13,000 cal yr B.P.) could occur on the terrace surfaces, which are supported by numerous surveys in the area particularly in Denton, Dallas, and Tarrant Counties (Ferring and Yates 1997:279). For the floodplains in the Upper Trinity River basin, the age of the sites on the floodplains can be predicted by their stratigraphic location (Ferring and Yates 1997: Table 18.2). Unfortunately, Ferring and Yates (1997) note that cutbank exposures along Elm Fork River are “poor” and alluvial units thicken as they progress downstream. Consequently, older sites will become increasingly difficult to discern downstream. This will be particularly evident for Paleoindian–Middle Archaic sites, which will likely require mechanical excavation for their discovery. In contrast, the Pilot Point alloformation is exposed within the cutbanks of the entire upper extent of the basin and is characterized as thick, dark, and clayey with a buried soil (West Fork soil) located beneath recently deposited sand (Ferring and Yates 1997:280). Closer and more relevant to site 41DN612 are the investigations conducted by TxDOT at Denton Creek and US 380 in east-central Wise County, approximately 13 miles (21 km) upstream from 41DN612 (Abbott n.d. 2011). This project included extensive geoarcheological investigations including providing a stratigraphic model for Denton Creek. Abbott (2011:131–133) encountered deposits encompassing the Late Pleistocene to recent in age. The oldest observed deposit is characterized as prismatic, sandy loam and interpreted to correlate with the aforementioned Carrollton alloformation dating to 30,000–20,000 years ago (Abbott 2011:131; Ferring 1994:47–48). Next, Abbott (2011:131–132) identified a clay paleosol with prominent slickensides, mottles of ferromanganese, and calcium carbonate (CaCO3) masses that he interpreted as part of the Sanger alloformation. As noted above, the Sanger alloformation dates to approximately 13,500–7,000 years ago (Ferring 2001: Table 3.2). Notably, Abbott (2011:132) indicates the Sanger alloformation dates to 11,500– 4,500 years ago. Above the Sanger alloformation deposits, Abbott (2011:132) observed a clayey floodplain interpreted to be the Pilot Point alloformation dating to approximately 4,500–1,800 years ago. Finally, the Pilot Point deposits at that crossing of Denton Creek were capped by clayey alluvium less than 3.3 feet thick and interpreted to be recent in age (Abbott n.d.). 15 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 28

Chapter 3. Cultural Setting Site 41DN612 is situated within the North Central Texas archeological region on a tributary of the Elm Fork within the upper Trinity River basin (Perttula 2004:7). The following prehistoric cultural chronology of North Central Texas (and the upper Trinity River basin) derives its information from major reports by Hofman et al. (1989), Peter and McGregor (1988), Prikryl (1987, 1990), and Yates and Ferring (1986) (Table 3.1). Table 3.1. Cultural Chronology for North Central Texas Cultural period Subperiod Time Interval Calendrical Date Paleoindian – Before 8,500 B.P. Before 6550 B.C. Early 8,500–6,000 B.P. 6550–4050 B.C. Archaic Middle 6,000–3,500 B.P. 4050–1550 B.C. Late 3,500–1,250 B.P. 1550 B.C. – A.D. 700 Late Prehistoric I 1,250–750 B.P. A.D. 700–1200 Late Prehistoric Late Prehistoric II 750–250 B.P. A.D. 1200–1600 Protohistoric – 250–150 B.P. A.D. 1600–1800 B.P. = years before present (present is defined as the year 1950) Sources: Hofman et al. 1989; Peter and McGregor 1988; Prikryl 1990:48; Yates and Ferring 1986 The temporal data recovered from 41DN612 (2080–1200 B.P.) primarily place the site deposits within the Transitional Archaic (i.e., the last 1,000 years of the Late Archaic), although there is a slight overlap with the Late Prehistoric I period (Turner et al. 2011). Consequently, the following chronology presents an overview of the Archaic and Late Prehistoric periods with special focus on the Late and Transitional Archaic, providing the framework for subsequent discussion of research objectives. Archaic The Archaic period in North Central Texas is subdivided into Early (8,500–6,000 B.P.), Middle (6,000–3,500 B.P.), and Late (3,500–1,200 B.P.) based on perceived (though not fully agreed upon by all scholars) technological, environmental, and adaptive changes (Perttula 2004:9; Peter and McGregor 1988; Prikryl 1990:48). In the eastern and southwestern United States and on portions of the Great Plains, development of horticultural-based, semi-sedentary to sedentary populations followed the Archaic period. In these areas, the Archaic truly represents a developmental stage of adaptation as Willey and Phillips (1958) define it. It generally is believed to represent a shift toward hunting and gathering of a wider array of animal and plant resources and a decrease in group mobility (Willey and Phillips 1958:107–108). For North Central Texas, this characterization of the Archaic is somewhat problematic. An increasing amount of evidence suggests that Archaic-like adaptations were in place before the Archaic (Bousman et al. 2002; Collins 1998, 2004:117–118; Collins et al. 1989) and that these practices continued into the succeeding Late Prehistoric period (Collins 2004:118–119; Prewitt 1981:74). 16 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas
