## Page 29

Early Archaic Within the North Central Texas region, the Early Archaic period (8,500–6,000 B.P.) is marginally better documented than the Paleoindian period. However, both Early and Middle Archaic sites are few in number due to extensive erosion of mid-Holocene deposits. Early Archaic sites, specifically, are difficult to define due to a lack of well-documented archeological sites with distinct Early Archaic components. Most sites in this region are temporally mixed with inconsistent artifact assemblages, thus providing poor cultural associations. Those sites that have provided valuable data regarding the Early Archaic Period (i.e., Prikryl 1990) are limited to surficial findings (Ferring 1994; Frederick et al. 2006; McKee et al. 2010; Prikryl 1990; Todd and Skinner 2006; Williams 2004). Lynott (1981) proposes that population increased slowly over time, responding to warmer and drier environmental conditions. It is thought that these factors may have led Archaic populations of the Cross Timbers and prairie areas of North Central Texas to develop a diversified hunting and gathering pattern based on bottomland resources of the rivers and major creeks (Hofman et al. 1989). In general, projectile points were manufactured from local lithic sources suggesting that populations were less mobile during the Early Archaic period relative to the preceding Paleoindian period. The Early Archaic diagnostics include Angostura, Bulverde, Gower, Hoxie, Martindale, Uvalde, and Wells projectile points, as well as early split-stem projectile points. The trend towards shorter, more triangular projectile points and the emergence of polished tools, as well as ground stone tools, supports a shift in subsistence strategies. This indicates a strategy that relies more heavily on the processing of plant resources and the hunting of smaller, more local game (Williams 2004; Ferring and Yates 1998). The construction and use of rock hearths and ovens, which had been limited during the Paleoindian period, become commonplace in the Early Archaic. The use of rock features suggests that retaining heat and releasing it slowly over an extended period was important in food processing and cooking and also reflects a specialized subsistence strategy. Such a practice probably was related to cooking plant foods, particularly roots and bulbs, many of which must be subjected to prolonged periods of cooking to render them consumable and digestible (Black et al. 1997:257; Wandsnider 1997; Wilson 1930). Botanical remains, as well as other organic materials, are often poorly preserved in Early Archaic sites, so the range of plant foods exploited and their level of importance in the overall subsistence strategy are poorly understood. But recovery of charred wild hyacinth (Camassia scilloides) bulbs from an Early Archaic feature at the Wilson-Leonard Site provides some insights into the types of plant foods used and their importance in the Early Archaic diet (Collins 1998). Middle Archaic The Middle Archaic (6,000–4,000 B.P) is, more or less, an extension of the Early Archaic Period. The drier climate that had been established in the Early Archaic continued into the Middle Archaic, becoming more mesic as time progressed (Freeman 2007). One distinguishable difference that dominates the archeological landscape in Central Texas during the Middle Archaic period is the dense distribution of thermal features (i.e., BRMs and FCR) (Freeman 2007). However, these features are notably lacking from the North Central Texas landscape, specifically in the lower Elm Fork region. A number of reasons have been suggested as to why the feature density is less; however, the three main theories include: a difference in available plant resources, a difference in underlying geology that hinders the processing of specific plant resources (i.e., acorns), and the possibility that the North Central Texas groups developed distinct adaptations to their paleoenvironment, which 17 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 30

is supported by the distribution of dart points across the region (Nickels et al. 2015; Prikryl 1990). These dart typologies include Carrollton, Dawson, Morrill, Wells, and basal-notched forms such as Andice and Bell, which differ from the Nolan, Travis, and Bulverde point types commonly found in association with the BRMs in the Central Texas region (Nickels et al. 2015; Prikryl 1990; Turner et al. 2011). There is evidence that large game hunting (i.e., bison) in the early Middle Archaic again became an important dietary component. Johnson and Goode (1994:25) suggest that the Bell and Andice dart types are parts of a specialized bison-hunting tool kit and believe that an influx of bison and bison-hunting groups from the Eastern Woodland margins during a slightly more mesic period marked the transition from the Early Archaic to the Middle Archaic. Bison populations decreased as more xeric conditions returned during the latter part of the Middle Archaic (Johnson and Goode 1994:25). In general, cultural patterns in Central Texas during the Middle Archaic period point toward mobile hunter- gather populations intensively harvesting and processing plant materials such as acorns, pecans, and cacti (though not as evident in North Central Texas), as well as hunting small and medium-size game such as deer and turkey, and bison during the earlier parts of the period. In addition to the aforementioned lithic assemblage, other artifacts from the Middle Archaic include choppers, gouges, and expedient tools such as the bifacial and unifacial Clear Fork tools. Grinding stones and bases, referred to as manos and metates, show up in Middle Archaic artifact assemblages as well as several perforators, drills, and awls. Late Archaic Prikryl (1990) places the Late Archaic period from 3500–1250 B.P. and describes a threefold increase in the number of Late Archaic sites compared to the Middle Archaic as well as a greater distribution of sites over the landscape. Story (1981:144) hypothesized these population increases could be a result of factors such as more favorable environmental condition, changes in subsistence practices and technology, and reductions in group mobility, though no evidence for sedentarism during this period exist. The climate during the Late Archaic is suggested by Ferring (1986) to have been more mesic than in the previous Middle Archaic period. In comparison, the West Fork paleosol that developed in the latter part of the Late Archaic (approximately 2,300–1,000 years ago) in the Upper Trinity Basin is said to be evidence of a wetter, more favorable environment (Abbott 2011; Ferring 1986:112; Hall et al. 2011:542–544). This change in climate in the latter part of the Late Archaic would have resulted in the expansion of the Eastern Cross Timber woodlands and provided more food resources for both humans and animals such as deer (Prikryl 1990:74). This increase in locally available resources would have offset the population growth and reduction in group territory sizes occurring during this time period. Sites found within the West Fork paleosol often contain burned clay/clay balls, animal bone, and varying amounts of mussel shell, but similar to site 41DN612, have little to no lithic material (Todd 2014:8). Deer, rabbit, turtle, birds, fish, freshwater mussel shell and a continuing increase in the exploitation of plant resources (evidenced by large, presumably communal burned rock features termed “Wylie pit”) characterize subsistence adaptations during this period (Ferring and Yates 1997:6). The so-called “Wylie pit” is a large human-made pit, used for large-scale processing of plant foods thought to have been used on the East Fork starting in the Late Archaic (Bruseth and Martin 1987; Prikryl 1990:76). These pits, which resemble the BRMs 18 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 31

of Central Texas, suggest that plant foods were part of a broad-based diet (Kibler and Scott 2000:134). These pits were sometimes repurposed as burials, as seen at sites such as 41COL141 (Prikryl 1987:60-62) and 41DN102 (Skinner et al. 1982:162) along the East Fork at the Roberts Reservoir (Prikryl 1990:76). Late Archaic diagnostics in the North Central Texas region include Bulverde, Castroville, Dallas, Edgewood, Elam, Ellis, Ensor, Gary, Godley, Marcos, Marshall, Palmillas, Trinity, and Yarbrough. Projectile point styles overlap with both Eastern and Central Texas archeological regions as well as with Oklahoma to the north, possibly suggesting increasing interaction with groups in those regions (Green et al. 2013; Prikryl 1990). Of these point styles, the Gary point is most common style in the Lower Elm Fork area followed by Dallas and Trinity and Godley, Ellis, Elam, Edgewood, and Yarbrough types. In addition, Prikryl (1990:55) found that lithic material used for tool manufacture in the Late Archaic in this area was predominantly locally available Ogallala quartzite (62 percent) rather than non-local chert (38 percent). This suggests a decrease in group mobility as compared to earlier periods and could be a result of an increase in exploitation of local resources and/or could point to a decrease in access to other areas, possibly as a result of territorial restrictions in Central Texas (Prewitt 1983; Skinner 1981). In addition, the first currently known evidence of ceramics was found at Cedar Creek and the Richland-Chambers reservoirs at the eastern margins of North Central Texas region (Bruseth and Martin 1987; Story 1965). Transitional Archaic The Transitional Archaic is defined as the last 1,000 years of the Late Archaic (2250–1250 B.P.) and is thought to be an intensification of the cultural patterns of that time (Turner et al. 2011). Population continues to rise, and locally sourced chert continues to be readily utilized. Large cemeteries appear in the central Texas region during this time, implying established territories and subsistence zones (Black and McGraw 1985; Collins 2004; Lang 2019). Late Prehistoric This period encompasses 1250–250 B.P. and is divided into two subperiods: Late Prehistoric I (1250–750 B.P.) and Late Prehistoric II (750–250 B.P.) (Turner et al. 2011). This period is technologically defined by the widespread shift from dart points and atlatls to arrow points and bows, as well as specialized stone tools such as end scrapers, small drills, beveled knives, and bifaces. Additionally, this period is marked by the emergence of ceramics, maize horticulture, and more permanent domestic structures (Black and McGraw 1985; Collins 2004; Lang 2019; Todd 2014; Turner et al. 2011). The climate during the Late Prehistoric I period was the same as the Late Archaic and, as such, subsistence behavior during this time continued to rely on the hunting and gathering strategies from the previous period. Diagnostic traits that emerge during this time include grog-tempered and sand-tempered pottery; maize horticulture and the occupation of floodplains; more permanent domestic structures; and Scallorn, Steiner, Catahoula, and Alba arrow points, which are more abundant in the Late Prehistoric I period (Todd 2014). The Late Prehistoric II period coincides with a drying event in North Texas, creating an environment conducive to the return of bison to the region. This period is distinctively marked by the appearance of Perdiz points, as well as Fresno, Washita, and Harrell points. Both local and Caddoan pottery emerge, as well as large, thin bifaces (Pipe Creek and Gahagan), end scrapers, prismatic blades, and stone tool assemblages used in the 19 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 32

hunting of medium to large mammals such as bison, deer, and antelope (Antilocapra americana) (Turner et al. 2011:49–51). Occupation sites transition during this time from floodplains settlements to sandy terraces located above the floodplains, and faunal tools (i.e., bison scapula hoes) are noted. This new settlement location appears to be conducive to agriculture, although no conclusive evidence has been found to indicate an extension of agricultural activity during this period, aside from the bison scapula tools noted above (Ferring and Yates 1998; Todd 2014). Historic Context The Historic period began in Denton County after the Texian Land and Immigration Company (TLIC) from Louisville, Kentucky, obtained a land grant from Congress in 1841. The grant included all of Denton County, and sections of neighboring counties, with the understanding that the TLIC would convince 800 families from outside of the Republic of Texas to settle within the land in three years (Wade 2020). The first settlement was in the southeastern section, near present Hebron and the land settled by the company became known as the Peters colony (Odom 2020). The majority of the settlers from the area came from the upper South. In 1850, approximately 40 percent of the setters were from Tennessee and Kentucky. However, settlement of the area was slow, due to land cultivation being limited to subsistence agriculture from lack of water transportation (Odom 2020). The Icarian Colony In addition to the Peters colony settlers, French immigrants negotiated with the Peters Land Company for a million acres of land and established the Icarian colony near present-day Justin in 1849. The Icarian experiment was organized by the French socialist Ètienne Cabet, who wanted to create a utopian settlement that would share wealth on an equal basis and abolish private property and capital (Davidson 2020). The initial participants consisted of less than 70 inhabitants with a promise from Cabet that an additional 1,500 inhabitants would follow (Davidson 2020). However, when the settlers arrived at the Icarian colony, less than 100,000 acres of the one million were available for their use, and the land was divided into unconnected half- mile squares. In addition, the land was not suited for cultivation, and the colonists did not have enough equipment or horses to produce a crop (Davidson 2020). By midsummer, malaria had spread through the colony and incapacitated the vast majority of the colonists. The second wave of settlers consisted of only 10 inhabitants instead of the promised 1,500 and the remaining colonists abandoned the Icarian experiment and returned to New Orleans in the winter of 1849, less than a year after the colony was established (Davidson 2020). Rural and Urban Growth (1870–1990) Denton County grew slowly until after the Civil War in the 1870s. During this decade, the population doubled in size and new residents began farms, which switched the county’s focus from subsistence agriculture to cultivation (Odom 2020). In the 1880s, Denton County experienced a second wave of growth as railroads entered the county. Production of subsistence agriculture and cattle ranching declined as railroads provided a method of shipping cotton and wheat. Denton County proved ideal for wheat and from 1890 to 1920 the county ranked first or second in wheat production in the state of Texas (Odom 2020). In addition, cotton 20 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 33

peaked in 1920, but declined by the 1980s as demand for cotton decreased. Due to railroads playing a vital part in the economy, town locations were determined by railroads up to the 1970s (Odom 2020). Denton County experienced a third wave of expansion in the twentieth century as a commercial and higher education center. In 1890, the Texas Normal College was established, which later became the University of North Texas. In 1993 the university had an enrollment of more than 20,000 students (Odom 2020). In addition, the Girls’ Industrial College, which later was renamed to Texas Women’s University, was established in 1903. The two colleges attracted population growth and improved the cultural life in Denton (Odom 2020). During World War II, the county began transitioning from rural to urban as Denton began to serve as an extension of Dallas-Fort Worth. Completion of Interstate Highway 35 in the 1950s increased commuting, and by the 1980s Interstate highways 35E and 35W forked in Denton. After completion of the highway, Denton was connected to Dallas-Fort Worth and was the residence of many commuters to the city (Odom 2020). In addition to urban development, the rural population nearly tripled in growth from 1960 to 1980. A transition from small rural plots to large, sprawling ranches was observed as the horse trade expanded in Denton County (Odom 2020). By 1983 horses had brought a significantly larger income than any agricultural product and both newcomers and older residents reverted a significant portion of Denton County’s cropland to pasture (Odom 2020). Historic Map Review A review of the historic topographic maps and aerial maps revealed two structures near the 41DN612 site area (Foster et al. 2006; Historic Aerials 2020; USGS 2020). The first structure is located approximately 459 feet north of the site on the north side of FM 407. The structure appears on the 1961, 1969, and 1987 topographic maps, but is not present on the 2013 topographic map and later. In addition, the structure is present on the 1968 historic aerial map and is not present on the next available map dated in 1981. The structure appears to be a barn or similar outbuilding with an associated corral (Historic Aerials 2020). The second structure is approximately 1,033 feet northeast of the site on the south side of FM 407. The structure appears on the 1961, 1969, and 1987 topographic maps, but is not present on the 2013 topographic map and later. However, no structure is apparent in this location on the historic aerials dating from 1968 to 2016 (Historic Aerials 2020). 21 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 34

Chapter 4. Research Design and Methods Investigations at site 41DN612 entailed multiple phases, including survey conducted by Raba Kistner (Neel and Meléndez 2019), and then staged mitigation and geoarcheological investigations conducted by SWCA. At each phase, the fundamental objectives changed, and accordingly the methodology evolved to address the new objectives. Techniques and methods were first adapted to define the limits and significance of site deposits, and then subsequently modified to gather solid, non-redundant data that would contribute to an understanding of the broad patterns of regional prehistory. SWCA’s investigations at site 41DN612 indicate the prehistoric deposits appear to be primarily associated with the Transitional Archaic, although there is slight overlap with the Late Prehistoric I Period. Within the deposits, two analytical units were defined (i.e., Upper and Lower units) (see Chapter 6 of this report) (Lawrence et al. 2020). The Upper Analytical Unit falls at the end of the Transitional Archaic and very beginning of the Late Prehistoric I Period between 1540–1200 cal yr B.P., and the Lower Analytical Unit occurs between 2080 and 1820 cal yr B.P. As previously mentioned, these analytical units appear to be separated by a recognized period of change on the Denton Creek drainage channel. Abbott (n.d.:7) noted a change along Denton Creek at the US 380 crossing approximately 13 miles (21 km) upstream from 41DN612 around 1,740 years ago. Although the noted change to the Denton Creek channel is referring to a localized event at the US 380 crossing, the drainage certainly experienced similar contemporaneous alterations upstream and downstream from that crossing. The changes to Denton Creek upstream from the project area approximately 1,700 years ago may explain the increase in sedimentation separating the two age groups observed at the site. Research Design The following research design was developed to address general questions pertaining to prehistoric land use in the upper Trinity Basin area of North Central Texas focusing on variability in burned rock features and functional implications, and subsistence practices and implications to land use and mobility during the Transitional Archaic. Although no lithic materials were recovered from the site, good faunal and floral preservation allowed SWCA to explore the issues of burned rock cooking technology (hot rock cooking) and subsistence strategies during the analysis and reporting stages of the investigations and then compare these data with known regional Transitional Archaic sites. The following research topics were proposed for the analysis and reporting of the data recovery investigations. Research Topic 1: Burned Rock Cooking Technology:  SWCA examined the data collected for the burned rock features at the site with the goal of inferring feature functions and changes in technology, resource exploitation over time, and site occupational intensity and frequency, to further our understanding of broader issues regarding settlement patterns, foraging strategies, and social organization in the Transitional Archaic period in North Central Texas.  A total of seven burned rock features were identified at 41DN612 during the course of the investigations. A large BRM (i.e., Feature A) and five dense burned rock clusters within or just below the BRM (i.e., Features 1 through 5) were encountered in the Upper Analytical Unit. The size of Feature A (over 8 m in diameter), as well as quantity of rocks and basin shape, indicate a high investment of labor for food 22 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 35

processing and/or an extended period of environmental stability. One burned rock feature (i.e., Feature 6) was identified in the Lower Analytical Unit. This feature is a discrete, burned rock cluster interpreted to be single- or limited-use hot rock cooking event.  The small, isolated burned rock features identified in the Upper and Lower Analytical Units are interpreted as single-use cooking features, which suggests a lower investment of labor for food processing and intermittent or brief occupations of the site area during this period. Conversely, the accumulation of the large BRM in the Upper Analytical Unit is suggestive of a higher investment of labor and more intensive and/or repetitive occupation of the site during the latter part of the Transitional Archaic (Black and Thoms 2014:205, 222; Dering 1999). Further study of the contexts and functions of these features as they relate to investment of labor and resource availability could yield information on economical and organizational aspects of landscape use, planning, and shifting resource bases over time. Specific research questions and data used to address these issues are presented below: 1) Are there variations in feature function at the site over time and/or within each of the analytical units?  Feature function was inferred using the feature data recorded during the field investigations (e.g., feature dimensions and morphology, coarse and fine matrix descriptions, and nonmatrix constituents) and results of macrobotanical and faunal analysis. The most common function attributed to burned rock features is their utilization as ovens or hearths (Black et al. 1997). In North Central Texas, earth ovens of varying sizes were especially common by 2500 B.P. in the Late Archaic, and stone-boiling features were also reported in this area during this period (Fields 2004; Rogers 1997; Rogers and Kotter 1995). Thoms (2009:577) provides the following, more-comprehensive list of hot-rock cooking methods/functions: a) grilling or otherwise cooking on open-air hearths with stone heating elements fired in situ, b) baking with stone heating elements, fired in situ or elsewhere, in closed pits and mounds (earth ovens), c) steaming with stone heating elements, fired in situ or elsewhere, in closed pits and mounds, and d) stone boiling in open pits and non-ceramic vessels with stones heated on nearby surface hearths/fires.  In addition to the methods of cooking, resources used for construction (e.g., rock, wood fuel) of the feature as well as the resource(s) processed or around each feature, were reviewed and compared to the other features at the site. 2) How many individual cooking events are estimated for the earth ovens within the site?  The burned rock at the site was size-sorted, weighed, and counted by material type (e.g., limestone, quartzite) for each unit by level and/or feature and a representative sample of material types was collected. Feature burned rock weights and rock sizes were used to estimate the number of cooking events associated with each feature classified as an earth oven, and for the BRM (earth oven facility). Based on data from excavated archeological sites and experimental earth ovens, a general range of burned rock weight per cooking event is estimated to be 100 to 250 kilograms (kg) (Black 1985; Black et 23 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 36

al. 1997; Dering 1999; Leach et al. 1998). In addition, the size and fragmentation of feature rocks can aid in the evaluation of use and reuse of features. In general, larger rocks are more likely part of a “primary structural element” of an earth oven, whereas smaller rocks are more indicative of reuse and/or discard (Black et al. 1997:83). 3) What is the relationship of site formation processes and the patterning of the cultural features observed within the site?  The geoarcheological analysis results and feature distribution throughout the site were used to determine what processes have affected the cultural manifestations observed at the site. Are the smaller, isolated features a result of more sedimentation covering the site during that time period, indicative of specific feature function and/or specific resource processing, a result of cultural/natural disturbances, or a combination of these factors? Did the large BRM feature accumulate due to a period of surface stability, allowing reuse of the site features creating a palimpsest, or is this a result of intensification of resource processing and the need for larger cooking features (i.e., increased labor investment)? Research Topic 2: Subsistence Strategies:  Through the analysis of burned rock feature technology, flora, and faunal samples, and regional paleoenvironmental data, the investigations could identify if there are any discernible changes amongst the isolable Transitional Archaic occupations at the site. As Todd (2014) mentions, only a limited number of analyses have been conducted for floral remains at archeological sites in the Upper Trinity Basin area, and even fewer of these studies are for sites from the Transitional Archaic. Nonetheless, the limited floral data combined with more robust faunal data suggest the foraging patterns of the prehistoric inhabitants of the Upper Trinity River basin can be characterized as broad diet breadth exploiting locally available resources such as deer, rabbit, small game, turtle, birds, fish, and freshwater mussel shell, and an increased use of plant resources.  Analysis of the preserved flora and faunal material and cooking features aided in determining whether the 41DN612 assemblage is consistent with these expectations or shows a different foraging strategy. Specific research questions associated with this topic include: 1) Is the 41DN612 assemblage consistent with expectations of a broad-based foraging strategy?  Results of the macrobotanical analyses on samples from feature contexts at 41DN612 primarily identified wood charcoal, interpreted to be fuel for cooking features, and carbonized bulb scale fragments (geophytes) including camas (Camassia spp.) and wild onion or garlic (Allium spp.) (Bush 2020). In addition, indeterminable starchy fragments were also identified and may represent bulbs, tubers, rhizomes, or large, starchy seeds. The tubers might be prairie turnips (Psoralea esculenta, also known as Pediomelum esculentum) or scurfpea (Pediomelum spp.) found at other sites in the Trinity Basin (Field et al. 1993; McGregor and Bruseth 1987; Reid 1977; Wedel 1978). Earth oven facilities were used to convert otherwise inedible foods such as these into more digestible starches and sugars (Dering 1999; Thoms 2008; Wandsnider 1997). Dering (1999:671) suggests that the use of large earth ovens, such as the BRM at 41DN612, is indicative of a broad-spectrum economy. Geophytes and other starchy foods 24 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 37

would not have been a primary carbohydrate source, rather one component of a much broader subsistence strategy that changed in response to factors such as availability of other, higher-ranked resources (e.g., deer).  The distribution and types of cultural features, macrobotanical remains, and faunal remains (i.e., bone and shell) within each analytical unit will be reviewed and compared against the broad-based diet breadth model presented above. 2) Can any inferences be made about seasonality of site use?  Over 140 mussel shells were recovered from the site from feature and non-feature contexts. The analysis focused on identifying each specimen to the most specific taxonomic order as the specimens allowed. Research was then conducted to try to determine when each mussel shell type would have been available to human gatherers throughout the year and if they were only available seasonally (e.g., in the warmer months). In addition, examination of the macrobotanical remains and faunal bone exploited by site occupants also shed light on the issues of seasonality. For example, most geophytes like the camas and wild onion identified from the site during the macrobotanical analysis, have spring and summer growing seasons (Acuña 2006). 3) Is there any evidence of intensive resource processing or tool manufacture at the site?  Classic signatures of intensive plant processing, such as manos, metates, or nutting stones, are absent at the site. However, the presence of a large BRM in the Upper Analytical Unit suggests intensive plant processing likely occurred on site during the Late Transitional Archaic. The feature function analysis (Research Topic 1, Question 3) aided in determining if intensive plant processing was occurring at the site and when, what resource(s) were being processed intensively, and what methods were being used to process those resources. In addition, examination of faunal remains identified if butchering or intensive processing (e.g., marrow extraction) was occurring at the site during this period.  No lithic tools or detritus were recovered from the site during the survey or excavation phases. Close examination of the freshwater mussel shells and faunal bone was conducted to determine if any evidence of shell-working activities or evidence for production of bone tools is present in the assemblage. Ultimately, this research design sought to provide information that would contribute to the model of prehistoric land use in North Central Texas presented above, as well as address issues pertinent to the understanding of the prehistory of Texas. The research goals proposed for the site were focused on burned rock technology, subsistence practices, and how cultural and environmental factors might have affected these activities throughout the Transitional Archaic. Field Methods Initial Field Methodology Given an accelerated schedule for the proposed construction, TxDOT requested that the adverse effects to site 41DN612 be mitigated through a combination of staged data recovery to detect any additional features and/or 25 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 38

burials within the project APE (monitored scraping of the site, described below), and alternative mitigation in the form of law enforcement training in best practices when dealing with possible Native American burials and archeological sites. Initial field investigations consisted of monitoring mechanical (i.e., backhoe) scraping in an 8×8-m block surrounding the positive BHTs on 41DN612. Overburden was removed mechanically, followed by systematic scraping into the archeological deposits. If intact features were encountered, scraping was to be ceased while features were exposed by hand, mapped with a Total Data Station (TDS), documented, photographed, and sampled. At least one liter of feature matrix was to be collected for flotation for macrobotanical data. Diagnostic artifacts and FCR were mapped in situ by TDS. Two negative BHTs excavated to the east during survey suggested that cultural materials would not extend in this direction. However, if features were found to extend beyond the block, the excavation would be enlarged accordingly. Once materials related to the known occupation were documented and removed, scraping would continue down until it was determined whether additional underlying occupations were present within the vertical APE. If any underlying features were encountered within the vertical APE, they would be documented and sampled in the same manner. As depths of impacts will not exceed 5 feet at this location, scraping would cease once this depth was reached. The Advisory Council for Historic Preservation was advised of the pending adverse effects to site 41DN612, and approved of the proposed mitigation strategy. The PI of the investigation is Kevin Hanselka (TxDOT), who was assisted in the field by personnel from SWCA and Integrated Environmental Solutions (IES). This proposed strategy was developed in consultation with interested Tribes. In the event that human remains are discovered during any phase of fieldwork or construction, investigators shall cover the remains, cease work in the immediate area, and procedures consistent with the TxDOT’s programmatic agreement and the Texas Health and Safety code will be initiated. If practical, work may continue in areas more than 5 m away from the identified remains. During the initial mechanical and hand scraping, it was discovered that the site did not consist of four discrete burned rock clusters, rather, the site contained a large BRM. As such, the field methodology had to be adjusted due to the additional complexities of the site (see below). Modifications to Field Methods As previously mentioned, it became necessary to modify certain elements of the excavation methodology once it became apparent that the site contained a large BRM. A combination of mechanical and hand scraping was first used to determine the outer boundary of the portion of the BRM present within the excavation block. The initial excavation block was also expanded to the north and east and an exploratory trench (BHT 1) was excavated to the south of the block to determine the extent of the BRM in each of those directions. A column sample (CS 1) was excavated on the western wall of BHT 1 to explore the historic fill deposits in this portion of the APE and determine if the BRM continued in this direction. The column sample was 50×50 cm, extending from the ground surface to the base of the vertical APE (i.e., 5 feet). The soils from the column sample were excavated in 10-cm levels and screened through ¼-inch hardware screen mesh. 26 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 39

Three traditional, 1×1-m excavations units (i.e., Units 1–3) were then placed over the presumed center of the BRM. As mention in Chapter 1, the purpose of the hand excavations were to: 1) identify, record, and sample any internal feature(s) within the BRM; 2) systematically quantify and characterize the rock material within the BRM; 3) identify and collect in situ datable materials; and 4) document the internal stratigraphy of the BRM and any underlying cultural deposits. The units were excavated in arbitrary 10-cm levels using standard archeological methods, and were documented using standardized field forms and photography. All artifacts and pertinent special samples were collected for analysis and bagged separately to examine site stratigraphy and occupation levels. Features encountered during the investigations were carefully exposed, documented, and sampled (see detailed feature excavation methods below). Upon completion of the hand unit excavations, additional stripping of the excavation block and area immediately south of the block was conducted. The stripping was done “broadcast style” (i.e., starting at the edge of the BRM and exposing a series of adjacent “cuts” to full depth, looking for internal features [if any]). Each cut (herein referred to as BHT) was completed, assigned a letter to designate the BHT, the wall cleaned and photographed, and inspected for internal cultural features before starting the next BHT. Any internal features discovered within the BHTs were exposed, photographed, documented, and sampled by hand. In addition, one “feature-focused” unit (i.e., Unit 4) was excavated during the mechanical stripping phase to further explore the horizontal and vertical extents of Feature A. To expedite the feature investigations, each layer of burned rock within the portions of Feature A in Unit 4 were exposed and photo-documented, but not hand-drawn or sampled; this approach was approved by Kevin Hanselka (PI, TxDOT), who was on site that day. The feature-focused unit was not excavated further after Feature A was fully documented. Ken Lawrence of SWCA and Jim Abbott of TxDOT conducted the geoarcheological investigations at the site concurrent with the mechanical stripping. Once all mechanical and hand excavations and stripping were complete, final photographs were taken and the site was backfilled. Feature Excavation Methods As an important data set, features were focal points of the archeological investigations. The main feature at the site, the large BRM, was designated Feature A. The other six features discovered during at the site were numbered, with the PA maintaining the list of consecutive numbers. Features identified during the hand excavations were exposed in plan, drawn, photographed, and described and documented on a special feature form. Charcoal samples for radiocarbon dating were collected as determined by the PA. Matrix samples were taken for flotation analysis, and the coarse matrix (burned rock) was size-sorted, counted, weighed, and discarded in the field. The remaining matrix was screened through ¼-inch mesh for full artifact recovery. Due to time constraints, features identified within the BHTs during the mechanical stripping were photo- documented but not hand-drawn and limited samples were collected at the discretion of the PA and PI. Geoarcheological Methods Geoarcheological field investigations were conducted in June 2019 and consisted of examining multiple BHTs and excavation units that provided an exposed stratigraphic profile across the site. The exposed BHTs were thoroughly examined and characterized using a modified soil profile documentation advocated by Birkeland (1999) and Schoeneberger et al. (2012) (Appendix 1). All Munsell colors, unless indicated otherwise, were 27 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 40

recorded dry. These field investigations, conducted by SWCA geoarcheologist Ken Lawrence, M.A., incorporated documentation of the physical characteristics of the horizon matrices as well as systematic sampling to provide matrix for laboratory analyses. Artifact Collection and Sampling Strategy All artifacts and samples recovered from each provenience unit and level were collected, bagged, and labeled accordingly, and were assigned a general bag number in the field inventory. Artifacts exposed during the initial mechanical scraping and exposure of the BRM were point-plotted using the TDS. In addition, artifacts and samples associated with features identified during the backhoe trenching were point-plotted using the TDS while point-plotted artifacts and samples from the excavation units were recorded using the unit datums. Point provenience artifacts and samples were assigned an individual bag number in the field inventory. Burned rock was sorted by material type and then size-sorted, weighed, and counted for each unit by level and/or feature (Appendix 2). A small sample of burned rock was collected to further evaluate material types but the rest of the burned rock was discarded in the field. The presence of snails was also noted on unit level forms but not collected. In conjunction with the excavations, special samples were collected from appropriate contexts across the site. Special samples included materials for radiocarbon dating (from feature and non-feature contexts), matrix samples for flotation, and matrix for geoarcheological analyses. Laboratory Processing and Analysis Methods An important component of the data recovery investigations at 41DN612 was the laboratory processing and analyses conducted subsequent to the fieldwork. These disparate analyses performed separately were designed to provide a more thorough picture from which to interpret the significance of the site. Artifact Processing The artifact processing and cataloging system was done in compliance with the Center for Archeological Studies (CAS) curation standards and guidelines. Artifact processing included washing, sorting, and tabulating the recovered materials with the resulting data compiled into the specimen inventory cataloging system. The field inventory was applied to the specimen inventory, which included assigning lot numbers to the bag numbers previously designated in the field and assigning specimen numbers to facilitate artifact analyses. The specimen inventory for the data recovery excavations are presented in Appendix 3. All artifact processing was accomplished at SWCA’s laboratory. Artifact Analysis Once the fieldwork was complete, SWCA analyzed all recovered artifacts. This included washing all artifacts and sorting into analytical categories, including historic materials, burned clay, and faunal remains (i.e., bone and shell). Analysis of historic artifacts focused on recording basic attributes and determining approximate manufacture dates (Appendix 4). Faunal analysis of animal bone and shell focused on determining species, number of individuals, elements, seasonality, butchering practices, and effects of post-depositional processes (Appendices 5 and 6). Data from the analyses were then used to determine cultural behaviors on the site. Site 28 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 41

analysis included examining artifact distributions, running all special samples, and comparing overall results to other regional data. Faunal Analysis Animal Bone The 41DN612 animal bone analysis was conducted in SWCA’s Austin office. The assemblage was sorted, thoroughly analyzed, and interpreted by Chris Shelton, M.A. Each faunal element was individually examined and analyzed both macroscopically, and with the aid of a 10x microscope combined with an oblique angle light source. When possible, each specimen within the assemblage was identified to skeletal element, as well as to the lowest level of taxonomic classification using comparative osteology manuals, such as Klein and Cruz- Uribe’s (1984) The Analysis of Animal Bones from Archeological Sites, Lyman’s (1994) Vertebrate Taphonomy, and Olsen’s (1973) Mammal Remains from Archeological Sites, as well as comparative faunal collections housed in the SWCA Austin lab. The faunal assemblage was computer catalogued using Microsoft Excel. The database consisted of 12 distinct criteria, including: taxon identification; common name of animal; estimated body size; element presence; cultural modification presence and type; bone element identification; element side; presence of growth plate fusion; presence and degree of surface weathering; evidence of burning, margin angle, as well as all relevant metrics (Appendix 5). Human taphonomy processes, such as presence or absence of butchery in the form of cut marks, chop marks and percussion impact breakage were cataloged to examine foraging techniques. The combination of these criteria can be analyzed individually or as groups in order to determine important economic, preference, and social information in regard to the site. Shell The 41DN612 shell analysis was conducted in SWCA’s Austin office. Analysis of the shell used macroscopic and low-power microscopic inspection to search for cultural modification and determine the genus and species of each shell. The assemblage was analyzed by SWCA biologist Stephen Van Kampen-Lewis and SWCA archeologist Michael Golden. SWCA archeologists and biologists used designations and distinctive characteristics defined by Howells (2014), who provide a thorough inventory of fresh water mussel shells in Texas, to identify the various species recovered from the excavations. Other comparative manuals, such as Graf and Cummings’s (2020) The Freshwater Mussels (Unionoida) of the World (and other less consequential bivalves) and Randklev et al.’s (2020) Mussels of Texas Project Database were also used to aid in the shell identifications. The shell assemblage was computer catalogued using Microsoft Excel. The database consisted of nine distinct criteria, including artifact type; taxon identification; common name; count; weight; umbo presence; side; adult/juvenile; and cultural modification presence/type along with specific provenience information for each analyzed specimen (e.g., Lot No., Unit, Point Provenience) (Appendix 6). 29 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 42

Special Samples As mentioned briefly above, special samples were systematically collected from appropriate contexts across the site. Special samples included materials for radiocarbon dating (from features, geomorphic units, and other contexts), matrix samples for flotation and/or fine screening (from features), and sediment samples for geoarcheological analysis (e.g., particle size and magnetic susceptibility [MS]). These types of samples, often critical in interpreting a site, were taken from both cultural and natural contexts. Special samples were immediately prepared and submitted to the appropriate laboratories for processing. Quick submittal of the samples ensured a fast turn-around time so that results could be integrated into the report. Radiocarbon Analysis Radiocarbon samples were collected from select locations at 41DN612 to provide an approximate age of specific strata found at the site and to aid in identifying analytical units. The subsequent radiocarbon analyses were conducted by Beta Analytic, Inc. (Appendix 7). The calibration and statistical modeling of the radiocarbon results discussed below was performed by Ken Lawrence using OxCal v4.3.2. Briefly, radiocarbon years (14C) are not calendar years, but rather a measurement of remaining 14C isotopes (Bartlein et al. 1995; Blackwell and Buck 2008; Blackwell et al. 2006; Blockley et al. 2007; McCormac and Baillie 1993; Mock and Bartlein 1995; Ramsey 2008a, 2008b, 2009:337; Stuiver and Suess 1966). Consequently, the 14C years must be converted to a calendrical format (i.e., calibrated) for general interpretation and comparison (Mook and Waterbolk 1985:20). This study uses the nomenclature proposed by Nordt (1992) and the journal of American Antiquity guidelines for distinguishing between uncalibrated radiocarbon and calibrated radiocarbon results. Specifically, the results of these analyses are here reported in both conventional radiocarbon age (14C yr BP) and the calendar calibrated (cal yr BP) results. The Gregorian calendar year A.D. 1950 is recognized as the beginning point of B.P. (Hua 2009; Mook and Waterbolk 1985; Stuiver and Polach 1977; Taylor 1997:67–68, 2009). Due to the proximity and tight vertical control of the collected radiocarbon samples, the conventional radiocarbon results of select samples from Beta Analytic, Inc., were recalibrated and examined with Bayesian modeling. Briefly, the calibration software (e.g., OxCal, Bcal, and Calib), interprets the conventional 14C data with a probability distribution (Banning 2000; Ramsey 1995, 1998, 2001, 2008a, 2009). The calibration software plots the 14C dating results onto the calibration curve and then characterizes the probability of the interpreted outcome. There are more than a half dozen software packages, but the one used for the 41DN612 analyses was OxCal Version 4.3.2, which can incorporate Bayesian statistics into the characterization of the dating results. Briefly, the calibration software (e.g., OxCal, Bcal and Calib), interprets the conventional 14C data with a probability distribution (Banning 2000; Ramsey 1995, 1998, 2001, 2008a, 2009). Broadly defined, Bayesian statistics enable a researcher to incorporate data from the calibration curve with new data (e.g., 14C results), as well as accounting for prior information, to suggest the most probable outcome (Bayliss and Ramsey 2004; Blackwell and Buck 2008; Buck et al. 1991; Buck et al. 1992; Buck et al. 1994; Buck et al. 1996, Buck et al. 2006; Buck 2004; Christian 1994; Heaton et al. 2009; Ilan 2001; Ramsey 2009). The technique uses Baye’s theorem that expresses the uncertainty of an event or set of parameters occurring before and subsequent to the results of an analysis (Buck 2004; Buck et al. 1996; Christian 1994). Put 30 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 43

another way, the Bayesian analysis of 14C calibration data considers all possibilities of the outcome (prior) with the measured data (likelihood), and then determines the probability of those results occurring (Ramsey 2009). A prior probability is inferred from relative dates (e.g., stratigraphic position) and then compared with the likelihood probability that is interpreted from the absolute dates (e.g., 14C dates), which culminates in the determination of the posterior probability. When comparing multiple events simultaneously, the large number of individual combinations that are considered for each outcome (iteration) can become infinite. To aid in these analyses by calibration software, a sampling algorithm, such as the Metropolis-Hastings or equivalent, is typically used (Ramsey 2009). Metropolis-Hastings is an algorithm of the Markov chain Monte Carlo (MCMC) method, which randomly examines each event across a defined distribution, gradually increasing the confidence of the result (Breyer 2020; Heaton et al. 2009; Ramsey 2009). Further, the implementation of MCMC allows for the inclusion of the uncertainty of multiple factors that can allow for the comparison of points as well as their deviations on the curve (Buck and Blackwell 2004:1101; Everitt 2002; Upton and Cook 2006). For specific OxCal v4.3.2 operations, the calibration curve utilized IntCal13 and the analyses were primarily conducted using default settings. However, output was set at both 68.2 percent (1σ) and 95.4 percent (2 σ) and rounded to the nearest decade for a minimum of 30,000 iterations. These analyses were conducted under the assumption that horizons at lower elevations will be older than shallower samples (i.e., Law of Superposition). The radiocarbon assays at 41DN612 were grouped in the identified stratigraphic horizon and were examined in OxCal using the Phase model under the Sequential setting. This setting allows for sets of radiocarbon samples to be grouped in sequence (i.e., stratigraphic) and contrasted both within the group and against other groups (Ramsey 2009). Further, the Sequential setting can operate under the assumption that another group (i.e., horizon) cannot be temporally contiguous or overlapping (Ramsey 2009). Ultimately, the intent of using this suite of techniques is to derive as much information as possible to compare the results and help identify the analytical units for 41DN612. Flotation and Macrobotanical Analysis Additional attempts were made to obtain plant-related subsistence data from 41DN612. In open, well-drained sites like 41DN612, plant remains rarely preserve unless they were previously exposed to 250–300°C temperatures with little oxygen, resulting in carbonization (Hard et al. 1996). Although resistant to biological deterioration once carbonized, they still may be destroyed by mechanical means (Miksicek 1987:20). When possible, charred fragments visible to the naked eye (“macrofossils”) were collected directly from the site’s exposures (i.e., hand excavation units and BHT walls). Smaller remains go unnoticed during excavation and often slip through screens, so SWCA collected soil samples for processing by water separation, or flotation (Struever 1968), in order to maximize data recovery. Twenty matrix soil samples were retained for this purpose from six features (i.e., Features A and 1–3, 5, and 6) and of those, 18 were processed for further analysis. The samples were processed in a 15-gallon metal wash pan filled about two-thirds with water. A large piece of fine window screen was clipped to the circumference of the rim, loose enough to hang well below the water surface. Individual soil samples were added to the water gradually and gently agitated by hand, allowing for the sediment matrix to break up and free any lightweight organic contents (light fraction) into the water column. 31 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 44

The light fraction was then scooped from the water using a fine-mesh aquarium net. The procedure was repeated until the entire sample was processed, and the light fraction set to dry. The heavier materials caught in the window screen catchment (heavy fraction) were rinsed clean, dried, and bagged separately from the light fraction. Once dry, each of the light and heavy fractions were weighed (in grams) and the light fractions were packaged to be sent to Dr. Leslie Bush for macrobotanical analysis. In addition to the analysis of light fractions from the feature matrix samples, SWCA sent Dr. Bush 32 fragments of wood charcoal collected directly from feature contexts and from select non-feature contexts for identification. The macrobotanical analysis report and analysis tables are included in Appendix 8. Geoarcheological Investigations The laboratory geoarcheological analyses included texture analyses, calcium carbonate content, and MS. The geoarcheological laboratory analyses (discussed in detail below) of 41DN612 were conducted in September– October 2019 and January–February 2020 at the SWCA Austin office laboratory by Ken Lawrence, the Texas A&M Soil Characterization Laboratory, and assistants Ashley Eyeington and Jessica Ulmer. Particle Size (Texture) Analysis To examine the texture of the matrix at site 41DN612, samples were submitted to Texas A&M Soil Characterization Laboratory. At the laboratory, the samples were removed from their initial shipping containers, placed in aluminum or other non-porous, non-corrodible shallow containers and oven dried at 65°C (+2°C) in a forced air oven for 16 hours or until dry. Following oven drying, samples were pulverized using an open-mesh- bottom hammer-style soil pulverizer. All soil exiting the pulverizer was screened to remove all particles greater than a 2-millimeter (mm) diameter. The texture analyses used the pipette method and identified the percentages of coarse fragments (>2.00 mm), sand (2.00–0.05 mm), silt (0.05–0.002 mm), and clay (<0.002 mm). Results from the analyses were provided in tabular form. Calcium Carbonate Content Analysis To examine the Calcium Carbonate (CaCO ) content of the selected matrix, samples were submitted to Texas 3 A&M Soil Characterization Laboratory. The CaCO analyses used the Chittick method on the matrix and identified 3 the percentages of CaCO , dolomite, and calcite. Results from the analyses were provided in tabular form. 3 Magnetic Susceptibility (MS) Analysis MS analyses of soils have been utilized for archeological investigations for several decades, and have largely been directed toward surveys (e.g., Crowther and Barker 1995; Dalan 1996, 2008; Rosendahl et al. 2014; Van Leusen et al. 2014; Wiewel and Kvamme 2014). More recently, these investigations have focused on archeological site formation and associated depositional processes (Dalan 2006, 2008). In particular, these analyses have assisted in identifying buried or thermally altered soils associated with cultural activities, as well as identifying the horizontal extent of cultural features (e.g., Dalan and Banerjee 1998; Dalan and Bevan 2002; Ellwood et al. 1995; Frederick 2010; Jones and Leffler 2003; Mauldin and Figueroa 2006; Nickels 2010; Peters and Thompson 1999; Rivers et al. 2004). 32 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 45

Extensive reviews of the processes of environmental magnetism and particularly the application of MS have been produced (e.g., Dearing 1999a, 1999b; Gale and Hoare 1991:201–229; Maher et al. 1999; Thompson and Oldfield 1986; Verosub and Roberts 1995). Briefly, however, magnetic minerals are prevalent in the natural environment and are sensitive to environmental changes (Gale and Hoare 1991:202). The measurement of MS, χ (Chi), is a quantification of the “magnetisability” of the material (Dalan 2008; Dalan and Banerjee 1998; Dearing 1999b:5; Gale and Hoare 1991:202–204). The mineralology, the size and shape of the grains, internal stress, and other factors can initially influence the susceptibility value (Dearing 1999a, 1999b; Gale and Hoare 1991:204). However, of relevance to this discussion, factors including organic content, pedogenesis, thermal alteration, and cultural activities (e.g., ash-charcoal and refuse) can subsequently alter (usually increase) the susceptibility values. The implications of using MS for archeological research is that when examining either vertical or horizontal areas, the susceptibility values can assist in identifying cultural activity areas that may otherwise be blurred at a macro level. Horizontally, the application of MS analysis has been used to define the limits of cultural features and living spaces in excavation blocks (e.g., Mauldin and Figueroa 2006; Wiewel and Kvamme 2014). Vertically, the MS results have been applied to recognizing and delineating cultural horizons (e.g., Frederick 2010, 2012; Lawrence and Frederick 2012). For the investigations at 41DN612, MS samples were collected from profile walls of excavation units (Units) 1 and 2. The MS samples were collected up profile in systematic 3- to 5-cm intervals. Each of the samples were mapped into the profile, effectively correlating them to site stratigraphy and excavation elevation. The select samples were placed in 8-cc inert plastic cubes and correlated with the BHT stratigraphy. These samples were subsequently analyzed using a Bartington MS2 meter and MS2B dual frequency sensor to examine both low (χlf) and high (χhf) frequency (470 and 4700 Hz, respectively) MS. The cube samples were measured at the high sensitivity setting (i.e., 0.1) compared to the normal sensitivity setting (i.e., 1.0). The high sensitivity setting was necessary since the frequency dependency of the MS samples were also calculated (Gale and Hoare 1991:223–224). The values for each cube sample were catalogued twice at both the low and high frequencies to provide an average value using the Système International (SI) scale. Each of the χlf, χhf, and the average values were recorded, as well as each sample’s weight in an Excel table. After every five readings, the Bartington MS2 meter was “zeroed” out to recalibrate the meter. The cube samples from this analysis were subsequently calculated using the methods outlined by Gale and Hoare (1991:223–226). Briefly however, the primary metric for recognizing MS values of interest is the coefficient of frequency dependency (χfd). This calculation is indicated as: χfd= 100 [(χlf-χhf)/ χlf] This calculation uses χlf as the ‘normal’ sensitivity value and contrasts it with the χhf value to reveal significant values of difference in percentages (Dearing 1999a:47, 1999b:17–18; Gale and Hoare 1991:226). Elevated values of χfd can represent concentrations of ultrafine magnetic grains (e.g., maghemite), which are commonly associated with pedogenesis (Dalan 2006:164; 2008:22). Several factors may cause an increase in χlf values including biological (e.g., bacteria) or inorganic (e.g., natural weathering of iron minerals) processes. In an archeological context, an increase in χlf values may reflect cultural activities from thermal features and general refuse (Dalan 2008; Dalan and Banerjee 1998; Frederick 2012). Ideally, when applied to a vertical profile on 33 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 46

an archeological site, collected in systematic intervals (e.g., 2–3 cm), and contrasted with cultural data (e.g., artifact frequencies), this analysis can provide valuable data to interpret site formation and cultural activities. Maximum Firing Temperature (Burned Clay) Analysis As part of the geoarcheological investigations of 41DN612, Ken Lawrence and Ashley Eyeington determined the maximum firing temperature of the burned clay samples recovered from the site. These analyses were conducted on five samples from Units 2 and 3 and one sample collected during the scraping at the site. Previous researchers (i.e., Rasmussen et al. 2012) have determined using a systematic step-wise refiring in combination with magnetic susceptibility analysis that the maximum temperature of burned clay and pottery between 400–1000°C can be approximated with relative certainty to within +25.8°C accuracy. The methods are discussed in more detail below. Subsequent researchers (e.g., Goodwin and Hollenback 2016; Karacic et al. 2016; Kostadinova-Avramova et al. 2018; Jordanova et al. 2019; Tema and Ferrara 2019) have tested the general approach advocated by Rasmussen and others (2012) and found it to be valid. Notably, Tema and Ferrara (2019) in their experiments with natural clay and analysis of ancient clay from two archeological sites do advise some caution. Specifically, the researchers observed that maximum temperatures of burned clay determined to be <300–400°C should be used with extreme caution since the thermomagnetic curve can be reversible at those lower temperatures (Tema and Ferrara 2019:70–74). Overall, the research in the determination of maximum firing temperature of burned clay with magnetic susceptibility analysis is agreed to be an effective and acceptably precise approach. The primary objective of determining the maximum firing temperature of the burned clay nodules at 41DN612 was to discern if these items were the product of cultural activities or rather natural phenomenon (e.g., range fire). Various researchers (e.g., Bellomo 1993; Canti and Linford 2000) have conducted experiments using several approaches including MS. Bellomo (1993:533) determined a natural grass fire typically had temperatures between 100°C and 225°C, tree stump fires produce temperatures ranging from 200°C to 250°C, and campfires can produce temperatures between 400°C and 600°C. Similarly, Canti and Linford (2000) concluded that surface campfires (i.e., hearths) typically remain below 500°C with a commensurate drop in temperature with depth away from the heating element. Therefore, any evidence of maximum temperatures in the burned clay analyses exceeding 300°C is attributed to cultural activity. Admittedly, the context of the burned clay at 41DN612 was within a recognized cultural feature in a prehistoric site. Therefore, the inferential leap attributing these burned clay samples to cultural origins is not large. However, if the maximum firing temperature for each of these samples can be approximated then the second objective of the study might be accomplished, which is to examine integrity of the site and feature. The premise is that if the firing temperature of separate samples are “equivalent” then an inference might be made that they are associated with the same thermal event. If those “equivalent” samples are scattered over a broad vertical span (e.g., > 50 cm) but are within proximity to each other (e.g., < 2 m), then it could be interpreted as an indicator of vertical translocation. This inference is based on the discrete size of a typical thermal feature. Conversely, if the “equivalent” samples are in a very discrete vertical context (e.g., <10 cm) but broadly distributed horizontally (e.g., >2 m) then translocation of the samples should be considered. In the instance of a broad burned rock midden as found at 41DN612, the translocation may be attributed to the reuse and/or 34 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 47

extraction of the cooked foodstuffs. Regardless, any interpretations would ideally be made with additional corroboration with available data. The determination of the maximum firing temperature of the burned clay samples utilized the approach of Rasmussen and others (2012). Specifically, beginning at room temperature, the susceptibility values of the samples were repeatedly examined with a Bartington MS2 meter and MS2B dual frequency sensor. At the end of each interval of being gradually cooked in a muffle furnace at prescribed temperatures for certain intervals of time the average χlf and derivative χlf was calculated. Rasmussen et al. (2012:1706) recommends calculating and plotting the squared first derivative value against the next subsequent value to provide a more accurate representation of the step-wise refiring temperature. Put another way, the value at one temperature stage (e.g., 250°C) is compared against the value of the next higher temperature stage (e.g., 350°C) with the process repeated up to 1,000°C. Using the plotted data, a notable change should mark the point when the original maximum firing temperature is exceeded (for example see Rasmussen et al. 2012: Figure 1). Ultimately, the prominent spike of a sample is correlated to the temperature and compared with the temperatures identified by previous researchers (e.g., Bellomo 1993; Canti and Linford 2000) to provide a context whether the burned clay nodule is naturally or culturally derived. To take into account some of the noted caution by previous researchers (e.g., Tema and Ferrara 2019), a sample was split (if big enough) into multiple samples and the respective fragments were examined parallel to each other. This allowed for a validity test of the maximum firing temperature of the clay nodule. Unfortunately, an unburned clay sample from off site to use for comparison was not examined in this burned clay study. The burned clay study at 41DN612 was an analytical approach devised after the field investigations at the site. The burned clay study solely used recovered data from the excavations at the site. Notably, future burned clay studies would benefit from incorporating off site “baseline” data. Curation Per specifications of the ACT permit issued for the data recovery (Antiquities Permit No. 8872), all project documents and artifacts must be curated at an approved curatorial facility. Artifacts recovered during the investigations were prepared for permanent storage (e.g., washing, labeling, and cataloguing) and curation using state archeological standards. All curated artifacts will be housed at the Center for Archaeological Studies, Texas State University, San Marcos (CAS). 35 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

Chapter 5. Overview of Data Recovery Investigations This chapter summarizes the results of SWCA’s data recovery excavations at the Hardeman Midden Site (41DN612) that were conducted from April 14 to June 22, 2019, to mitigate project effects to the site. The results from the initial mechanical scraping, hand unit excavations, and mechanical trenching undertaken at the site are discussed, followed by a summary of the natural stratigraphy at the site. An overview of the radiocarbon and geoarcheological sample analyses results, particularly regarding site formation processes are also provided. Chapter 6 presents more-detailed information on recovered materials and cultural features encountered during these excavations. Previous work at the site during the initial survey by Raba Kistner was summarized in Chapter 1 (Neel and Meléndez 2019). Scraping Investigations During the initial scraping, soils were removed by backhoe from an 8×8-m block surrounding previously identified features identified during the initial APE survey (Neel and Meléndez 2019) (Figure 5.1; see Figure 1.4). During the monitoring of the scrape area, identified cultural materials were point plotted and all faunal bone and shell were collected. Burned rock were plotted and material types and size grades were noted; however, the rock was not collected. As previously mentioned, the initial 8×8-m excavation block was expanded to determine the extent of the BRM. This included scraping an additional 2 m to the east and excavation of BHT 1 to the south of the southeast corner of the excavation block (Figures 5.2 and 5.3). Figure 5.1. Hand Excavations A total of 3.21 m3 of soils were excavated across the site among three 1×1-m traditional excavation units (i.e., Units 1–3) and one 50×50-cm column sample (CS) unit (i.e., CS 1) (Table 5.1; see Figure 1.4). One feature- focused unit (i.e., Unit 4) was also excavated during the investigations.; however, no matrix was screened so this is not included in the total cubic meters of soils excavated. The units were laid out on top of the presumed central portions of the BRM (Feature A). During the hand excavations, one additional feature (Feature 1) was identified and sampled. Table 5.1. Excavated Volume of Hand Excavations Units Excavation Unit Excavation Unit Top Elev. Bottom Elev. No. of Total Cubes Size (m) (m) Levels Excavated Unit 1 1×1 m 153.24 152.30 9 0.94 Unit 2 1×1 m 153.24 152.30 9 0.94 Unit 3 1×1 m 153.25 152.30 9 0.95 CS1 50×50 cm 153.84 152.34 15 0.38 Total 3.21 The following section describes the findings within each excavation unit and column sample. The datum used for the units was shot in with the TDS and had an elevation of 153.5 m. The datum set up for the column samples had an elevation of 153.94 m. All excavation unit and column sample elevations referenced below are in relation to these datum elevations. Due to undulations in the ground surface, the first levels in the excavation units were thicker than 10 cm (e.g., Unit 1, Level 1 beginning at 153.24 m and terminating at 153.1 m [14-cm-thick level]). Unit 1 Unit 1 was placed over a dense concentration of burned rock exposed during the initial mechanical scraping (Figure 5.4). The unit was excavated in nine levels from elevation 153.24 to 152.3 m (26 to 120 cm below datum [cmbd]. A total volume of 0.94 m3 was excavated from Unit 1 (see Table 5.1). The excavations in Unit 1 revealed that the dense concentration of burned rock (Feature A) extended to approximately 58 cmbd or an elevation of 152.92 m. The concentration consisted of approximately 6 layers that extended throughout the entire unit. A dense cluster of large, tabular burned rock was encountered at 35 to 40 cmbd (153.15–153.1 m); this was interpreted to be an internal feature (possibly a heating element) within the larger BRM and was designated as Element 1 (see Feature A discussion below). Below that, between 58–80 cmbd (152.92–152.7 m), a sharp decrease in burned rock was encountered. In Level 6 (152.7– 152.6), within Stratum IV, an isolated burned rock concentration was observed within the central portion of the unit. Charcoal was observed within the center of the burned rock cluster. This phenomenon was designated as Feature 1 (see Feature 1 discussion below). Cultural materials encountered in Unit 1 included 29 faunal bone, four unmodified shell, one metal fragment, one clay artifact (i.e., burned clay), and over 40 kg of burned rock. In addition, 14 charcoal samples, eight feature matrix samples, four soil matrix samples, and 68 geoarcheological samples (59 MS and nine bulk matrix samples) were collected from Unit 1. 38 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 56

Column Sample (CS) 1 CS1 was excavated off the west wall of BHT 1 to explore both the historic and prehistoric deposits in this portion of the site. The CS was 50×50-cm in size and was excavated in 15 levels from elevation 153.84 to 152.34 m (10 to 160 cmbd) (Figure 5.11). A total volume of 0.38 m3 was excavated from CS1 (see Table 5.1). The CS encountered a disturbed upper zone in Levels 1 and 2 containing 175 pieces of historic debris (e.g., metal, glass, brick) that extended to 30 cmbd or an elevation of 153.64 m. Below this zone, isolated historic artifacts (i.e., single clear bottle glass fragments) were recovered from Levels 3, 4, and 6 and isolated prehistoric artifacts (i.e., burned rock, faunal bone) were recovered from Levels 6 and 7. A small cluster of burned rock was identified in the northeast corner of Level 8 between 80–90 cmbd (153.14–153.04 m). Only diffuse prehistoric artifacts including burned rock, faunal bone, mussel shell, and burned earth were encountered in the remaining levels between 130–160 cmbd (152.64–152.34 m). Historic artifacts encountered in CS1 included 146 glass fragments (6 amber/brown bottle, 127 colorless bottle 1 cobalt blue bottle, and 12 colorless window), one aluminum circular saw blade fragment, one brick fragment, one terracotta ceramic fragment, eight round-headed nails, one bolt, and 17 miscellaneous metal fragments. The prehistoric artifact assemblage included four unidentifiable faunal bone fragments, one burned clay nodule, one mussel shell identified as threeridge (Amblema plicata), and 13 burned limestone rock. ## Page 57

Backhoe Trench Investigations In addition to the scraping of the main excavation block and subsequent hand excavations, a series of BHTs were excavated. Specifically, 12 BHTs were placed over the main excavation block and an additional four BHTs were excavated south of this area (see Figure 1.5). The BHTs (BHTs A–P) were systematically placed and oriented to determine the site deposits and their character. BHTs A–D were excavated roughly east to west, while BHTs E–P were excavated north to south (Figure 5.12). During the BHT investigations at the site, five additional cultural features were identified (i.e., Features 2–6). A total of approximately 307.7 m3 of soils were excavated during the initial mechanical excavations and subsequent stripping. Site Stratigraphy The stratigraphy observed in the BHTs and in the excavation block area was very similar across the site (see Figure 5.12). Five horizons were observed extending to approximately 195 cmbs (Appendix 1). The typical stratigraphy observed in the BHTs consisted of a surface horizon (Stratum I) of brown to dark grayish brown (10YR 4/3–4/2) firm, silt clay with angular blocky structure, subrounded pebbles (5 percent), asphalt chunks (1 percent) with a clear and smooth lower boundary (Appendix 1). Stratum I also contained clear and brown colored glass fragments. Beginning around 12–15 cmbs, the second horizon (Stratum II) consists of a brown (10YR 4/3) friable silty clay loam with a subangular blocky structure with a clear and smooth lower boundary at approximately 40–45 cmbs (Appendix 1). Stratum II also contained clear glass fragments. The third horizon (Stratum III) consists of a black to very dark brown (10YR 2/1–2/2) friable silt clay loam with a subangular blocky structure, 1-mm-diameter calcium carbonate (CaCO3) filaments, dendritically arranged (2 percent) with a clear and smooth lower boundary (Appendix 1). Stratum III also contained scattered burned rock. Beginning around 85–100 cmbs, the fourth horizon (Stratum IV) consists of dark grayish brown (10YR 4/2) friable, clay loam with angular blocky structure, 1-mm-diameter CaCO3 filaments, dendritically arranged (5 percent), with a gradual and smooth lower boundary (Appendix 1). Stratum IV contained scattered burned rock. Beginning around 150–180 cmbs, the fifth horizon (Stratum V) consists of brown to yellowish brown (10YR 5/3–5/4) friable, clay loam with 1- to 3-cm slickensides (3 percent), 1-mm-diameter CaCO3 filaments (2 percent) and an unobserved lower boundary (Appendix 1). Stratum V contained scattered burned rock. Notably, a large section of the site and scrape area has been disturbed by the excavation of a large refuse pit (Figure 5.13; see Figure 1.4). The linear pit was roughly oriented southwest to northeast and was about 35 feet long and minimally 12 feet wide. The southern end of the refuse pit was not observed and certainly extends south of the southernmost BHT (BHT D). The refuse pit was excavated into Strata II–IV and capped by Stratum I. No evidence was observed that the refuse pit was excavated into Stratum V. The base of the refuse pit was deepest (i.e., 133 cmbs) in BHT D and gradually got shallower to the north between BHT A (i.e., 109 cmbs) and the excavation block where the refuse pit was solely confined to Stratum II. The refuse pit was observed to contain dense brick fragments, numerous complete or nearly complete glass bottles, abundant glass fragments (i.e., cobalt, colorless, milk and brown glass), abundant tile, whiteware, a washing machine, a doll leg, foundation rubble, a pipe, a 50-gallon drum, a pot, barbed wire, and miscellaneous metal. The foundation rubble, washing machine, and pipe was limited to BHT A and B (Figures 5.14 and 5.15). 45 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 60

Figure 5.15. Washing machine, facing north. Within BHT 1, the historic debris appeared to have been burned based on the presence of melted glass, charred materials, and dark soil matrix. A representative sample of complete glass bottles (n=27) was collected from the backdirt and within BHT 1. Based on the observed temporally diagnostic artifacts within the refuse pit, the assemblage likely dates from the 1890s to the 1960s (Intermountain Antiquities Computer System Guide [IMACS] 2001; Society for Historical Archaeology [SHA] 2020). In addition, faunal bone collected from Level 7 of CS1 (70–80 cmbs; 153.24–153.14 m) was radiocarbon dated to 1955–1956 cal AD. It appears that the pit was excavated (likely by a bulldozer) subsequent to 1950 from the north to south toward Denton Creek, and then filled in with assorted debris and capped. Although not a perfect correlation with the mapped soil pedons for the area (i.e., Frio and Medlin-Sanger series), the stratigraphy encountered at 41DN612 most closely matches the pedon attributed to the Frio series (NRCS 2020). The Frio series has a recorded pedon of A1 (0–20 cmbs), A2 (20–56 cmbs), A3 (56–74 cmbs), A4 (74–102 cmbs), and Bk (102–203+ cmbs) (NRCS 2020). Within 41DN612, the observed soil pedon is interpreted to be an Ap1 (~0–12 cmbs), Ap2 (~12–45 cmbs), Ab (~45–95 cmbs), Bk (~95–170 cmbs), and Bkss (~170 cmbs+). 48 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas
