## Page 61

Chapter 6. Geoarcheological Analyses This chapter summarizes the results of radiocarbon, particle size distribution, and MS analyses conducted at 41DN612 previously presented in the Analytical Unit Definition and Geoarcheology Report (Lawrence et al. 2020). Radiocarbon Analysis Twenty samples (mixture of faunal bone and charcoal) were selected for radiocarbon analysis at 41DN612 to provide chronostratigraphic data for the site (Table 6.1; Appendix 7). All faunal bone and macrobotanical samples were identified to the most specific taxonomic order possible to inform the selection of radiocarbon samples for Accelerated Mass Spectrometry (AMS) dating (e.g., selection of short-lived, carbonized, or economic species) (Appendices 5 and 8). Of the 20 samples, 15 radiocarbon samples had a good elevational and stratigraphic context (Figures 6.1 and 6.2). These samples were modelled in OxCal v4.3.2 by stratigraphic order with the Phase model under the Sequential setting. Four phases were created for this modelling analysis. Specifically, the samples confidently associated with Stratum III deposits were assigned under a phase (i.e., Stratum III), samples located at the base of Stratum III or top of Stratum IV were assigned under Stratum III/IV phase, samples confidently associated with Stratum IV deposits were assigned under a phase (i.e., Stratum IV), while the sample confidently associated with Stratum V deposits was assigned under the Stratum V phase. The results of the modelling illustrated a reasonably good stratigraphic order through the 41DN612 excavations (Figure 6.3). Most interesting is that the dates tend to group into two periods regardless of assigned horizon (Figure 6.4). The most recent group crosses Stratum III, Stratum III/IV, and one of the Stratum IV samples roughly falls between 1540 and 1200 cal yr BP. The older group is within Stratum IV and V and roughly falls between 2080 and 1820 cal yr BP (see Figure 6.4). The vertical spread of the more recent group of ages (1540–1200 cal yr BP) across Strata III and IV is likely attributed to the use and reworking of the BRM designated Feature A. This thick scatter of broadly distributed burned rock of Feature A was certainly utilized multiple times by prehistoric inhabitants. Consequently, the excavation of the food materials and subsequent reuse of the BRM at an unknown time likely mixed the dateable materials. It is interesting that within the nine assays composing the 1540–1200 cal yr BP group of ages, the deepest assay (S-18) dates to and clusters with assays S-14 and S-166 around 1310 to 1290 cal yr BP, while the shallowest assay (S-32) clusters with assays B-7, S-36, and S-154 around 1530 to 1460 cal yr BP. Simply put, the oldest age is shallower, while the younger age is stratigraphically deeper (see Figure 6.4). For the older group of ages identified at 41DN612 (i.e., 2080–1820 cal yr BP), a similar argument (i.e., mixing and reuse) is more tenuous due to the good context of the Stratum V assay (S-155) that is approximately 32 cm below the next assay in the group of ages (B-6) in Stratum IV. The almost perfect overlap of these two assays is undetermined with the current data. Unfortunately, Bayesian modelling of the assays through OxCal provided no resolution. Regardless, both groups of dates fall in the period commonly recognized as the Transitional Archaic (Turner et al. 2011:51). The older group in Stratum IV and possibly Stratum V falls near the beginning of the Transitional Archaic, while the more recent group in Stratum III and the top of Stratum IV falls near the end of the Transitional Archaic period. 49 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 68

Notably, one assay (S-40) falls between the two identified age clusters at 41DN612 (see Figure 6.4). This sample was collected from BHT A within Stratum IV and it is separated from the excavation block assays composing most of the chronometric data for 41DN612. The S-40 assay has a very large temporal span and roughly dates to 1700–1550 cal yr BP. This age for S-40 falls closer to the younger age group cluster identified at 41DN612. The rate of deposition on this small sample of dates at 41DN612 was grossly approximated comparing the more recent, shallower ages against the oldest, deepest assay. The approximate rate of deposition at 41DN612 across these prehistoric occupations appears to be approximately 7.5 cm per century. The gap between the two age groups were also compared using sample B-5 in Stratum IV and S-154 in Stratum III/IV. There is approximately 320 years between these two assays and at least 22 cm in elevation, which would approximate a rate of deposition 14.5 cm per century. This estimate doubles the overall estimation of deposition at 41DN612 and Denton Creek. The previously mentioned investigations by Abbott (n.d.:7) on Denton Creek, upstream from 41DN612, noted that Denton Creek around 1740 B.P. avulsed (i.e., abandoned its channel) and began forming a new channel. This dramatic change to the Denton Creek drainage likely explains the notable increase in sedimentation separating the two age groups observed at 41DN612. Further, the aforementioned West Fork paleosol developed within and caps the Pilot Point alloformation, which itself dates from 4,500 to 1,800 years ago (Abbott 2011). By extension then, the West Fork paleosol would date to the latter parts of this period. Previous researchers (Hall et al. 2011), conducting investigations on the Clear Fork of the Trinity River, examined the West Fork paleosol and refined the age of the buried soil as dating more specifically from 2300–1000 years ago. These dates, performed on humate samples, aligned stratigraphically in comparison to charcoal assays in the study (Hall et al. 2011:542–544). The West Fork paleosol encompasses the two age groups observed in the radiocarbon analyses at 41DN612. Further, the observed Stratum III was a buried soil and appears to correlate with the West Fork paleosol, while the underlying Stratum IV and V are similarly associated with the Pilot Point alloformation. Particle Size Distribution A series of soil geomatrix samples were collected at 41DN612 to help characterize the deposits (Figure 6.5). The geomatrix samples (n=10) were predominantly collected from Unit 1 to sample each of the identified strata. One of the samples (S-153) was collected from BHT N (adjacent to Unit 1) that targeted Stratum V at 152.0–151.9 m elevation. The results of the particle size texture analyses from the geomatrix samples by the Texas A&M Soil Characterization Laboratory indicates that Stratum I is a loam, Stratum II is a clay loam, Strata III and IV are clays, and Stratum V is a silty clay to clay (Table 6.2). 56 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

One MS column was collected from a profile in Unit 1 (see Figure 6.5). The second column was collected from a profile in Unit 2 (Figure 6.6). The two columns were positioned on opposite ends of the excavation block with the Unit 1 column on the south end and Unit 2 column on the north end. The southern MS column in Unit 1 extended vertically from the ground surface to the top of Stratum V. The northern MS column in Unit 2 began in the middle of Stratum III and continued downward into Stratum V. Effectively, the two MS columns overlap vertically through 41DN612 deposits. The MS results for the Unit 1 column suggests dynamic fluctuations in the upper two strata that are associated with the disturbance from the refuse pit (see Figure 6.5). Within Stratum III, the MS results for Unit 1 column shows a dramatic and steady increase in values between 153.41 and 153.07 m (see Figure 6.5). These increased values coincide with the zone of BRM designated as Feature A (Figures 6.7 and 6.8). The underlying Stratum IV has areas of increased value, but these spikes are more localized (see Figure 6.5). The spikes in values occur roughly between 152.99 and 152.95 m (see Figure 6.5). As noted previously, Stratum IV contained scattered burned rock throughout the horizon and contained several discrete features. One feature (Feature 1) is recorded near the Unit 2 MS column that likely correlates with the increased MS at 152.99– 152.95 m (see Figure 6.6). Below these spikes in χlf and χhf values in Stratum IV, the values very gradually decrease with depth as the column descends into Stratum V. The MS in Unit 1 within Stratum V indicates the χlf and χhf values were still gradually decreasing in value at the base of the column (see Figure 6.5). 58 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 74

Similarly, the MS analyses within Unit 2 column shows a dramatic and steady increase in values between 153.24 and 153.14 m (see Figure 6.6. These increased values may coincide with the zone of BRM designated as Feature A (see Figures 6.7 and 6.8). More obvious is the elevated values in the underlying Stratum IV (see Figure 6.6). The spikes in values occur roughly between 152.99 and 152.76 m (see Figure 6.6). Near the MS column is a recorded feature (Feature 1) that certainly correlates with the increased MS at 152.99–152.76 m (see Figure 6.6). Below these spikes in χlf and χhf values in Stratum IV, the values very gradually decrease with depth as the column descends into Stratum V. The MS in Unit 2 within Stratum V indicates the χlf and χhf values were still gradually decreasing in value at the base of the column (see Figure 6.6). The results of the MS analyses were combined for the two columns and organized by elevation (Figure 6.9). As with the results in the individual columns, the dynamic fluctuations appear to primarily correlate with recent disturbance in Strata I and II, Feature A (BRM) in Stratum III, and Feature 1 in Stratum IV (see Figure 6.9). Below Feature 1, the MS values gradually decrease with a few, isolated fluctuations down into Stratum V. Burned Clay MS Analyses The analyses of the maximum firing temperature of burned clay were conducted on six samples at 41DN612 with two in Unit 2 (i.e., Samples 1 and 2), three in Unit 3 (i.e., Samples 3, 4, and 5), and one found during mechanical trenching (i.e., Sample 6). This analysis was conducted to accomplish two objectives. The first primary objective was to approximately determine the maximum firing temperature of the encountered burned clay samples and by extension, determine if the samples were attributed to natural or cultural processes. As noted above in the methods section, previous researchers (i.e., Bellomo 1993; Canti and Linford 2000) have correlated specific temperature ranges to natural fires versus cultural thermal activities. The analyses used a slightly modified system used by previous researchers (Rasmussen et al. 2012). When possible, each burned clay sample was broken into two subsamples (i.e., Samples 1A and 1B, 2A and 2B, 3A and 3B, and 6A and 6B) for a total of 10 samples. Notably, two samples (i.e., Samples 4 and 5) were too small to divide into subsamples. Each subsample split was of a comparable size and allowed the respective sample results to be compared and verified (Tables 6.3 and 6.4). Of note, the MS analysis used S.I. 0.1 and recorded both low (χlf) and high (χhf) frequencies to determine if any discrepancies were present. Since all the results were consistent, only one of the data rows (i.e., χlf) was selected for illustration. SWCA calculated both the average χlf and derivative χlf as recommended by Rasmussen et al. (2012:1706). The derivative process is an unambiguous method to establish the threshold temperature rather than solely reading the susceptibility values versus the temperature values. For comparison at 41DN612, the results of the χlf values of the refiring are illustrated in Figure 6.10, while the derivative values of those samples are illustrated in Figure 6.11. The χlf values of Samples 1A, 2A, 3A, 4, and 5, do not exhibit any fluctuation until 850°C, whereas Samples 1B, 2B, 3B, and 6A and 6B were unchanged (see Figure 6.10). In the derivative values, Samples 1A, 1B, 2A, 2B, and 3A 62 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

Samples 3B, 6A, and 6B exhibited no change in the derivative values. In general, the results of the derivative χlf of all six samples suggest that the burned clay samples do not have a clearly defined maximum firing temperature above 250°C (see Figure 6.11). Without a demonstrable change across the derivative results for the burned clay, at least two options are possible: 1) the maximum firing temperatures were at or below 250°C, which is in the natural fire temperature range (see Bellomo 1993:533), or 2) the samples were located on the periphery of a low-temperature cooking event (see Canti and Linford 2000). Given the abundance of burned rock, the latter seems to be the case. This is particularly true regarding Samples 1, 2, 3, and 4, which are all located within or in proximity to the large BRM identified as Feature A. Sample 5 is not definitively associated with any feature and may be the result of natural firing or may have been displaced by natural processes such as bioturbation. ## Page 79

collected by TxDOT geoarcheologist Jim Abbott during mechanical scraping and no provenience was available; therefore, the sample cannot be placed into spatial context with the identified features at the site. Regarding the fluctuation in derivative values observed for most of the samples above 850°C, this is inferred to be attributed to natural processes (see Figure 6.11). This interpretation is based on several factors including the absence of pottery at 41DN612 and the consistency across the samples at that threshold. As mentioned previously, researchers have identified that a typical thermal feature reaches temperatures above 250°C and below 500°C (Bellomo 1993; Canti and Linford 2000). In contrast, researchers have previously estimated the firing temperatures of ceramics to range from a low of 600°C–650°C (Zhushchikhovskaya and Nikitin 2014:124) to 800°C–900°C (Bong et al. 2008:304; Rasmussen et al. 2012). Understandably, the range of firing temperatures will vary depending on temper, technology, and fuel source to name a few factors. Based on these data, the range of firing temperatures for intentional ceramic production is interpreted to exceed 650°C. Therefore, the absence of ceramics at 41DN612 is notable. If the fluctuation in derivative values at temperatures above 850°C in the 41DN612 samples was attributed to cultural activities, then some presence of ceramics at the site would be expected. Rather, the derivative value fluctuation observed above 850°C is likely mineralogical in origin. Previous researchers have noted that there can be a destruction or formation of minerals at higher temperatures, particularly above 800°C (Bong et al. 2008:303–304; Rasmussen et al. 2012:1711– 1712). Considering the consistency of the 41DN612 samples exhibiting a change at the 850°C, temperature does suggest a common cause (e.g., mineralogical). To resolve this question a mineralogical analysis of the samples (e.g., x-ray diffraction) would need to be conducted. With the available data the most likely answer for this fluctuation is that it is attributed to natural causes and these samples had not previously been heated to that high temperature (i.e., >850°C). Accordingly, the fluctuation at 850°C indicated by the derivative values in these samples is not interpreted to be from cultural activities. In summary, there is no evidence of the burned clay samples at 41DN612 having a clearly defined maximum firing temperature above 250°C. This may be that the temperatures never fully exceeded 300–400°C for long durations of time as cautioned by previous researchers (e.g., Tema and Ferrara 2019). Another possibility may be suggested by previous researchers working on hearth features in France (Brodard et al. 2012). These researchers used thermoluminescence properties of quartz and MS of sediment systematically around a prehistoric hearth to identify the maximum temperature (i.e., paleotemperature) of the samples in relation to the hearth (Brodard et al. 2012). Not surprisingly, the MS values dramatically decreased further away from the hearth. Interestingly, the main hearth exceeded 600°C and the thermal map suggests a reduction to <350°C within 70 cm (Brodard et al. 2012:357). Regardless, the results at 41DN612 suggest that the burned clay samples were either thermally altered at temperatures below 250°C from a natural fire, not heated above 300°C for extended periods of time, or on the periphery of a thermal feature (Brodard et al. 2012; Canti and Linford 2000). Based on the context of most of these samples clearly within a 67 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 80

cultural feature, they appear to have been on the periphery of a main thermal heating source (i.e., Feature A). Analytical Unit Definitions and Integrity Overall, the data indicate that at least two periods of prehistoric occupation occurred at 41DN612 during the Transitional Archaic that are separated by an apparent depositional change along the Denton Creek drainage. The older group is associated with Stratum IV and roughly falls between 2080 and 1820 cal yr BP (i.e., Lower Analytical Unit). The more recent activities (i.e., Upper Analytical Unit) are associated with Stratum III and the BRM phenomenon (Feature A) subsequent to the changes to Denton Creek (around 1740 B.P.) and occurred around 1540–1200 cal yr BP (Figure 6.12). For most of 41DN612, the Upper Analytical Unit (1540–1200 cal yr BP) has been truncated and removed by the historic and recent refuse disposal activities. This disturbance from the refuse disposal activity to the Upper Analytical Unit is primarily located south of the excavation block and extends over 26 feet to the south (see Figure 1.4). On the northern half of the excavation block, road construction fill was observed to partially truncate and cap the upper horizons (Figure 6.13). This fill was observed to extend over the northern third of the excavation block (see Figure 6.7). Fortunately, the Upper Analytical Unit (1540–1200 cal yr BP) primarily associated with Stratum III appears to be unaffected by the road construction fill, as the horizon was sloping downward in this portion of the block (see Figure 6.7). Portions of the Lower Analytical Unit (2080–1820 cal yr BP) observed in Stratum IV and the upper portions of Stratum V appears to have some disturbance. As with the Upper Analytical Unit, the historic refuse pit was seen to intrude into Stratum IV across BHTs A–D (see Figures 1.4 and 6.7). The lowermost Stratum V appeared to be unaffected by the refuse pit excavation in the vicinity of the excavation block. Overall, within the excavation block, Strata IV and V containing the Lower Analytical Unit (2080–1820 cal yr BP) appeared to be intact. Summary The stratigraphy at 41DN612 appears to correlate with the Pilot Point alloformation, which is capped by the West Fork paleosol. The Pilot Point alloformation is recognized as dating to 4,500–1,800 years ago, while the West Fork paleosol is interpreted to date to 2,300–1,000 years ago (Abbott 2011; Hall et al. 2011:542–544). Strata I and II are associated with modern and historic disturbances; however, Stratum III appears to correlate with the West Fork paleosol, while the underlying Stratum IV and V correlate with the Pilot Point alloformation. The prehistoric deposits at 41DN612 appear to be solely associated with the Transitional Archaic. The Upper Analytical Unit falls at the end of the Transitional Archaic, while the Lower Analytical Unit occurs at the beginning of the Transitional Archaic. These analytical units appear to be separated by a recognized period of change within Denton Creek when the drainage channel changed course around 1,740 years ago (Abbott n.d.:7). The change to the Denton Creek drainage likely explains the notable increase in sedimentation separating the two age groups observed at 41DN612. 68 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 83

Chapter 7. Artifacts, Ecofacts, and Features Archeology is the study of the material record to derive interpretations of past societies and their contexts. But what endures through the destructive processes of time is often only a small fraction of the overall material culture. Items manufactured from hides, wood, and other perishables disintegrated long ago, and are generally lost to the archeological record. In addition to natural processes that bias the archeological record, there are social filters that influence what is discarded on residential sites and what is discarded elsewhere. Accordingly, archeological assemblages are inaccurate reflections of the total material culture of any given past society. This chapter presents descriptive data on the features, artifacts, and ecofacts that were investigated or recovered during the excavations. The materials are not organized by component or stratum, but rather by basic descriptive categories. The intent of this chapter is to present the data as objectively as possible with only brief chronological or stratigraphic divisions. A few low-level interpretations are provided on functions of tools and features, but these are kept at a minimum. The subsequent chapter will develop more of an interpretive context that will address the issues of moving from the material record to inferences on prehistoric society and behavior. Cultural materials recovered during the data recovery excavations on site 41DN612 include features and artifact categories composed of various materials, including historic-age materials and floral and faunal remains (Appendix 3). Stone usually comprises the vast majority of artifact assemblages at sites as a result of depositional and post-depositional (e.g., preservation) site formation processes; however, site 41DN612 is unique in the fact that lithic tools and debitage were absent and the only stone recovered was abundant burned rock. Site 41DN612, however, yielded a relatively good collection of perishable materials, including bone, shell, and floral remains. Some of these are artifacts, and many are ecofacts, defined as plants, animals, or rocks that were not clearly culturally modified or produced but were nevertheless culturally introduced or otherwise part of the overall site context. Charcoal and bone, which forms the basis for determining the site’s chronological parameters through radiocarbon dating, were abundant and well preserved. Dates reported in this chapter are corrected, 2-Sigma calibrated dates (cal yr B.P.). Both macro- and micro-floral remains were also well preserved. Artifacts Over the course of the data recovery efforts, SWCA collected 519 artifacts (Table 7.1; Appendix 3). Historic artifacts (n=209) encompassed the majority of the artifact assemblage (Appendix 4). Burned rock was abundant (n=6,429) but was counted and discarded in the field for the most part except for 32 examples of burned rock, which were included in the total collected artifact count above (Appendix 2). Faunal remains formed the next largest category, with a total of 155 unmodified mussel shells and 99 bone fragments; however, the majority of bone were very fragmented and likely originated from relatively few individual specimens (Appendix 5 and 6). In addition, 21 burned clay artifacts were recovered. 71 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 87

Lastly, three brick fragments and two ceramic sherds were recovered during the current investigation. The ceramic sherds include one slip decorated creamware sherd (Lot No. 132-9) and one terracotta sherd (Lot No. 231-8). Creamware was introduced in the latter parts of the eighteenth century as an alternative to china and continued to be made until ca. 1820 (Aultman et al. 2014; Marciniszyn 2017). Two of the three brick fragments (Lot No. 1-16) are stamped with “FERRIS” and were recovered from the surface of the Southern Scrape Area. These bricks are marked with the Ferris Brick Company maker’s mark, who operated the company from 1923–present (Hart 2010; McKnight and Dunn 2016). Burned Clay SWCA recovered 21 pieces of burned clay during excavations conducted at site 41DN612. All the pieces of burned clay exhibited amorphous shapes, with sizes ranging from 10–40 mm in diameter. A representative sampling of the pieces of burned clay is depicted in Figure 7.5. Most of the burned clay was recovered from in and around Feature A (discussed below), a large BRM radiocarbon dated to the late Transitional Archaic. The pieces of burned clay were associated with charcoal flecks, burned bone, mussel shell fragments, and abundant FCR. a. b. Figure 7.5. Representative sample of burned clay nodules collected from site 41DN612 (a. Lot No. 139-1; b. Lot No. 210-6 [two nodules]). Given their association with a hot rock cooking feature, the pieces of burned clay were tested to see if they were utilized as heating elements for earth ovens. Pieces of burned clay have been associated with earth ovens archeologically at sites across North and Central America (Hays et al. 2016; Simms et al. 2013). In experimental recreations, amorphous clay balls have been found to retain heat longer than fire embers and serve to replenish stored heat through conduction (Hunter 1975:67; Hudgins 1993:51). Alternatively, the 75 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


![Image 3 - Page 87](assets/Nielsen et al 2021 - Hardeman Midden Site (41DN612) Data Recovery/Nielsen et al 2021 - Hardeman Midden Site (41DN612) Data Recovery_p87_img3.png)

## Page 88

pieces of burned clay could result from natural causes, such as burning brush or tree roots in clay or sandy soils (Hudgins 1993). At the Loeve-Fox site (41WM230) Prewitt (1982) identified a concentration of burned clay that was interpreted as a burned tree root. The pieces of burned clay depicted in the site report appear similar to those recovered from this site (Prewitt 1982:Figure 14a). The small size of the burned clay (i.e., 10–40 mm) at site 41DN612 suggests they may be a result of natural causes or they were unintentionally manufactured along the periphery of the burned rock features during firing. Recent studies have shown that microbotanical residues from plants and other materials cooked in earth ovens become embedded in cook stones used as heating elements within the oven (Thoms et al. 2015). Furthermore, the maximum firing temperature analysis results presented in Chapter 6 support the interpretation that the burned clay nodules from the site were thermally altered as a result of natural fire or due to proximity to a thermal feature (i.e., Feature A) (Canti and Linford 2000). Based on the context of the burned clay, they appear to have been on the periphery of a thermal feature (i.e., Feature A). Burned Rock Burned rock from each of the hand-excavated units and features contexts was first sorted by materials type (e.g., limestone, sandstone) and then size-sorted into four size grade categories (0–5 cm, 5–10 cm, 10–15 cm, and 15+ cm diameter). The rocks were then counted and weighed by material type and size grade (Appendix 2). A total of 6,429 burned rock was documented within the controlled hand-excavation units and features at site 41DN612. Most of the rock (n=6,068; 94.4 percent) was associated with Feature A. Burned limestone (n=6,266) was prevalent in the site deposits and accounted for 97.5 percent of the burned rock assemblage. Silicified limestone was present (n= 147; 2.3 percent) along with very sparse Quartzite (n=7; 0.1 percent) and Sandstone (n=3; <0.1 percent). The majority of burned rock was within the 0–5 cm diameter size grade class (n=5459; 84.9 percent); which is indicative of reuse of the features and would be expected within a large BRM. A total of 784 rocks were within the 5–10 cm diameter size class, comprising 12.2 percent of the assemblage. The 10–15 cm diameter size class included a total of 160 burned rock (2.5 percent) and the 15+ cm diameter size class contained 27 burned rock (0.4 percent). The total weight for the burned rock at the site was 179 kg. The highest total weight was within the 5–10 cm diameter size class (74 kg; 41.4 percent), followed by the 10–15 cm diameter size class (61 kg; 34.2 percent). The 0–5 cm diameter size grade class had a total weight of 25 kg (14.0 percent) and the 15+ cm diameter size class had a total weight of 19 kg (10.4 percent). The count, weight, and size distribution of the burned rocks were used to estimate number of cooking events within each of the features (see Chapter 8). Faunal Remains Bone SWCA recovered and analyzed a total of 99 vertebrate faunal bone from the excavations (Appendix 5). As a result of the high degree of fragmentation, weathering, and other taphonomic factors, few of the fragments 76 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 89

could be attributed to specific elements and/or specific taxa (Appendix 5). From the vertebrate assemblage, three specimens could be attributed to biological class Aves, 14 specimens of the biological order Testudines (turtle/tortoise), and 36 specimens could be attributed to class Mammalia. Two specimens from class Mammalia could be attributed to large (cow/bison size) bovid, and two more within the Mammalia class could be attributed to the order Rodentia. Only nine specimens could be accurately attributed to the species level, all of which are white-tailed deer (Odocoileus virginianus). Bone preservation within the assemblage ranged from excellent to weathered beyond recognition. The most prevalent taphonomic processes identified during the analysis are general weathering, geogenic acid etching, and rodent gnawing (Appendix 5). The assemblage is highly fragmented, with only seven (7.1 percent of the assemblage) whole elements. Of the seven whole elements, six have been identified as distal limb elements attributed to white-tailed deer. The whole element not attributed to species level is a small juvenile mammalian femur measuring 8.5 cm in length with growth plates missing from each epiphysis. Anthropogenic taphonomy is present on the surface of seven specimens, all of which have been identified as white-tailed deer, or as deer-sized mammal elements that could not be definitively attributed to species due to the small size of the fragments and lack of identifying landmarks (Appendix 5). A proximal epiphysis of a tibia attributed to white-tailed deer (Lot No. 228-1) exhibited a percussion impact with surrounding crushed bone and two additional percussion notches on the oblique, spirally fractured cortical margin. A long bone mid-shaft fragment attributed to a deer-sized mammal (Lot No. 23-1) also exhibited a percussion notch on an oblique, spiral cortical margin. The surfaces of both specimens with impact marks were moderately obscured by geogenic acid root etching, which possibly obscured any additional anthropogenic marks (e.g., cut marks). The percussion marks on the long bone elements generally indicate marrow processing. The oblique angles and spiral shape of the fracture would indicate perimortem (green) fractures (Johnson 1985). A total of four elements from the assemblage exhibited anthropogenic cut marks on the cortical surface. A long bone mid-shaft fragment (Lot No. 23-5) exhibited five v-shaped, parallel cut marks, and was found in the same location as the mid-shaft fragment with a percussion notch (Lot No. 23-1). Two additional deer-sized, long bone mid-shaft fragments (Lot Nos. 155-1 and 156-1) also had parallel cut marks on the cortical surface and were found in Unit 1 between 90 and 102 cmbd (Figure 7.6). At least six cut marks are present at the distal end of a proximal phalanx attributed to white-tailed deer (Lot No. 242-1). In addition to the cut marks, a calcaneus (Lot No. 41-1), attributed to white-tailed deer, exhibited deep chop marks (Figure 7.7). The interior surface of the chop marks has been soil stained to the same degree as the surrounding cortical surface, indicating that the marks were made near the time of deposition and are not the result of damage occurring during excavation. The cut marks located at the midshaft are likely the result of meat package removal, while the chop marks and cut marks located near distal limb joints are likely an indication of disarticulation and/or skinning (Binford 1981:46-47). 77 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 91

Only two fragments in the assemblage (Lot Nos. 72-1 and 249-B1) exhibited indications of burning. Both specimens were small, unidentifiable fragments, which were burned to nearly calcined. One specimen (Lot No. 241-1), a long bone mid-shaft fragment attributed to a deer-sized mammal, exhibited a small patch of polish on the cortex near one of the obliquely fractured ends. The polish could be attributed to pot polish, polishing from the surrounding soil, or from gastric acid. No other specimens in the assemblage exhibited similar polish. However, small carnivore tooth pits and U-shaped tooth drags were observed on the cortical surface of the specimen, thus faunal gastric acid is the likely cause. The number of specimens within the assemblage is relatively low. Although biological class Aves and biological orders Bovidae, Testudines, and Rodentia are all represented in the assemblage, only nine elements could be attributed to species. All nine of these elements were attributed to a single species, white-tailed deer, and each of the elements present are represented only once, except for two 3rd phalanxes, which cannot be accurately sided. Therefore, the number of identified specimens per taxon (NISP, using species) is nine, while the minimum number of individuals (MNI) is one (Grayson 1984). Additionally, the observed anthropogenic taphonomy can be associated with butchery and was only present on elements attributed to white-tailed deer and deer-sized mammal elements. Thus, the butchery of a single deer can securely be attributed to the site assemblage. Shell Other faunal remains encountered during excavations included 89 freshwater mussel shell umbos and 66 observed fragments, for a total of 155 collected specimens. SWCA biologist Stephen Van Kampen-Lewis conducted analysis of the shell assemblage, focusing on identifying the various species of mussel shell commonly found in the Trinity River drainage system in Denton County. A total of five species were identified for 66 mussel shells within the 41DN612 assemblage; the remaining shells were too fragmented for a definitive identification (Table 7.2; Figure 7.8). ## Page 92

a. b. c. d. e. Figure 7.8. Example of five mussel shell species identified within site 41DN612 assemblage (a. Threeridge [Lot No. 101-1]; b. Lilliput [Lot No. 12-1]; c. Threehorn wartyback [Lot No. 108-1]; d. Louisiana pigtoe [Lot No. 81-1]; and e. Rock pocketbook [Lot No. 128-1]). The vast majority of the shells was associated with cultural features, and 32 percent of the assemblage (n=49) exhibited evidence of cultural modification through battering, cutting, burning, and other damage from harvesting or use. Accordingly, these shells are cultural artifacts or ecofacts that indicate subsistence. Battering was the most common modification observed; however, one specimen (Lot No. 195-1) was burned and two specimens (Lot Nos. 102-1 and 198-1) had evidence of possible cutting (Figures 7.9). Multiple parallel lines were observed under microscopic view on Lot No. 102-1, which appear to be cut marks (Figure 7.10). Parallel grooves were observed on Lot No. 198-1; these were not as distinct but are also possible cut marks (Figure 7.11). Of note, the remaining 106 shell specimens (68 percent) were either too fragmentary or had too much post-depositional damage to determine if any kind of cultural modification was present. Texas’ freshwater mussels exhibit phenotypic plasticity depending on inhabited drainage and/or specific environmental conditions that can change the shell shape (e.g., elongate forms occurring in swiftwater and rounded forms occurring in slackwater). Some species are also sexually dimorphic, further complicating identification. As such, identifying individuals is often difficult, even when assessed by experts (Hess et al. 2018). However, knowing a mussel’s home drainage greatly reduces the potential list of species and increases the potential for correct identification. SWCA assumed all shells are from the Trinity River drainage and the species list was assembled using an online database from Randklev et al. (2020) that lists documented species from Denton County. Identification guides by Graf and Cummings (2020) and Howells (2014) were also consulted for shell identification. 80 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 94

Figure 7.11. Lot No. 198-1 Closeup (0.75x magnification) ventral side with parallel grooves that are possible cut marks illustrated. Overall, the shells and shell fragments represent a mix of currently common and uncommon species known to inhabit the Trinity River drainage. Most identified species are from areas with stable substrate in moving water such as creeks or rivers. The most common identified species (Threeridge) is known from both rivers and impoundments. No species were identified that are usually found in slow-moving backwaters or impoundments with soft, muddy substrate. In addition to a food source, these shells might have been selected for use as pendants or other adornments; however, no shell ornaments were identified within the 41DN612 assemblage. Vertical mussel migration within the substrate during stressful conditions is well documented throughout the United States, with many species burying themselves deeper during winter (Amyot and Downing 1997; Block et al. 2013; Schwalb and Pusch 2007). The Texas Parks and Wildlife Department (TPWD 2020a) prefers mussel relocations from April through November or when water temperatures are above 60°F, based on relocation protocol from Carlson et al. (2008) and Mackie et al. (2008). However, very little research has been conducted on seasonal mussel assemblage movements in Central Texas. As such, it is unclear if any mussel species burrow deeper during winter than during other seasons. It is also unclear if stream-dwelling species are physically capable of burrowing deeper, considering the generally rocky nature of Central Texas river substrate. The lack of clarity regarding seasonal vertical migration specifically in Central Texas makes it difficult to determine mussel availability to human gatherers throughout the year. 82 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

Special Samples The data recovery excavations at 41DN612 recovered 176 special samples from appropriate contexts across the site. These included materials for radiometric dating, matrix samples for flotation, flora, and wood identification samples, and geoarcheological samples, which included bulk matrix (geomatrix) and MS samples. SWCA recovered 32 charcoal samples from various contexts throughout the site, with the majority procured directly from feature contexts. Of these, 10 samples were submitted for radiocarbon dating, including eight from feature contexts. These results are provided in Appendix 7. Three additional sample were initially submitted for radiocarbon dating but ended up being too small to be processed. Of note, an additional 10 faunal bone samples were also submitted for radiocarbon dating. A total of 20 feature matrix samples were collected, 18 of which were subjected to flotation and two were fine- screened. The heavy fraction of the flotation samples was sorted for bone, mussel shell, burned clay, and burned rock. The light fraction of the flotation samples, as well as selected flora and wood identification samples recovered from the excavations, were subjected to macrobotanical analysis. The results of the macrobotanical analysis from each of the features, including the flora and wood identification samples, are discussed in the feature section below and are also presented in Appendix 8. In addition, four bulk matrix samples from non-feature contexts were collected and subjected to fine-screening in order to look for lithic materials; unfortunately, no lithic materials were identified. SWCA collected 10 geomatrix samples for particle size analysis and 100 geoarcheological samples for MS analysis. The geomatrix samples were collected from Unit 1 and BHT N to sample each of the identified strata. The geoarcheological samples were collected from two vertical sample columns (south wall of Unit 1 and north wall of Unit 2), on opposite ends of the excavation block. Features Seven cultural features (i.e., Feature A and Features 1–6) were documented across the site over the course of the data recovery investigation; all features were documented within the Transitional Archaic component and yielded dates that are consistent with the prevailing chronological placement of the Transitional Archaic. It should be noted that Feature 4 was only photographed and documented during the investigation, due to the lack of coherency of the feature and the likelihood of it being an extension of the BRM (i.e., Feature A) thoroughly documented and sampled during excavation of Units 1–3. Feature A (Burned Rock Midden) Feature A consisted of a large, circular, BRM with an associated diffuse mussel shell, bone, and charcoal scatter within the Upper Analytical Unit (Table 7.3). Most of the feature was located within Stratum III; however, as mentioned previously, Feature A was also observed to extend into the underlying Stratum IV due to excavation and prehistoric reuse of the thermal feature (see Figure 6.7). Further, the results of several of the radiocarbon assays were vertically incongruous (i.e., younger dates below older dates) implying multiple uses of the BRM. 83 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 98

Feature A contained numerous layers of burned rock primarily within Stratum III, between 25–80 cmbd (153.25–152.7 m). Within Units 1–3, a total of 6,068 burned rocks were mapped, sorted, and tabulated (see Table 6.3; Appendix 2). Burned rock material types included thermally altered limestone (n=5,917), silicified limestone (n=147), sandstone (n=2), and burned quartzite (n=2). Most of the burned rock (n=5,250; 86.5 percent) were less than 5 cm in diameter (Figure 7.14; see Table 7.3). The next dominant size class was 5–10 cm in diameter (n=661; 10.9 percent) followed by 10–15 cm diameter (n=140; 2.3 percent), and over 15 cm in diameter (n=17; 0.3 percent). The burned rocks were angular to subangular in shape and the majority were highly fragmented, though some fractured in situ and unfractured rocks were observed. Feature A ranged in thickness within the excavation units from 33 cm in Unit 1 to 54 cm thick in Unit 2. A total of 13 flotation samples (S-1 to S-9, S-25, S-27, S-34, and S-35) and 11 charcoal samples (S-10, S-11, S-19, S-26, S-28 through S-33, and S-166) were collected from within Feature A (see Table 6.3). The macrobotanical analysis of the floated light fraction and collected charcoal samples from the feature matrix identified numerous species of charred wood. In order of predominance, the primary wood species include indeterminable hardwood, white oak group (Quercus sect. Quercus), mulberry (Morus spp.), unspecifiable oak (Quercus spp.), slippery elm (Ulmus rubra), hickory/pecan (Carya spp.), elm, and diffuse-porous hardwood (Appendix 8). Less common species (less than two NISP) include plum/cherry (Prunus spp.), ring-porous hardwood, ash (Fraxinus spp.), juniper/redcedar (Juniperus spp.), American elm (Ulmus americana), viburnum (Viburnum spp.), and legume (Fabaceae) (Appendix 8). One carbonized camas bulb scale and one wild onion bulb scale were also identified along with 13 indeterminable bulb scales. The floated light fraction also included 12 additional starchy fragments that were unidentifiable, one bark fragment, and one indeterminable botanical. Artifacts recovered from Feature A include 21 burned clay nodules, 22 pieces of bone, and 39 mussel shell. Identified species within the bone assemblage included deer, indeterminable rodent, turtle/tortoise, and indeterminate fragments, some of which might be avian and snake (Appendix 5). The identified mussel shell included 13 threeridge, two lilliput, and 24 indeterminate mussel shell (Appendix 6). Five samples (one bone and four charcoal) from Feature A were submitted for AMS radiocarbon dating (see Table 7.3). Two dates can be associated with the interior features (Elements 1 and 4) and are discussed below. The remaining three samples were collected from the BRM matrix. Slippery elm from a charcoal sample (S-32; Lot No. 212-1) collected from Unit 3 Level 1 at 153.15 m produced a calibrated age range of 1450– 1510 cal B.P. Turtle or tortoise bone (B-7; Lot No. 183-1) collected from Unit 2 Level 4 at 152.82 m produced a calibrated age range of 1470–1530 cal B.P. A wood charcoal sample belonging to the white oak group (S-28; Lot No. 190-1) from Unit 2 Level 5 at 152.78 m produced a calibrated age range of 1350–1410 cal B.P. Chronologically, this places Feature A near the end of the Transitional Archaic period (see Table 7.3). 86 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 99

Figure 7.14. Top of Feature A exposed in Unit 1 Level 1 depicting primarily <5 cm diameter burned rock, view facing north. Within the larger BRM, four internal features were identified (Elements 1–4) and are interpreted to be remnants of individual primary heating loci within the larger earth oven facility (Figures 7.15 and 7.16). The first internal feature (Element 1) was initially exposed in Level 1 of Units 1–3 (and also encountered in Unit 4) and consisted of a dense cluster of FCR measuring approximately 65 cm north/south by 100 cm east/west. The base of the larger (primarily 8–15 cm in diameter) feature rocks was at 35 to 40 cmbd (153.15–153.1 m). The larger tabular rocks slanted down slightly towards the center of the burned rock concentration and charcoal flecking was observed between and around the periphery of the feature. A wild onion bulb scale (S-1; Lot No. 134-1) was initially submitted for radiocarbon dating, but the sample was too small after pretreatment to be analyzed. A carbonized camas bulb (S-166; Lot No. 137-1) from the feature matrix was then submitted and produced a calibrated age range of 1260–1320 cal B.P. Element 2 was initially exposed in the profiles of Unit 2 (east wall) and Unit 3 (north wall) and consisted of a basin-shaped alignment of FCR primarily measuring 10–15+ cm in diameter, some of which were fractured in situ (see Figure 7.15). The base of the feature rocks was at approximately 45–50 cmbd (153.05–153.0 m) and charcoal flecking was observed between and below the feature rocks. Burned rock associated with this element was also observed in Unit 4; however, no charcoal samples were collected during excavation of this feature-focused unit. 87 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 102

Summary Feature A consists of a BRM located within Strata III and IV within the Upper Analytical Unit. Four possible individual primary heating loci were identified within the BRM during the hand excavations suggesting that Feature A was a large earth oven cooking facility that prehistoric inhabitants used and reused during the Transitional Archaic (Black and Thoms 2014). Specifically, the chronometric data (i.e., Lot Nos. 194-1 and 137- 1) for Feature A temporally overlap and demonstrate use between 1340–1260 cal BP. Subsequent disturbance from a historic refuse dump likely destroyed the most southern extent of the feature. Feature 1 Feature 1 was an isolated, oval shaped, burned rock concentration identified within Stratum IV in the Upper Analytical Unit (Table 7.4).The feature was initially encountered in the central-eastern portion of Unit 1 between 152.66–152.59 m. Feature 1 measured 12 cm north/south by 20 cm east/west in size once fully exposed. Two layers of approximately 7-cm-thick rock were visible in the profile of Feature 1. This feature was confined to Unit 1 between Levels 6 and 7 and was approximately 25 cm below the Feature A deposits in Unit 1, which terminated at 152.92 (Figure 7.17). The feature consisted of thermally altered limestone (n=17) of various shapes and sizes (see Table 7.4). Most of the altered limestone (n=10) was 0–5 cm in diameter and the rest (n=7) measured 5–10 cm in diameter. A majority of the thermally altered rock was sub-rounded, while the larger pieces were tabular in shape. The burned rock within Feature 1 was stacked to create two layers of intact pieces overlapping with little to no soil matrix between the rocks. Six of the limestone rocks were clustered in the northwestern portion of the feature. Charcoal sample (S-18) was taken from this portion of the feature (Figure 7.18). The remaining 11 burned rocks were encountered overlapping with some of the fractured in situ rock in the southeastern portion of the feature. The surrounding matrix of Feature 1 between 84–91 cmbd (152.66–152.59 m elevation) contained evidence of burning (e.g., charcoal). Once the feature was exposed, SWCA collected a 1-gallon-size bag of matrix for flotation and macrobotanical analysis. The sample was collected immediately below Feature 1, from 90–96 cmbd and the remaining matrix was screened in the field. Additionally, one charcoal sample (S-18) was collected from the feature. The macrobotanical analysis of the floated light fraction and collected charcoal sample from the feature matrix identified unspecifiable oak and diffuse-porous hardwood. The charred hardwood (S-18; Lot No. 152-2) returned a calibrated age range of 1348–1276 cal B.P. (see Table 7.4). Chronologically, this places Feature 1 within the later part of the Transitional Archaic in the Upper Analytical Unit. Artifacts recovered from Feature 1 included five pieces of bone and one tooth enamel fragment. One bone specimen was identified as deer and the rest of the assemblage consisted of unidentifiable fragments (Appendix 5). Summary Feature 1 is an isolated, burned rock cluster located in Stratum IV, within the Upper Analytical Unit. No pit or basin was observed but the presence of thermally altered rocks fractured in situ and charcoal suggests Feature 1 represents a single- or limited-use hot rock cooking event during the late Transitional Archaic. 90 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 104

Feature 1 Figure 7.18. Feature 1, showing overlapping cluster of burned rock, plan view. Feature 2 Feature 2 consisted of a discrete burned rock cluster within Stratum IV in the Upper Analytical Unit identified at a similar elevation to Feature 1 (Table 7.5). Burned rock associated with the feature was initially encountered in the north wall of BHT A, between 152.7–152.5 m. Feature 2 was only partially exposed by BHT A; therefore, complete dimensions of the feature are unknown. Two burned rock layers approximately 23 cm thick were observed in the BHT profile. The width of the feature diminished with depth; the top of the feature was 36 cm, the center was 33 cm, and the base was 18 cm wide. The feature was confined to geomorphic Stratum IV and was approximately 35 cm below the historic refuse concentration. Of note, Feature 2 contained large cobbles, as opposed to prevalent scattered burned rock (<5 cm) within the same soil strata. The feature consisted of thermally altered limestone (n=13) with various shapes and sizes. The altered limestone appeared angular in shape and was fractured in situ. Most of the burned rock (n=7) measured between 5–10 cm in diameter, with five burned rock <5 cm and one cobble larger than 15 cm (see Table 7.5). The exposed portions of Feature 2 suggest the feature was composed of two layers of burned rock, with the larger cobbles occupying the upper layer (Figure 7.19). The burned cobbles of Feature 2 are located within Stratum IV between 152.7–152.5 m, suggesting the feature was constructed on the same occupational surface as Feature 1. The internal matrix from Feature 2 contained small flecks of charcoal, which indicated burning. In addition, evidence of in situ thermal alteration was observed and consisted of burned soil surrounding the feature rock, as well as thermal fractures and flecks of charcoal below the burned rock. 92 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 106

Once the feature was uncovered, the matrix was collected from between the burned rocks for flotation and macrobotanical analysis. Additionally, two charcoal samples (S-38 and S-39) were collected from beneath the burned rock cluster. The macrobotanical analysis of the floated light fraction from the feature matrix and collected charcoal samples identified white oak group, viburnum, indeterminable hardwood, and a carbonized walnut hull (Juglandaceae) (Appendix 8). A sample of the white oak group (S-38; Lot No. 243-1) was initially submitted for radiocarbon dating, but the sample was too small after pretreatment to be analyzed. The carbonized nut hull (S-40; Lot No. 245-5) recovered from the floated light fraction was then submitted and produced a calibrated age range of 1696–1544 cal B.P. Chronologically, this places Feature 2 within the latter part of the Transitional Archaic at the base of the Upper Analytical Unit. No other artifacts were recovered in association with the feature. Summary Feature 2 consists of a stacked burned rock cluster located in Stratum IV within the Upper Analytical Unit. No pit or basin was observed but the presence of thermally altered sediment and rocks fractured in situ suggests Feature 2 represents a single- or limited-use hot rock cooking event during the late Transitional Archaic. Feature 3 Feature 3 consisted of a stacked burned rock cluster within Stratum IV in the Upper Analytical Unit identified at a similar elevation (approximately 10 cm below) to Features 1 and 2 (Table 7.6). Burned rock was initially encountered in the east wall of BHT I, between 152.56–152.51 m. Overlapping and stacking of burned rock approximately 5 cm thick were observed in the BHT profile (Figure 7.20). Feature 3 was only partially exposed by BHT I; therefore, complete dimensions of the feature are unknown. The width of the feature increased toward the center; the top of the feature was 22 cm wide, the center was 26 cm wide, and the base was 24 cm wide. The feature contained a mixture of limestone and a granular based rock (i.e., quartzite). The source of the quartz raw material is currently unknown. However, quartzite gravels and cobbles are recorded as a component of Holocene and Pleistocene aged fluviatile terrace deposits on several geologic atlas maps in the region (Barnes 1972, 1987; McGowen et al. 1991). Three of these geologic regions (i.e., Abilene, Sherman, and Wichita Falls-Lawton sheets) are either upstream or overlap the project area. Notably, Denton Creek is recorded as having these fluviatile terrace deposits that are mapped within 1.5 miles southwest of 41DN612 (McGowen et al. 1991). The large granular rock at 41DN612 that appears to be quartzite is most likely to have originated from the fluviatile terrace deposits along Denton Creek. The feature consisted of thermally altered limestone (n=11) and quartz (n=7) of various shapes and sizes. Most of the burned rock (n=11) was 0–5 cm in diameter. Four altered quartz measured between 5–10 cm in diameter and the remaining altered rock (n=3) measured 10–15 cm in diameter. The thermally altered rock was angular in shape, fractured in situ, and primarily clast-supported (i.e., overlapping with little to no soil matrix between the rocks). 94 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 108

Once the feature was uncovered, SWCA collected the feature matrix from between the burned rocks for flotation and macrobotanical analysis. A granular rock sample was also collected for identification purposes. The internal matrix from Feature 3 resembled the surrounding matrix and did not contain any evidence indicative of burning (e.g., reddening, charcoal flecking). However, thermal alteration was observed, as many of the altered rocks exhibited in situ thermal fracturing. No other cultural materials were observed and Feature 3 was not fully excavated. The macrobotanical analysis of the floated light fraction from the feature matrix identified a single carbonized indeterminable botanical weighing less than 0.01 grams. Due to the small size of the specimen, it was not submitted for radiocarbon dating. Summary Feature 3 is a discrete burned rock cluster located in Strata IV within the Upper Analytical Unit. The feature is below the cultural deposits associated with the BRM (Feature A) and may be on the same occupational surface as Features 1 and 2; however no radiocarbon dates were obtained for Feature 3. SWCA did not fully excavate the feature; however, no evidence of other features or cultural materials were encountered immediately adjacent to the feature. No pit or basin was observed but the presence of thermally altered rocks fractured in situ suggests Feature 3 represents a single- or limited-use hot rock cooking event during the early Transitional Archaic. Feature 4 Feature 4 is an irregularly shaped burned rock cluster and possible extension of Feature A within the Upper Analytical Unit (Table 7.7; Figure 7.21). Feature 4 was initially encountered in the floor of BHT K within Stratum III, between 153.04–152.99 m. The feature measured approximately 80 cm north/south by 45 cm east/west and consisted of a thin layer of burned rock approximately 5 cm thick. Once the feature was exposed, SWCA photographed and documented the burned rock layer. The burned rock within Feature 4 was mostly small, intact pieces with little to no overlap. The feature is within similar elevations to the burned rock associated with Feature A, exposed in Units 1 and 2; however, no mussel shell or other faunal remains were found within the feature matrix. The feature matrix was consistent with the surrounding matrix and lacked evidence indicative of burning. No cultural materials, matrix, or burned rock samples were collected and a burned rock sort was not conducted. Summary Feature 4 is an irregularly shaped burned rock layer located in Stratum III, within the Upper Analytical Unit. No pit or basin was observed. The lack of thermally altered sediment or rocks fractured in situ suggests Feature 4 represents a thin layer of discarded burned rock (i.e., clean out activity) associated with Feature A, which dates to the late Transitional Archaic. 96 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

Feature 5 Feature 5 consisted of a coherent, circular burned rock cluster with a central concentration of thin, flat burned rock slabs within Stratum IV in the Upper Analytical Unit (Table 7.8). The feature burned rock was initially encountered within BHT N between 152.85–152.7 m. To further capture and delineate the feature, SWCA excavated BHT O to the east down to the feature elevation. Once fully exposed in BHTs N and O, Feature 5 measured 100 cm north/south by 75 cm east/west in size (Figure 7.22). Three layers of burned rock approximately 15 cm thick were observed in the profile; the burned rock were adjoining but not stacked. The feature contained a mixture of limestone and a granular based rock (Quartzite). The sample was collected immediately below the central rock cluster of Feature 5, from 90–96 cmbd. The remaining matrix was screened in the field; however, no cultural materials were recovered. The internal matrix of Feature 1 between 84–91 cmbd (152.85– 152.7 m) resembled the surrounding soils and did not contain any evidence indicative of burning. However, evidence of in situ thermal alteration (e.g., reddening, thermal fractures) was observed. The macrobotanical analysis of the floated light fraction from the feature matrix identified indeterminable carbonized bulb scales, white oak group, American beautyberry (Callicarpa americana), and indeterminable hardwood (Appendix 8). The charred American beautyberry (S-154; Lot No. 253-3) returned a calibrated age range of 1522–1351 cal B.P. (see Table 7.8). Chronologically, this places Feature 5 within the later part of the Transitional Archaic in the Upper Analytical Unit. No other cultural materials were encountered within or surrounding the feature and no temporally diagnostic artifacts were found in direct association with the feature. Summary Feature 5 is a coherent, circular burned rock cluster located within Stratum IV, within the Upper Analytical Unit. No pit, basin, or presence of thermally altered sediment indicative of burning was evident, but rocks fractured in situ suggests Feature 5 represents a single- or limited-use hot rock cooking event during the late Transitional Archaic. 99 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

Feature 6 Feature 6 consisted of an isolated, oval shaped, burned rock concentration within Stratum V in the Lower Analytical Unit (Table 7.9; Figure 7.23). The feature was initially encountered in the floor of BHT P, between 152.08–151.98 m. Feature 6 was the deepest feature observed at site 41DN612. The feature measured approximately 50 cm north/south by 30 cm east/west. A single layer of burned rock approximately 10 cm thick was observed in the BHT profile. The feature consisted of thermally altered limestone (n=34) of various shapes and sizes (see Table 7.9). Most of the altered limestone (n=17) was 0–5 cm in diameter. A portion of the altered limestone (n=15) was 5– 10 cm in diameter. The remaining altered limestone (n=2) measured 10–15 cm in diameter. A majority of the thermally altered rock were angular in shape and mostly fractured in situ. The rocks were primarily adjoining and supported. Once the feature was exposed, SWCA collected a 1-gallon-size bag of matrix for flotation and macrobotanical analysis. The sample was collected immediately below Feature 6, from 151.99–151.93 m. The internal matrix of Feature 6 resembled the surrounding soils and did not contain any evidence indicative of burning. ## Page 114

Chapter 8. Review of Research Topics and Findings Research Issue 1: Burned Rock Technology A total of seven burned rock features were identified at 41DN612 during the investigations within the Upper and Lower Analytical Units. A large BRM (i.e., Feature A), two dense burned rock clusters in association with the BRM (i.e., Features 4 and 5), and three isolated burned rock features (i.e., Features 1–3) were encountered in what has been designated as the Upper Analytical Unit within Strata III and IV. The lowermost burned rock feature (i.e., Feature 6) at the site located in Stratum V was identified in the Lower Analytical Unit. The following questions were developed to explore the burned rock technology at the site. 1) Are there variations in feature function at the site over time and/or within each of the analytical units? The interpretation of burned rock features and their functions has long been of interest to address questions about technology, subsistence, and social changes across time (e.g., Black et al. 1997; Black and Thoms 2014; Collins 1972; Derring 1999; Freeman 2007; Kelley and Campbell 1942; Weir 1976). Various methods and analytical data—including feature dimensions and morphology, coarse and fine matrix descriptions, nonmatrix constituents, and results of macrobotanical and faunal analysis—can provide information on the context and functions of these features. This information can then be related to investment of labor and resource availability, which could yield information on economical and organizational aspects of landscape use, planning, and shifting resource bases over time. The burned rock features at 41DN612 fall into two groups consisting of isolated hearth features and a BRM. The small, isolated burned rock features (i.e., Features 1–6) in the Upper and Lower Analytical Units are interpreted as single- or limited-use cooking features, which suggests a lower investment of labor for food processing and intermittent or brief occupations of the site area during this period. Conversely, the accumulation of the large BRM in the Upper Analytical Unit is suggestive of a higher investment of labor and more intensive and/or repetitive occupation of the site during the latter part of the Transitional Archaic (Black and Thoms 2014:205, 222; Dering 1999). As mentioned in Chapter 4, we were hopeful that by using Thoms’ (2009:577) more-comprehensive list of hot-rock cooking methods/functions, the function of each feature could be determined and compared across space and time. Feature A is a large, circular, BRM with an associated diffuse mussel shell, bone, and charcoal scatter within Strata III and IV within the late Transitional Archaic component. The feature was initially recorded as four separate burned rock clusters during survey by Raba Kistner (Neel and Meléndez 2019:12). During SWCA’s investigations, the clusters were observed to be connected and additional burned rock was observed in all directions past the initial boundary of the four clusters. The feature was reclassified as a BRM and designated as Feature A. The approximately 9 m north/south by 11 m east/west feature is representative of Thoms (2009:577) definition of a closed pit baking feature with stone heating element (i.e., earth oven). Earth oven facilities are large, specialized features that are often reused over long periods of time. The remnants are often seen as an irregular pit surrounded by discarded burned rock (oven clean out and discard) and previously abandoned hearth centers (Black and Thoms 2014). In archeological contexts, when an area is 102 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 115

reused over time a large homogenous deposit may form (i.e., BRM) due to the continued reuse, disturbance, and mixing that occurs during the construction of new hearths (Kelly and Campbell 1942:320; Weir 1976). Excavations of Units 1–4 positioned at the center of Feature A exposed various base depths revealing an irregular pit with depths ranged from 50–80 cmbd (153.0–152.7 m) (see Figure 6.7). Within the larger BRM (Feature A), four internal features were identified (Elements 1–4) and are interpreted to be remnants of individual primary heating loci within a larger earth oven facility. All four units exhibited an increase in larger tabular rocks (Element 1) from 35 to 40 cmbd (153.15–153.1 m) focused near the center of the excavated block (Figure 8.1). Another cluster of larger rocks (Element 2) fractured in situ was encountered near the center of the block from 45 to 50 cmbd (153.05–153.0 m). Two additional smaller clusters of large rocks fractured in situ were encountered in Unit 2 at 60 cmbd (Element 3 at 152.9 m) and Unit 2 at 75 cmbd (Element 4 at 152.75 m) (see Figure 7.15). These clusters could indicate a series of primary heating elements (i.e., central oven bed with large, often tabular, fractured in place rocks in a circular pattern) that have been deflated through reuse and/or borrowing of materials for later iterations of the earth oven facility (Black and Thoms 2014:213–216; Kelly and Campbell 1942:320). Unit 2 Unit 4 Unit 1 Unit 3  Figure 8.1. Overview of possible individual primary heating element (Element 1) from Units 1–4 in Feature A at 35 to 40 cmbs (153.15–153.10 m). Although the rest of the feature was not excavated, the size and diffuse pattern of burned rock noted on the exposed surface is likely representative of discarded FCR patterns (i.e., the irregular discard of small and fractured rocks that are no longer useful during cleanout or reuse) typical of earth oven facilities (Black and 103 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 116

Thoms 2014:218) (see Figure 7.12). Additional individual primary heating elements may also remain in the unexcavated portions of the BRM. Feature 4 is an irregularly shaped burned rock layer located within Stratum III within the late Transitional Archaic component. It was encountered in the floor of BHT K and measured approximately 80 cm north/south by 45 cm east/west and 5 cm thick. No mussel shell or other faunal remains were found within the feature matrix. Interestingly, this feature was encountered at 46–51 cmbd (153.04–152.99 m), which correlates to the middle of the BRM (Feature A) (Figure 8.2). Due to its characteristics (i.e., size, irregular arrangement, thin single layer, small unfragmented rocks, and lack of thermally altered sediment), Feature 4 is likely the discarded remains of FCR from oven cleanout and reuse of the larger Feature A (Black and Thoms 2014:218). Feature 5 is a circular burned rock cluster with a central concentration of thin, flat, burned rock slabs located in Stratum IV within the late Transitional Archaic component. It was exposed within BHT N and BHT O and measured 100 cm north/south and 75 cm east/west. Feature 5 consists of three layers, approximately 15 cm thick, with adjoining but not stacked burned rock. A majority of the rocks were less than 10 cm in size, but the 24 (of 94 total) rocks larger than 10 cm were concentrated in the central portion of the feature with the smaller rocks falling along the periphery. These larger and tabular central rocks may represent the remains of an individual primary heating element because they were fractured in situ (Black and Thoms 2014:213–216). The feature is also 84–91 cmbd (152.85–152.7 m), which places it in spatial association with the lower boundary of Feature A (see Figure 8.2). Due to its characteristics (i.e., size, circular shape, and core of tabular in situ fractured rocks) and association with Feature A, Feature 5 likely represents what Thoms (2009:557) classified as a closed pit earth oven. Its spatial association with Feature A may indicate that Feature 5 represents an earlier single- or limited-use cooking event that was later encompassed and disturbed by events in association with Feature A (Thoms 2009:586). Feature 5 may represent a small earth oven and early iteration of the more-intensive activities seen in association with Feature A (Black and Thoms 2014:205). Features 1 and 2 were identified at 84 to 91 cmbd (152.66–152.59 m), which places them immediately below Feature A (see Figure 8.2). Feature 1 is an isolated, oval shaped, burned rock concentration initially encountered in the central-eastern portion of excavation Unit 1. Feature 1 measured 12 cm north/south by 20 cm east/west and consisted of two layers, approximately 7 cm thick. Feature 2 consists of a discrete burned rock initially encountered in the north wall of BHT A. Feature 2 was only partially exposed by BHT A and was not fully excavated. The profile of the feature consists of two burned rock layers, approximately 23 cm thick. The width of the feature diminished with depth, as the top of the feature is 36 cm, the center to 33 cm, and finally terminating at 18 cm wide at the base. Although the thickness of the feature varied, the feature was confined to geomorphic Stratum IV. Immediately below Feature 1 and 2 at 94–99 cmbd (152.56–152.51 m) another feature (Feature 3) was identified (see Figure 8.2). Feature 3 consisted of a stacked burned rock cluster initially encountered in the east wall of BHT I. The profile of Feature 3 consists of overlapping and stacking burned rock measuring approximately 5 cm thick. The width of the feature increases toward the center, as the top of the feature is 22 cm wide, the center to 26 cm wide, and finally terminating at 24 cm wide at the base. Feature 3 was only partially exposed by BHT I and was not fully excavated. 104 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas
