## Page 118

Features 1, 2, and 3 likely represent limited-use open-air hearths (Thoms 2009:577, 586). These features are small in size (total number of FCR for Feature 1 is 17 and for the exposed portions of Features 2 and 3, FCR is 13 and 18, respectively) with evidence of in situ fractured rocks. Alternatively, these features could represent early limited-use earth ovens. Black and Thoms (2014:204–205) argue that smaller ovens are often overlooked or misidentified due to their ephemeral context and more limited preservation in relation to larger earth oven facilities. Nonetheless, these features do likely correspond to earlier less-intensive hot rock cooking techniques that were later expanded upon, resulting in the more-intensive earth oven facility (Feature A). Feature 6 is the deepest feature identified at the site and the only one not immediately associated with the larger BRM feature (see Figure 8.2). It is an isolated, oval shaped, burned rock concentration initially encountered in the floor of BHT P, between elevations 152.08–51.98 m (142–152 cmbd). The feature measured approximately 50 cm north/south by 30 cm east/west. The profile of Feature 6 consisted of a single layer of burned rock, approximately 10 cm thick. Due to its characteristics (size, circular shape, and in situ fractured rocks with thermally altered sediment below), Feature 6 likely represents a single- or limited-use open-air hearth (Thoms 2009:577). Features 1, 2, and 3 (Upper Analytical Unit) and Feature 6 (Lower Analytical Unit) are all small single- or limited- use hearths. All four features consist of two to three layers of burned rock that could represent multiple uses. The small size of the features and incomplete excavation of Features 2 and 3 make it difficult to determine how many cooking events occurred within these features. Feature 6 lies well below the remaining features and is expected to represent one of the earliest ephemeral occupations of the site. Features 1, 2, and 3 lie stratigraphically below Feature A and near the interface of the Upper and Lower Analytical Units. These features may indicate the beginning of consistent occupation of the site. In the Upper Analytical Unit we see a shift to larger, more-labor intensive, and reused earth ovens (Features 5 and A) with Feature 4 representing an FCR discard loci from oven clean out and reuse. The seven features identified across site 41DN612 conform with hot rock cooking models in which initial stone cooking techniques are represented by few rocks in small, single- or limited-use open-air hearths or griddles. The more labor-intensive earth ovens typically follow or post-date the regular use of open-air hearths (Thoms 2009:586–587; Fields 2004: 362–363). Theories on the transition from limited and low-intensity stone cooking features to the increased use of higher-intensity features include climate change, increased population density, decreased mobility, and increased occupation and reuse of sites. These pressures likely resulted in changes in subsistence strategies with a focus on land use intensification (Fields 2004:364; Freeman 2007; Thoms 2008, 2009:588; Wandsnider 1997). In addition to the methods of cooking, resources used for construction (e.g., rock, wood fuel) of the feature as well as the resource(s) processed or around each feature were reviewed and compared to the other features at the site. For the construction or processing, there were no evident differences used between the features or analytical units. A majority of the stone used in the construction of the features was limestone with local quartzite occurring only in very minimal numbers in Feature A (n=2), Feature 3 (n=7), and Feature 5 (n=5). Interestingly, the quartzite found in Feature 5 did encompass the largest size clast and were utilized for the primary heating element, but this was not the case in the other features with quartzite. 106 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 119

The fuel types utilized in these features consisted of various hardwoods suggesting opportunistic collection. Specifically, Feature A contained white oak, slippery elm, and hickory; Feature 1 contained an indeterminate hardwood; Feature 2 contained walnut; Feature 5 contained white oak, American beautyberry, and indeterminate hardwood; and Feature 6 contained American beautyberry. Similarly, the procured food resources were highly variable across all of the features. Feature A contained a wild onion, a carbonized camas bulb, mussel shell, deer, indeterminate rodent, turtle/tortoise, and several indeterminate fragments that might be avian and snake. Feature 1 contained fragments of deer; Feature 2 contained a carbonized walnut hull; Feature 3 contained indeterminate botanical fragments; Feature 4 had no identifiable flora or fauna materials; Feature 5 contained carbonized bulb scales; and Feature 6 contained deer. 2) How many individual cooking events are estimated for the earth ovens within the site? To estimate the number of cooking events with the earth oven facility, the number, weight, and size distribution of the burned rocks as well as the rate of fragmentation were used to evaluate use and reuse of the features. Previous data from both archeological and experimental earth ovens have provided an estimated general range of between 100 to 250 kg of burned rock per cooking event (Black and McGraw 1985; Black et al. 1997; Campbell 2012; Dering 1999; Leach et al. 1998). In addition, larger rocks are more likely part of a “primary structural element” of an earth oven, whereas smaller rocks are more indicative of reuse and/or discard (Black et al. 1997:83). Feature A has been recognized as an earth oven facility with multiple cooking events represented. One of the earliest cooking events in association with the facility is Feature 5. This feature consists of a total of 94 burned rocks ranging in size from less than 5 cm to more than 15 cm in size and weighing a total of 19.0 kg. Based on previous research and the characteristics of the feature (i.e., size, predominance of small clast rocks, and occurrence of distinct individual primary heating loci) it is assumed that at least one cooking event occurred at this feature (Black and McGraw 1985; Black et al. 1997; Leach et al. 1998). Three layers of adjoining but not stacked rocks were observed. These layers may represent reuse of the feature and associated rocks, but subsequent disturbance from reuse of the facility has distorted these events. A total of four units were excavated from Feature A. Unit 4 was excavated as a feature-focused unit and information was not recording as to the number and weight of materials per 10-cm level. The remaining three units were fully documented in 10-cm levels, including documenting burned rock characteristics per level. Using these metrics and suggested calculations from Black and McGraw (1985:299), estimations were made as to the total area, density, and volume of burned rock from Feature A. The total area is estimated to be 77.8 m2, the volume is 31.37 m3, and density is 207.35 kg per m3. Based on these calculations, the entire BRM area is estimated to consist of 6,504 kg of rock and anywhere between 86 (reuse of rocks twice) and 173 (reuse of rocks four times) cooking events may have occurred within the BRM (Black and McGraw 1985:299; Black et al. 1997; Dering 1999). In attempting to estimate the number of firing or cooking events of a feature, one of the major factors involves determining the rate of burned rock fracture to approximate reuse and discard (Black et al. 1997; Black and Lucas 1998; Campbell 2012; Leach et al. 1998). Experimental research done by Leach et al. (1998) to estimate rates of burned rock reuse and discard determined that, generally, rocks could only be utilized once 107 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 120

or twice before becoming too thermally fractured for use. Taking this into account, a conservative estimation of between 43 and 86 cooking events is likely more representative of the BRM at 41DN612. The number of burned rocks were plotted by size for Features 1, 2, 3, 5, 6, and Elements 3 and 4 for Feature A (Figure 8.3–8.7). Feature 4 was omitted because detailed rock sorting and counting was not completed for that feature. In addition, Elements 1 and 2 were omitted because no rock sort data was obtained from the portions of the elements that extended into Unit 4. The hope was to establish the frequency distribution of small (<5 cm) to large rocks (>15 cm) as an indicator of reuse for each feature. If a feature is composed of predominantly smaller rocks, then it is more likely the feature has been reused. In contrast, if a feature consisted of mostly large rock, then the feature is less likely to have been reused. It should be noted that the hearth features have a relatively low sample size due to the small size of the feature and the fact that they were not fully excavated (e.g., Features 2 and 3). Despite this, all six populations were generally positively skewed, meaning the five features and two elements support the hypothesis that reuse was occurring. Features 1, 2, and 3 are predominately composed of rocks smaller than 10 cm in size. The exclusive occurrence of rock sizes less than 10 cm in Feature 1 suggests that reuse was occurring; this in conjunction with the small size of the feature fits with the model that Feature 1 represents a limited-use hearth (see Figure 8.3). Based on the amount of moderately sized rocks (5–15 cm) in Features 2 and 3, there was likely only some reuse occurring, which fits with the model of these features representing limited-use hearths (see Figure 8.4). Although the rock sizes within Features 1, 2, and 3 suggest reuse, the minimal sample size (n=23, n=13, n=28, respectively) does not provide a robust enough sample to assume the distribution model is indeed representative of the population. However, these features were included to extract as much information as possible about the site and its occupational history. This distribution model is seen with the population from Features 5 and 6 as well (see Figure 8.5). 15 10 5 0 <5 cm 5-10 cm 10-15 cm >15 cm Figure 8.3. ## Page 123

Although the distribution of Feature 5 is still positively skewed, the population is predominantly composed of rocks between 5 and 10 cm in size (64.9 percent) (see Figure 8.7). This suggests some reuse, but still only limited activity. The distribution of Feature 5 may not be wholly represented as it is situated within the BRM and anthropomorphic disturbances may have altered the population. The distribution of Feature 6 also suggests limited reuse because only two of the total 34 rocks (5.9 percent) are larger than 10 cm in size (see Figure 8.7). The two elements analyzed from Feature A are strongly right skewed, with 84 percent of Element 3 and 71 percent of element 4 consisting of rocks smaller than 5 cm in size (see Figures 8.6 and 8.7). The long- tailed size-frequency distribution seen in both elements strongly suggests intensive reuse. Both elements have a much larger sample size and were completely excavated, in contrast with Features 2 and 3, but the dramatically skewed distribution likely suggests that reuse of individual features was more intensive within the BRM. This supports the model proposed of increased intensification through time at 41DN612. 3) What is the relationship of site formation processes and the patterning of the cultural features observed within the site? The geoarcheological analysis results and feature distribution throughout the site were used to determine what processes have affected the cultural manifestations observed at the site. Are the smaller, isolated features a result of more sedimentation covering the site during that time period indicative of specific feature function and/or specific resource processing, a result of cultural/natural disturbances, or a combination of one or more of these factors? Did the large BRM feature accumulate due to a period of surface stability, allowing reuse of the site features creating a palimpsest, or is this a result of intensification of resource processing and the need for larger cooking features (i.e., increased labor investment)? Site 41DN612 is on the left bank (east side) of a slight interior meander bend that is laterally growing from continual flood deposits. As mentioned previously, Denton Creek at this crossing has an SR value of 1.4, suggesting a dynamic fluvial history. This is partially evident from the oxbow channel situated approximately 35 m southeast of the site. To help in examining the site formation processes, 15 radiocarbon samples from good elevational and stratigraphic context were considered (Appendix 7). The radiocarbon data indicate that at least two periods of prehistoric occupation occurred at the site during the Transitional Archaic. Specifically, the Upper Analytical Unit within Stratum III, the interface of Strata III and IV, and the upper portion of Stratum IV dates between 1540 and 1200 cal yr BP at the end of the Transitional Archaic. The Lower Analytical Unit roughly falls between 2080 and 1820 cal yr BP and the beginning of the Transitional Archaic. As previously discussed, the rate of alluvial deposition at 41DN612 was approximated comparing select radiocarbon assays and their stratigraphic elevation. These data suggest an average rate of deposition across both analytical unit occupations appears to be 7.5 cm per century (see Chapter 6). However, when comparing the temporal gap of 320 years between the two analytical units, the estimated rate of deposition is 14.5 cm per century, doubling the average rate of deposition for the site. As previously noted, this period of increased sedimentation appears to temporally coincide when Denton Creek avulsed and established a new channel upstream from 41DN612 around 1740 B.P. (Abbott n.d.:7). The period of dynamic change along Denton Creek drainage roughly correlates stratigraphically between the lowest feature (Feature 6) dated to between 2159 and 2001 cal yr BP and the lowermost portions of the Upper 111 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 124

Analytical Unit that includes Features 1, 2 and 3. Specifically, Feature 2 returned an estimated date of between 1696 and 1544 cal yr BP and Feature 1 was dated roughly between 1348 and 1276 cal yr BP (Feature 3 was not able to be dated). Notably, these features are stratigraphically below the BRM (Feature A). Once the new channel of Denton Creek was established, a period of stability was seen as marked by the formation of the West Fork paleosol, which correlates with Stratum III and the BRM (Feature A). As a result, it appears that although the BRM did accumulate during a period of stability allowing reuse of the site features creating a palimpsest, the occurrence of single-use features (i.e., Features 1, 2, and 3) immediately below the BRM and their accumulation after the shift in Denton Creek’s channel does suggest an increase in land use intensification (i.e., single- or limited-use hearths being replaced by earth oven hot rock cooking technology). Sources for the lithic raw materials, particularly quartzite, composing the burned rock features at 41DN612 is difficult to identify. As noted above, several regional geologic maps record the presence of gravel and cobble- sized quartzite as a component of the fluviatile terrace deposits. Within the Sherman sheet that encompasses the project area, fluviatile terrace deposits are mapped along Denton, Hickory, and Clear Creeks (McGowen et al. 1991). Each of these mapped deposits are within 1.5 miles, 9 miles, and 17 miles, respectively. Admittedly, the presence of quartzite at these mapped locations is unknown, but the quartzite at the site most likely came from one of these drainages, with Denton Creek being the most reasonable interpretation. Limestone, the primary component of the burned rock features, is more prevalent in the area. Limestone from the Fort Worth Limestone and Duck Creek Formation are mapped within 0.25 mile of 41DN612 in the uplands east of the site (McGowen et al. 1991). It is interpreted that limestone clast materials were readily available both in the Denton Creek channel base and in the surrounding uplands for use in the burned rock features at 41DN612. As to initial size of the procured FCR raw materials at 41DN612, only an approximate size range can be made due to the extensive reuse of the FCR observed in the features. Accounting for this limitation, the clast materials identified as “primary structural elements” are the best gauge for initial procured rock size. Although a few clast materials were identified larger than 15 cm, the most consistent ‘large’ rock materials were between 10 and 15 cm in size. It is here interpreted that the primary size of initial procured clast materials at 41DN612 tended to be 10–15 cm in size and the reuse of the thermal features subsequently fragmented and reduced the rock. Research Issue 2: Foraging Strategies Research has shown that earth oven facilities were used to convert otherwise inedible foods (i.e., geophytes and starches) into more digestible starches and sugars (Dering 1999; Thoms 2008; Wandsnider 1997). Dering (1999:671) suggests that the use of large earth ovens, such as the BRM at 41DN612, is indicative of a broad- spectrum economy that may be a result of changes in resource availability of other, higher-ranked resources (e.g., deer). The following questions were developed to explore foraging strategies used by the groups who inhabited site 41DN612. 1) Is the 41DN612 assemblage consistent with expectations of a broad-based foraging strategy? Macrobotanical analyses was conducted primarily on samples from the feature contexts at 41DN612. The results primarily identified wood charcoal, which is interpreted to be fuel for cooking features, and carbonized 112 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 125

bulb scale fragments (geophytes) including camas and wild onion or garlic (Appendix 8; Bush 2020). In addition to the geophytes, indeterminable starchy fragments were also identified that may represent bulbs, tubers, rhizomes, or large, starchy seeds. Previous macrobotanical research by Gayle J. Fritz during the Richland Chambers Reservoir project found that nuts (hickory, pecan, and acorn) as well as prairie turnip were prominent food sources for hunter gatherers during the Late Archaic through Late Prehistoric (McGregor and Bruseth 1987). A relative of the prairie turnip, scurfpea was also commonly found at Late Archaic to Early Caddoan sites around the Lake Cooper area (Fields et al. 1993). The occurrence of these starchy carbohydrates at other sites within the Trinity River Basin, as well as their documented importance historically to the Great Plains region, suggests that the indeterminate starchy fragments from 41DN612 may have been from the Psoralea tuber family (Field et al. 1993; McGregor and Bruseth 1987; Reid 1977; Wedel 1978). Archeological plant remains at site 41DN612 were sparse (0.02 g/liter) and consisted primarily of wood charcoal and bulb scale fragments (Appendix 8) (Bush 2020). The macrobotanical remains analyzed from the light fractions of the flotation samples were all collected from feature contexts; however, several non-feature charcoal samples from below Feature A and the trenching activities were also analyzed. The identified plant remains from the features consisted of wood charcoal, bulb scale fragments, starchy fragments (i.e., bulb, tuber, or rhizome), a small piece of bark, and two unidentified botanical fragments (Table 8.1) (Bush 2020). Four camas bulb fragments (Units 1 and 2, Feature A) were identified as either Atlantic camas (Camassia scilloides) or prairie camas (C. angusta). A wild onion or garlic bulb fragment was also identified (Unit 1, Feature A). The remaining 16 samples were indeterminate regarding species (Appendix 8). Additionally, indeterminable starchy fragments found in seven samples may also represent bulbs, but the possibility of tubers, rhizomes, or even large, starchy seeds cannot be ruled out (see Table 8.1) (Bush 2020:7). Identification was attempted for 293 wood charcoal fragments, but due to the small size of most fragments, only 228 could be identified to genus or species (Table 8.2; Appendix 8). The identified samples were collected from Features A, Features 1–3, and Features 5–6, as well as a few non-feature contexts. Oaks (NISP=66) were the most numerous, composing 22.5 percent of the identified assemblage. Oak specimens that could be identified below the genus level were all white oak group. ## Page 127

with evidence of percussion impacts, four specimens with cut marks, and one specimen with chop marks. These anthropogenic taphonomic signatures are typically associated with butchery. As such, the butchery of at least one deer can be assumed to be represented within the site assemblage. Only one specimen (Lot No. 155-1) with cut marks was found in association with a feature (i.e., Feature 1). A second specimen (Lot No. 228-1) with percussion impacts was found at a similar elevation as Lot No. 155-1, but in Unit 3. Finally, one specimen with cut marks (Lot No. 242-1) was found in close association to the upper elevation of Feature 6 (152.09 m). The remaining specimens with anthropogenic taphonomic signatures were found within the BRM area in the Upper Analytical Unit, but generally below the BRM and not in direct association with any feature (Appendix 5). The vertical distribution of the deer bones exhibiting anthropogenic taphonomy may indicate that more than one deer is represented within the faunal assemblage, and that deer was being processed during both the early and Late Transitional Archaic (in both the Upper and Lower Analytical Units). Finally, other faunal remains encountered during the 41DN612 investigations included 155 mussel shell fragments of which 89 were umbos. Of the 155 mussel shells, 66 (42.6 percent) were identified by genus and species. The remaining 89 (57.4 percent) could not be confidently identified because of their fragmentary nature. Of those that were classified, five species were identified including threeridge, rock pocketbook, threehorn wartyback, Louisiana pigtoe, and lilliput. These encompass a mixture of both common and uncommon species to the Trinity River. The most frequent identified species (n=47; 71.2 percent) is Amblema plicata, known as the threeridge because of its exterior flutes and ridges. These are found throughout drainages in central and east Texas (Howells et al. 1996:34–35). Historically, these typically thick-shelled mussels were used for button production or polished shell ornaments. The thickness and luster of this species may also have been utilized in prehistoric times; however, no shell ornaments were identified within the 41DN612 assemblage. These shells were common throughout the site and present in both the Upper and Lower Analytical Units; therefore, no clear vertical or horizontal concentration could be identified in the distribution of this species. Lilliput mussel shells comprised 16.7 percent and were primarily identified within the Upper Analytical Unit deposits; however, two specimens were identified in the Lower Analytical Unit (Unit 2 Level 7 and BHT N). Louisiana pigtoe comprised 9.1 percent and, similar to the lilliput, was mostly found in the Upper Analytical Unit contexts with only one specimen in the Lower (Unit 1 Level 8). The remaining two species only had one specimen each in the assemblage and both were in the Upper Analytical Unit deposits. The identified species are typical of flowing water environments with a stable substrate (i.e., creeks or rivers). The mussel shells were collected from across the site, and 123 were found in general association with the BRM feature. Overall, analysis of the flora and faunal remains at site 41DN612 is characteristic of a broad diet breadth exploiting locally available resources such as deer, small game, turtle, birds, and freshwater mussel shell, and an increased use of plant resources. Archeologists have documented a long history of bulb exploitation in Texas, and the use of earth oven facilities to transform otherwise inedible geophytes into nutritionally optimal starches and sugars seems to be part of a continent-wide trend that began in the early Holocene (Thoms 2008; Wandsnider 1997). Although the occurrence of plant remains at 41DN612 is rather low, it is possible that the earlier earth ovens were used to cook meat (i.e., deer) and items other than plant foods. Alternatively, 115 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 128

later cooking events may have differentially destroyed the earlier bulb scale fragments, which are less common and more delicate than wood charcoal (Bush 2020). 2) Can any inferences be made about seasonality of site use? Due to the paucity of identifiable flora and faunal remains, it is difficult to make determinations as to the seasonality of the assemblage. As mentioned, the occurrence of camas, wild onion (or garlic), and other indeterminate bulb scales and starchy fragments do suggest a spring to early summer procurement event (Acuña 2006; Boyd et al. 2004). As the macrobotanical analysis was only able to identify two specimens to species, it is difficult to narrow down the season, but within the Western Cross Timbers Allium typically grow between March and June and camas between March and May (Acuña 2006:28–29). As these plant remains were largely found in association with Feature A in the excavated units, it is likely that at least one or more of the cooking events represented within the central portion of the BRM occurred during the spring between March and May. Only six elements from the faunal assemblage were able to be generally aged (Appendix 5). Of these, four were determined to be adult and two were designated as juvenile. All the elements, except for one juvenile, were also identified as deer. Two of the adult elements were found below Feature A in Units 1 and 3 (Lot Nos. 143-1 and 224-1). The juvenile classified as white-tailed deer (Lot No. 227-1) was collected from below Feature 1. This suggests that at least Feature 1 was utilized in spring or summer as white-tail deer fawns in the Western Cross Timbers are typically born between April and July with a majority of births occurring in June (TPWD 2020b). Finally, very little research has been conducted on seasonal mussel assemblage movements in Central Texas. As such, it is unclear if any mussel species burrow deeper during winter than during other seasons. It is also unclear if stream dwelling species are physically capable of burrowing deeper, considering the generally rocky nature of Central Texas river substrate. The lack of clarity regarding seasonal vertical migration, specifically in Central Texas, makes it difficult to determine mussel availability to human gatherers throughout the year. Overall, the limited flora and faunal remains recovered from the site make determinations of seasonality difficult. Based on the identified macrobotanical remains, we can estimate at least one or two occurrences of plant banking in the spring or early summer. Additionally, the faunal elements that were able to be identified to species and age indicate a late spring to early summer kill event. Although the flora and faunal remains are sparse, site 41DN612 does appear to mirror other Late Archaic sites recorded in the Trinity River basin. Previous investigations have reported evidence of broad-based subsistence strategies and small seasonal camp sites along the tributaries of the Trinity River (Ferring and Yates 1998; McGreger and Bruseth 1987; Tinsley and Dayton 2011). It has been suggested that these ephemeral campsites along the creeks were used seasonally for specific hunting and foraging activities, while more- permanent habitation sites were located on the uplands along the Trinity River proper (Tinsley and Dayton 2011: 181). Interestingly, these Late Archaic campsites typically have a broad-based diet breath but focus on deer and other large game (i.e., bison). Additionally, a late spring to early summer occupational period is commonly suggested due to the identification of young deer and bulbs in many of the assemblages (McGreger and Bruseth 1987; Peter and McGreger 1988; and Tinsley and Dayton 2011). 116 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 129

3) Is there any evidence of intensive resource processing or tool manufacture at the site? As previously mentioned, anthropogenic taphonomy is present on the surface of seven specimens, all seven of which have been identified as white-tailed deer, or as deer-sized mammal elements that could not definitively be attributed to species due to the small size of the fragments and lack of identifying landmarks. The taphonomic signatures identified include two specimens with evidence of percussion impacts, four specimens with cut marks, and one specimen with chop marks. The observed anthropogenic taphonomy can be associated with butchery and was only present on elements attributed to white-tailed deer and deer-sized mammal elements. Thus, the butchery of a single deer can securely be attributed to the site assemblage. None of the faunal bone showed evidence of modification for tool use. The vast majority of the shells was associated with Feature A, and 32 percent of the assemblage, exhibited evidence of cultural modification (i.e., battering, cut edges, burning). It is likely that the remaining shell was also culturally modified; however, these specimens were either too fragmentary or had recent breakage and cultural modification could not be ascertained. None of the mussel shell exhibited intentional modifications for tool use or adornments. Additionally, archeologists have documented a long history of bulb exploitation in Texas, and the use of earth oven facilities to transform otherwise inedible geophytes into nutritionally optimal starches and sugars seems to be part of a continent-wide trend that began in the early Holocene (Thoms 2008; Wandsnider 1997). Although the occurrence of plant remains at 41DN612 is rather low, the incidence of bulb scale fragments and starchy fragments (i.e., bulb, tuber, or rhizome) does indicate that at least some intensive plant processing was being conducted. This intensive plant processing may have been minor in scale, with other foods (i.e., deer and mussel) being more important. The heavy reuse and internal mixing of Feature A also may have destroyed other fragile plant remains. 117 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 130

Chapter 9. Site Summary and Conclusions On behalf of TxDOT, SWCA conducted staged mitigation on the Hardeman Midden site (41DN612), within the APE of the proposed FM 407 Project in Denton County, Texas (CSJ: 1310-01-027). The site is a stratified Transitional Archaic occupation situated on a broad flat floodplain of Denton Creek and Denton Creek Slough, an abandoned channel of Denton Creek. SWCA and its subcontractor conducted the data recovery investigations of 41DN612 from April 14 to June 22, 2019; geomorphological assessments were conducted during the last week of fieldwork. Field investigations consisted of a combination of mechanical and hand excavation of all site deposits within the APE. Excavations terminated at 5 feet as depths of project impacts would not exceed 5 feet. Overall, SWCA excavated 310.1 m3 of sediment from the site. Subsequent to the fieldwork, analyses included radiocarbon dating, flotation sampling, geoarcheological analyses, identification of faunal remains, and assessment of the artifacts and features. As described in Chapter 6, the stratigraphy at 41DN612 appears to correlate with the Pilot Point alloformation (dating to 4,500–1,800 years ago), which is capped by the West Fork paleosol (interpreted to date to 2,300– 1,000 years ago). Within these deposits, five depositional strata were identified. Strata I and II are associated with modern and historic disturbances; Stratum III appears to correlate with the West Fork paleosol; and the underlying Strata IV and V appear to correlate with the Pilot Point alloformation. Based on the results of the various analyses, particularly the chronometric analyses, the prehistoric deposits at 41DN612 appear to be primarily associated with the period commonly recognized as the Transitional Archaic, although there is a slight overlap with the Late Prehistoric I period. Within these deposits, two cultural or archeological analytical units were defined (i.e., Upper and Lower Analytical Units). The Upper Analytical Unit falls at the end of the Transitional Archaic (and very beginning of the Late Prehistoric I period) calibrated between 1540–1200 cal yr B.P.), whereas the Lower Analytical Unit occurs in the early Transitional Archaic between 2080 and 1820 cal yr B.P. These analytical units appear to be separated by a recognized period of change within Denton Creek when the drainage channel changed course around 1,740 years ago (Abbott n.d.:7). The change to the Denton Creek drainage likely explains the notable increase in sedimentation separating the two age groups observed at 41DN612. During these excavations, seven cultural features were exposed, including a large earth oven facility (Feature A) and six isolated burned rock features (Features 1–6), interpreted to be single- or limited-use cooking features. All of the features were within the Upper Analytical Unit except for Feature 6, which was within the Lower Analytical Unit. A total of 519 artifacts were collected during the investigations including 209 historic artifacts and 310 prehistoric artifacts. The historic artifacts were primarily within a large refuse pit that truncated portions of the Feature A, and was limited to the Upper Analytical Unit. The prehistoric assemblage consisted of faunal remains (155 mussel shells and 99 faunal bone) and burned clay (n=21). No lithic tools or debitage were recovered during the field investigations or during flotation or fine-screening of the feature matrix. In addition, 6,429 pieces of burned rock were documented during the investigations and 176 special samples were collected. Overall, previous studies and literature reviews show a paucity of burned rock features within the Trinity River basin (e.g., Black et al. 1997; Lintz et al. 2008; Prikryl 1990; Yates and Ferring 1986). In a report presented by Geo-Marine, a literature review of the Texas Archeological Sites Atlas Database was conducted to compare the occurrence of thermal features reported within the Trinity River and Brazos River basins (Lintz et al. 2008). Lintz et al. (2008) reported that thermal features are rarely reported within the Trinity River basin and twice as 118 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

## Page 131

likely to be reported within the Brazos River basin. This suggests that the occurrence of seven distinct features at 41DN612 is significant and may provide valuable information on the regional history of the Trinity River basin during the Transitional Archaic period. The smaller hearth features identified at 41DN612 are typical of the small burned rock features encountered within the Trinity River basin during the Late Archaic to early Late Prehistoric (Black et al. 1997; Lintz et al. 2008; Prikryl 1990; Tinsley et al. 2011). Interestingly, larger burned rock features and earth ovens are particularly rare, making the occurrence of Feature A rather unique. The excavations were limited to the APE and the exposure afforded only a partial glimpse of the overall site. Based on the assemblage, the site is interpreted as a seasonal (likely spring and summer), ephemeral campsite utilized primarily for hunting/foraging and cooking activities, as indicated by the flora and faunal assemblage and associated site furniture. Small groups exploited the riparian zone resources, making forays into the landscape to hunt and forage. Over time, there was an increase in land use intensification as evidenced by the single- or limited-use hearths being replaced by earth oven hot rock cooking technology to process lower-ranked resources (e.g., geophytes and starches) Regional Comparison To place the activities at site 41DN612 into a regional context, a brief review of archeological sites in the Trinity River Basin was conducted. The main purpose of this literature review was to identify sites with burned rock middens, and if possible, to compare the features and occupations of those sites with site 41DN612. The review focused on three main types of sources: projects completed in the region (Brown and Lebo 1990; Ferring and Yates 1997; Parrish and Burson 2002; Raab and Moir 1981; and Shanabrook et al. 2002), site specific investigations (Brown and Lebo 1991; Ferring and Yates 1998; Lintz et al. 2008; Peter and Harrison 2011; and Shelton 2016), and sources that discuss regional archeological histories (Crook III and Hughston 2016; Fields 2004; Prikryl 1990; Sciscenti 1971; and Todd 2014). A total of 161 Late Archaic to Late Prehistoric sites were identified in the literature review (Table 9.1). Identifying sites with burned rock middens proved to be difficult due to the inconsistency of language used to describe features, the paucity of information reported on some sites, and time constraints of this project. Of the 161 sites, a total of 24 sites were found to include burned rock midden features, and an additional 26 sites were found to potentially include burned rock middens (Figure 9.1). Those sites notated as having the potential to contain burned rock middens were sites in which the language or reporting was limited but seemed to indicate a likelihood of midden or earth oven activity (e.g., cultural midden noted with dense mussel and FCR; no midden noted but 14 burned rock features were reported). During this brief review, several trends became clear. The occurrence of burned rock middens or large earth oven facilities appears to increase as one moves westerly within the Trinity River Basin with sites downstream having less abundant burned rock assemblages (Lintz 2008; Peter and Harrison 2011:273). Secondly, site use appears to mirror residential mobility, as proposed by Binford (1982), with site use intensifying through the Late Archaic and into the Late Prehistoric periods (Ferring and Yates 1997:305; Peter and Harrison 2011:31). Finally, burned rock middens appear to support subsistence practices focused on plant processing and commonly on mussel consumption (Lintz 2008:127; Peter and Harrison 2011:272). 119 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas


---

Table 9.1. Archeological Sites Within the Trinity River Basin County No BRM Confirmed BRM Potential BRM Total No. Reviewed Collin 0 0 6 6 Dallas 21 5 1 27 Denton 31 10 10 51 Freestone 13 1 1 15 Kaufman 0 0 1 1 Limestone 0 2 0 2 Navarro 18 1 0 19 Rockwall 0 0 3 3 Tarrant 28 5 4 37 Totals 111 24 26 161 Comparing the burned rock midden at site 41DN612 with middens of other sites in the region is more difficult and could not be completed within the bounds of this project. A compilation of testing and mitigation level data would need to be synthesized and analyzed for a true comparison. Two sites (i.e., 41TR170 and 41TR198) were earmarked as being similar to site 41DN612 (Lintz 2008; Peter and Harrison 2011). These sites resemble 41DN612 in the size, density, structure, and variability of thermal features. The similarity ends there though, as both sites exhibit a much more diverse array of artifacts, including lithic tools. In fact, during the literature review no other sites were identified consisting solely of a large multi-use burned rock midden with no other cultural materials. Though, it should be noted that many sites had relatively small lithic assemblages. This literature review was not meant to be intensive, but instead was conducted to bolster the discussion on burned rock middens in the Trinity River Basin. Lintz (2008) conducted a similar, and more intensive, review of the distribution of thermal features in the Trinity and Brazos River basins. He points out that a regional synthesis of thermal features is lacking and found similar issues during his review in identifying such sites due to inconsistencies in language and the scarcity of detailed reporting. Overall, the Trinity River basin would benefit from such a study. It would not only strengthen our understanding of the region but allow for comparisons to neighboring regions such as the Brazos River basin and Central Texas. Conclusions The data recovery investigations at the Hardeman Midden site sought to address environmental, technological, and adaptive changes throughout the Transitional Archaic. The overall artifact recovery at the site was low and mostly consisted of flora and faunal remains; however, feature recovery was high. Some disturbances (i.e., historic refuse pit) were noted within the upper deposits of the site; however, this only impacted a portion of the Feature A deposits. The late Transitional Archaic component contained intact, well-preserved archeological deposits containing preserved flora and faunal material and numerous cooking features. The early Transitional Archaic occupation was more difficult to characterize, due to the limited quantities of artifacts and presence of only a single cultural feature. Despite these limitations, the data recovered from the cultural components show diachronic shifts between the technological and foraging strategies from the early to late Transitional Archaic. The early Transitional Archaic appears to be a period of relatively low occupational intensity and the faunal assemblages indicate groups had a broad diet breadth exploiting locally available resources such as deer, 121 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 134

small game, turtle, birds, and freshwater mussel shell. Fracturing and fragmentation of the faunal remains suggests butchering and intensive processing (e.g., marrow extraction) was occurring at the site during this period. The late Transitional Archaic groups were continuing to exploit locally available resources; however, these groups had more substantial occupational debris, including several isolated burned rock features and a large earth oven cooking facility. The earlier groups appear to have had intermittent or brief occupations of the site area based on the presence of limited, single-use cooking features. In contrast, the later groups appear to have had more intensive and/or repetitive occupations. These groups were exploiting locally available floral resources for both fuel and food sources and some intensive processing of plant materials appears to have occurred at the site based on the presence of plant bulb remnants and an earth oven facility. No lithic tools or debitage were recovered from the site and no bone or shell tools were identified in the assemblage. This is very unusual and intriguing, as distinct cut marks were observed on several of the faunal remains (i.e., bone and shell). One explanation, which corelates with Tinsley and Dayton’s (2011:181) hypothesis, is that this site was used solely for specific hunting/foraging activities and food processing, while habitation and other activities (e.g., tool production) was occurring elsewhere as lithic raw materials are rare in the region. Recommendations In concurrence with the previous survey recommendations, the Hardeman Midden site is considered eligible for designation as an SAL and for the NRHP. The early and late Transitional Archaic components revealed intact, well-preserved archeological deposits that significantly contributed to our understanding of Transitional Archaic patterns. These investigations mitigated the project-specific effects, not the entire site. Any future project that could impact deeper deposits (i.e., deeper than 5 feet), or those beyond the current ROW, warrant further consideration to assess the possibilities for additional contributing components. With these considerations, no further work is recommended. 122 NRHP Mitigation at 41DN612, The Hardeman Midden Site, Denton County, Texas

## Page 221

February 03, 2020 Mrs. Mercedes Cody SWCA Environmental Consultants 4407 Monterey Oaks Blvd Austin, TX 78749 United States RE: Radiocarbon Dating Results Dear Mrs. Cody, Enclosed are the radiocarbon dating results for 17 samples recently sent to us. As usual, the method of analysis is listed on the report with the results and calibration data is provided where applicable. The Conventional Radiocarbon Ages have all been corrected for total fractionation effects and where applicable, calibration was performed using 2013 calibration databases (cited on the graph pages). The web directory containing the table of results and PDF download also contains pictures, a cvs spreadsheet download option and a quality assurance report containing expected vs. measured values for 3-5 working standards analyzed simultaneously with your samples. Reported results are accredited to ISO/IEC 17025:2005 Testing Accreditation PJLA #59423 standards and all chemistry was performed here in our laboratory and counted in our own accelerators here. Since Beta is not a teaching laboratory, only graduates trained to strict protocols of the ISO/IEC 17025:2005 Testing Accreditation PJLA #59423 program participated in the analyses. As always Conventional Radiocarbon Ages and sigmas are rounded to the nearest 10 years per the conventions of the 1977 International Radiocarbon Conference. When counting statistics produce sigmas lower than +/- 30 years, a conservative +/- 30 BP is cited for the result. The reported d13C values were measured separately in an IRMS (isotope ratio mass spectrometer). They are NOT the AMS d13C which would include fractionation effects from natural, chemistry and AMS induced sources. When interpreting the results, please consider any communications you may have had with us regarding the samples. Thank you for prepaying the analyses. As always, if you have any questions or would like to discuss the results, don’t hesitate to contact us. Sincerely, Ronald E. Hatfield President Page 1 of 35

## Page 222

REPORT OF RADIOCARBON DATING ANALYSES Mercedes Cody Report Date: February 03, 2020 SWCA Environmental Consultants Material Received: January 17, 2020 Conventional Radiocarbon Age (BP) or Percent Modern Carbon (pMC) & Stable Isotopes Laboratory Number Sample Code Number Calendar Calibrated Results: 95.4 % Probability High Probability Density Range Method (HPD) Beta - 550627 SWCA52105.01_41DN612_S-14 1300 +/- 30 BP IRMS δ13C: -24.5 o/oo (95.4%) 660 - 770 cal AD (1290 - 1180 cal BP) Submitter Material: Charcoal Pretreatment: (charred material) acid/alkali/acid Analyzed Material: Charred material Analysis Service: AMS-Standard delivery Percent Modern Carbon: 85.06 +/- 0.32 pMC Fraction Modern Carbon: 0.8506 +/- 0.0032 D14C: -149.42 +/- 3.18 o/oo ∆14C: -156.59 +/- 3.18 o/oo (1950:2020) Measured Radiocarbon Age: (without d13C correction): 1290 +/- 30 BP Calibration: BetaCal3.21: HPD method: INTCAL13 Results are ISO/IEC-17025:2005 accredited. No sub-contracting or student labor was used in the analyses. All work was done at Beta in 4 in-house NEC accelerator mass spectrometers and 4 Thermo IRMSs. The "Conventional Radiocarbon Age" was calculated using the Libby half -life (5568 years), is corrected for total isotopic fraction and was used for calendar calibration where applicable. The Age is rounded to the nearest 10 years and is reported as radiocarbon years before present (BP), “present" = AD 1950. Results greater than the modern reference are reported as percent modern carbon (pMC). The modern reference standard was 95% the 14C signature of NIST SRM-4990C (oxalic acid). Quoted errors are 1 sigma counting statistics. Calculated sigmas less than 30 BP on the Conventional Radiocarbon Age are conservatively rounded up to 30. d13C values are on the material itself (not the AMS d13C). d13C and d15N values are relative to VPDB-1. References for calendar calibrations are cited at the bottom of calibration graph pages. Page 2 of 35

REPORT OF RADIOCARBON DATING ANALYSES Mercedes Cody Report Date: February 03, 2020 SWCA Environmental Consultants Material Received: January 17, 2020 Conventional Radiocarbon Age (BP) or Percent Modern Carbon (pMC) & Stable Isotopes Laboratory Number Sample Code Number Calendar Calibrated Results: 95.4 % Probability High Probability Density Range Method (HPD) Beta - 550628 SWCA52105.01_41DN612_S-30 1310 +/- 30 BP IRMS δ13C: -25.1 o/oo (68.0%) 656 - 727 cal AD (1294 - 1223 cal BP) (27.4%) 737 - 769 cal AD (1213 - 1181 cal BP) Submitter Material: Charcoal Pretreatment: (charred material) acid/alkali/acid Analyzed Material: Charred material Analysis Service: AMS-Standard delivery Percent Modern Carbon: 84.95 +/- 0.32 pMC Fraction Modern Carbon: 0.8495 +/- 0.0032 D14C: -150.48 +/- 3.17 o/oo ∆14C: -157.64 +/- 3.17 o/oo (1950:2020) Measured Radiocarbon Age: (without d13C correction): 1310 +/- 30 BP Calibration: BetaCal3.21: HPD method: INTCAL13 Results are ISO/IEC-17025:2005 accredited. No sub-contracting or student labor was used in the analyses. All work was done at Beta in 4 in-house NEC accelerator mass spectrometers and 4 Thermo IRMSs. The "Conventional Radiocarbon Age" was calculated using the Libby half -life (5568 years), is corrected for total isotopic fraction and was used for calendar calibration where applicable. The Age is rounded to the nearest 10 years and is reported as radiocarbon years before present (BP), “present" = AD 1950. Results greater than the modern reference are reported as percent modern carbon (pMC). The modern reference standard was 95% the 14C signature of NIST SRM-4990C (oxalic acid). Quoted errors are 1 sigma counting statistics. Calculated sigmas less than 30 BP on the Conventional Radiocarbon Age are conservatively rounded up to 30. d13C values are on the material itself (not the AMS d13C). d13C and d15N values are relative to VPDB-1. References for calendar calibrations are cited at the bottom of calibration graph pages. Page 3 of 35

No sub-contracting or student labor was used in the analyses. All work was done at Beta in 4 in-house NEC accelerator mass spectrometers and 4 Thermo IRMSs. The "Conventional Radiocarbon Age" was calculated using the Libby half -life (5568 years), is corrected for total isotopic fraction and was used for calendar calibration where applicable. The Age is rounded to the nearest 10 years and is reported as radiocarbon years before present (BP), “present" = AD 1950. Results greater than the modern reference are reported as percent modern carbon (pMC). The modern reference standard was 95% the 14C signature of NIST SRM-4990C (oxalic acid). Quoted errors are 1 sigma counting statistics. Calculated sigmas less than 30 BP on the Conventional Radiocarbon Age are conservatively rounded up to 30. d13C values are on the material itself (not the AMS d13C). d13C and d15N values are relative to VPDB-1. References for calendar calibrations are cited at the bottom of calibration graph pages. Page 15 of 35

Quality Assurance Report This report provides the results of reference materials used to validate radiocarbon analyses prior to reporting. Known-value reference materials were analyzed quasi-simultaneously with the unknowns. Results are reported as expected values vs measured values. Reported values are calculated relative to NIST SRM-4990B and corrected for isotopic fractionation. Results are reported using the direct analytical measure percent modern carbon (pMC) with one relative standard deviation. Agreement between expected and measured values is taken as being within 2 sigma agreement (error x 2) to account for total laboratory error. February 19, 2020 Mrs. Mercedes Cody SWCA Environmental Consultants 4407 Monterey Oaks Blvd Austin, TX 78749 United States RE: Radiocarbon Dating Results Dear Mrs. Cody, Enclosed are the radiocarbon dating results for three samples recently sent to us. As usual, the method of analysis is listed on the report with the results and calibration data is provided where applicable. The Conventional Radiocarbon Ages have all been corrected for total fractionation effects and where applicable, calibration was performed using 2013 calibration databases (cited on the graph pages). The web directory containing the table of results and PDF download also contains pictures, a cvs spreadsheet download option and a quality assurance report containing expected vs. measured values for 3-5 working standards analyzed simultaneously with your samples. Reported results are accredited to ISO/IEC 17025:2005 Testing Accreditation PJLA #59423 standards and all chemistry was performed here in our laboratory and counted in our own accelerators here. Since Beta is not a teaching laboratory, only graduates trained to strict protocols of the ISO/IEC 17025:2005 Testing Accreditation PJLA #59423 program participated in the analyses. As always Conventional Radiocarbon Ages and sigmas are rounded to the nearest 10 years per the conventions of the 1977 International Radiocarbon Conference. When counting statistics produce sigmas lower than +/- 30 years, a conservative +/- 30 BP is cited for the result. The reported d13C values were measured separately in an IRMS (isotope ratio mass spectrometer). They are NOT the AMS d13C which would include fractionation effects from natural, chemistry and AMS induced sources. When interpreting the results, please consider any communications you may have had with us regarding the samples. Our invoice has been sent separately. ## Page 267

FLOTATION SAMPLES AND RADIOCARBON MATERIAL FROM THE HARDEMAN MIDDEN SITE (41DN612), DENTON COUNTY, TEXAS SWCA #52105.01 Revised report (added discussion of chronology) February 12, 2020 Prepared for: Mercedes Cody, Archaeologist SWCA Environmental Consultants Building 1, Suite 110 4407 Monterey Oaks Blvd. Austin, TX 78749 Prepared by: Leslie L. Bush, Ph.D., R.P.A. Macrobotanical Analysis 12308 Twin Creeks Rd., B-104 Manchaca, Texas 78652 8-1


---

## Page 268

Macrobotanical Analysis, 7/11/20, p. 2 Thirty-two carbon samples and eighteen flotation samples from the Hardeman Midden Site (41DN612) were submitted for sorting, identification, interpretation, and recommendation of materials for radiocarbon dating. The site is characterized by burned rock concentrations with bone and mussel shell. The dearth of lithic debitage and charcoal suggested to the initial investigators that the deposits they examined did not represent hearths in primary context but rather discard piles from hearths constructed and used nearby (THC Site Form 2/29/2019). Additional investigations revealed that the site is a complex burned rock midden overlain by historic-age household and structural debris (Nielsen and Carpenter 2019:3). Investigations focused on the burned rock midden that, based on geoarchaeological evidence, includes components dating from the Middle Archaic to Late Prehistoric periods (Nielsen and Carpenter 2019:4). SITE SETTING The Hardeman Midden Site is located in southwestern Denton County, Texas between an old channel and an oxbow of Denton Creek. Denton Creek eventually flows into the Trinity River via the Elm Fork. Plants of such riparian zones tend to tolerate flooding and other disturbances better than their upland counterparts. A great many species may share the canopy in a floodplain forest, and “dominance is absent or poorly defined” (Lee 1945:163). Common trees of riparian areas in north-central Texas include elms (Ulmus spp.), hackberies (Celtis spp.), green ash (Fraxinus pennsylvanica), bur oak (Quercus macrocarpa), Shumard oak (Q. shumardii), pecan and other hickories (Carya spp.), roughleaf dogwood (Cornus drummondii), soapberry (Sapindus saponaria), and American beautyberry (Callicarpa americana) (Bezanson 2000:115). Floodplains also support useful vines such as grapes (Vitis spp.) and Virginia creeper (Parthenocissus quinquefolia). Plants with edible bulbs such as camas (Camassia spp.) and wild onions and garlic (Allium spp.) grow on the banks. Oxbows, sloughs, marshes, and other wetlands associated with Denton Creek would have offered plant resources such as cattail (Typha spp.) and aquatic tubers or rhizomes including arrowhead (Sagittaria spp.) and spatterdock (Nuphar lutea). Upland vegetation differs from riparian vegetation today, as it would have in the past. Denton County lies in the eastern portion of the Cross Timbers vegetation area, a belt of wooded lands between the Blackland Prairie to the east and the Fort Worth Prairie to the west. Vegetation protected from modern human impacts in the Cross Timbers today is an oak savannah of post oak and blackjack oak (Quercus stellata and Q. marilandica) with a mixed-grass understory characterized by by grasses such as little bluestem (Schizachyrium scoparius), Indiangrass (Sorghastrum nutans), side-oats grama (Bouteloua curtipendula), and 8-2


---

## Page 269

Macrobotanical Analysis, 7/11/20, p. 3 forbs (Bezanson 2000:115-116; Diggs et al. 1999:45-48; Gould 1962). Mean annual precipitation in Denton County during the period 1951-1980 was 33.5 inches (851 mm). Precipitation has a bimodal distribution with modest peaks in May and September. The frost-free season averages 226 days and runs from March 27 through November 8 (NFIC 1987:465-466). METHODS Radiocarbon samples were examined under a Leica S9i stereoscopic microscope at 6.1-55 X magnification. Materials were identified regardless of size or carbonization status for up to twenty-one specimens from each bag. Only three bags contained more than twenty specimens (Bags 54, 215, and 265). The additional specimens from these bags likely represent the same genus as the first twenty-one specimens. Plant parts were identified to the lowest possible taxonomic level by comparison to materials in the Macrobotanical Analysis comparative collection and through the use of standard reference works (e.g., Core et al. 1979; Hoadley 1990; InsideWood 2004; Panshin and de Zeeuw 1980; Wheeler 2011). Plant nomenclature follows the PLANTS Database (USDA, NRCS 2019). Taxon identification for wood fragments was obtained by snapping to reveal a clean transverse section. When necessary, tangential or radial sections were examined for ray seriation, presence of spiral thickenings, types and sizes of intervessel pitting, and other minute characteristics. After identification, specimens of each taxon were counted, labeled, recorded, and weighed on an Ohaus Scout II 200 x 0.01 g electronic balance. To retain suitability for radiocarbon dating, samples were placed on freshly washed glassware when not in specimen containers, and they were handled only with vinyl gloves and metal forceps. Contact with paper and other plant products was avoided. Flotation samples were processed at SWCA in a Flote-Tech machine (Dausman 1989, Hunter and Gassner 1998, Rossen 1999). They were sorted according to standard procedures in the Macrobotanical Analysis laboratory (Pearsall 2015). Each flotation light fraction was weighed on an Ohaus Scout II 200 x 0.01 g electronic balance before being size-sorted through a stack of graduated geologic mesh. All carbonized botanical materials that did not pass through the No. 10 mesh (2 mm square openings) were sorted under a Leica S9i stereozoom microscope at 6.1-55 X, then counted, weighed, recorded, and labeled. Gastropods, small rocks, and uncarbonized botanical material larger than 2 mm (usually rootlets) were weighed, recorded, and labeled. Materials that fell through 8-3


---

## Page 270

Macrobotanical Analysis, 7/11/20, p. 4 the 2 mm mesh were examined under a stereoscopic microscope at 6.1-55 X magnification for carbonized botanical remains that had not been previously identified in the 2 mm size fraction. Identifiable botanical materials were removed from residue, counted, weighed, recorded, and labeled. Uncarbonized macrobotanical remains other than rootlets (seeds, nut hull, and semi-carbonized wood) were recorded on a presence/absence basis on laboratory forms. Wood charcoal identification was attempted for up to twenty specimens larger than 2 mm from each flotation sample. Only one of the samples from 41DN612 yielded twenty wood charcoal specimens larger than 2 mm (Bag 257). For the remaining samples, all specimens larger than 2 mm were identified, and identification was attempted for progressively smaller fragments until either twenty fragments were identified or identification became impractical. Wood charcoal fragments were snapped to reveal a transverse section and examined under a stereoscopic microscope at 6.1-55 X magnification. When necessary, tangential or radial sections were examined for ray seriation, presence of spiral thickenings, types and sizes of intervessel pitting, and other characteristics. Botanical materials were identified to the lowest possible taxonomic level by comparison to materials in the Macrobotanical Analysis comparative collection and through the use of standard reference works (e.g., Core et al. 1979; Davis 1993; Hoadley 1990; InsideWood 2004; Martin and Barkley 1961; Musil 1963; Panshin and de Zeeuw 1980; Wheeler 2011). Plant nomenclature follows that of the PLANTS Database (USDA, NCRS 2019). RESULTS Radiocarbon material Identifications of material for possible radiocarbon dating are given in Table B.1. Fragments of a camas bulb were identified in Bag 112 (Figure B.2). The remainder of the material consisted of wood charcoal and soil. A fragment of slippery elm wood charcoal (Ulmus rubra) from Bag 215 is shown in Figure B.3. Some wood charcoal fragments were incompletely carbonized, usually partly or completely very dark brown rather than black in color. Bag 2 consisted of a wood fragment that was completely burned on one side and unburned on the other. Incompletely carbonized material may be associated with historic or modern activity in the site area, especially in samples from shallow depth. Incomplete carbonization is noted in Table B.1. 8-4


---

## Page 271

Macrobotanical Analysis, 7/11/20, p. 5 Flotation samples Uncarbonized plant materials other than rootlets are shown on a presence/absence basis in Table B.2. Archaeological plant materials recovered are given in Tables B.3 and B.4 by count and weight respectively. All flotation samples had an original soil volume of 3.0 liters. Uncarbonized and semi-carbonized (historic or modern) plant remains. Most uncarbonized plant parts in the samples appear in the form of rootlets that are clearly related to the modern vegetation at the site. Uncarbonized seeds (Table B.1) are a common occurrence on most archaeological sites, and they usually represent seeds of modern plants that have made their way into the soil either through their own dispersal mechanisms or by faunalturbation, floralturbation, or argilliturbation (Bryant 1985:51-52; Keepax 1977; Miksicek 1987:231-232). In all except the driest areas of North America, uncarbonized plant material on open-air sites can be assumed to be of modern origin unless compelling evidence suggests otherwise (Lopinot and Brussell 1982; Miksicek 1987:231). The seeds, leaves, and fruits at 41DN612 consist of grasses, weedy annuals, and pecan family nut hulls that appear to relate to the current vegetation. Semi-carbonized remains are also interpreted as historic or modern here. They consist of semi-carbonized wood found in Levels 1 and 2 of Units 1 and 2 and a fragment of nut hull found in the north wall of BHT A (Feature 2). The antiquity of a fully carbonized fragment of nut hull from this same context is therefore sufficiently doubtful that it is reported here with the historic and modern remains (Table B.1) rather than the ancient remains (Tables B.2 and B.3). The wood is of an unknown type that does not appear among the fully carbonized wood charcoal. Carbonized (ancient) plant remains. Overall density of carbonized plant remains was low (0.02 g/liter). Average density was the same for feature and non-feature contexts. Plant remains consisted of wood charcoal (0.81 g), bulb scale fragments, starchy fragments that may be bulb, tuber, or rhizome fragments, a small piece of bark, and two botanical fragments that could not be further identified. Bulbs and other geophytes. A bulb fragment identifiable as camas (Camassia spp.) was recovered from Unit 2 Level 2. (The camas fragments identified in radiocarbon samples were from Unit 1.) These may represent Atlantic camas 8-5


---

## Page 272

Macrobotanical Analysis, 7/11/20, p. 6 (Camassia scilloides) or prairie camas (C. angusta). Bulbs of the two species are similar, with the plants distinguished largely by the smaller scape height, fewer floral bracts, and earlier bloom period of Atlantic camas and the persistent flower stalks of prairie camas (G. Yatskievych 1999:503-504; K. Yatskievych 2000:297- 298). A bulb fragment identifiable as wild onion or garlic (Allium spp.) was recovered from Unit 1 Level 1. Drummond’s onion (A. drummondii) and meadow garlic (A. canadense) are the two most common species in the area today, but prairie onion (A. stellatum) and plains onion (A. perdulce) are also present (Kartesz 2015). Archaeologists have documented a long history of bulb exploitation in Texas, with camas bulbs at the Wilson-Leonard Site currently the oldest known, dating to approximately 8,000 years before present (Acuña 2006:57; Mehalchick et al. 2004:Table 8.18). The use of earth oven facilities to transform otherwise inedible geophytes into nutritionally optimal starches and sugars seems to be part of a continent-wide trend that began in the early Holocene (Thoms 2008; Wandschnider 1997). In addition to camas and wild onion, edible bulbs available in Denton County today include crow poison (Nothoscordum bivalve) and fawnlilies (Erythronium spp.) (Ogden 1991). It is not clear whether prairie celestials (Nemastylis geminiflora) or the rain lilies (Zephyranthes spp. [syn. Cooperia spp.] and Habranthus tubispathus) are edible. Death camas (Zigadenus nuttallii, syn. Toxicoscordion nuttallii) is emphatically not edible. Indeterminable starchy fragments found in seven sample may also represent bulbs, but the possibility of tubers, rhizomes, or even large, starchy seeds cannot be rule out. Edible tubers currently recorded for Denton County include Indian breadroot (Pediomelum hypogaeum), greenbrier (Smilax spp.), and groundnut (Apios americana) and the aquatic tubers of arrowhead and rhizomes of spatterdock (Kartesz 2015). The range of sunchoke (Helianthus tuberosus) may also have extended as far south as Denton County at times in the past. Wood charcoal. In keeping with the riparian nature of the site area, many types of wood were present in the samples. Identification was attempted for 148 wood charcoal fragments. Due to the small size of most fragments, only 101 could be identified to genus or species. Oaks (Quercus spp.; n=42) were the most numerous, making up 42% of the identified assemblage. Oak specimens that could be identified below the genus level were all white group oak (Quercus sect. Quercus; Figure B.4). Mulberry was the next most common wood (n=16; Figure B.5), followed by viburnum (Viburnum spp.; n=12), American beautyberry (n=13), and hickory/pecan (n=10). Plum/cherry (Prunus spp.), ash, juniper (Juniperus 8-6


---

## Page 273

Macrobotanical Analysis, 7/11/20, p. 7 spp.), and American elm (Ulmus americana) were represented by one or two specimens each. Wood charcoal is interpreted as fuel for hearths, ovens, or other cooking features. The common white group oak of the area, bur oak, has excellent coaling qualities and a specific gravity of 0.64 (Alden 1995:101). In general, the heat value of a wood is directly related to its specific gravity (Marcouiller and Anderson n.d.). Coaling properties, which are especially important in earth oven cooking, relate to the third stage of the burning process. After evaporation of within-cell moisture (first stage), wood is converted to charcoal (second stage, signified by flames). In the third stage, the glowing coals burn slowly, without flame, and can be left for hours without attention (Collier and Turner 1981, Marcouiller and Anderson n.d.). Mulberry has a specific gravity of 0.66 and produces excellent coals but has a reputation for throwing sparks (Kuhns and Schmidt 1998). Other woods recovered in the samples would also have been useful in fires. For example, softwoods such as juniper tend to ignite more easily than hardwoods (Collier and Turner 1981). Shrubs such as viburnum and beautyberry may have provided small-diameter woods that were also relatively easy to ignite. Chronology. Radiocarbon dates for the Hardeman Midden can be roughly assigned to two periods: the fifth through mid-eighth centuries of the common era, and the last three centuries prior to the common era. The earlier dates come from Feature 6 and the lower levels of Units 1 and 3 (Levels 7 and 9). Feature 5 falls in the later group of dates, at cal. A.D. 428-599 (Beta 550632). Material from Feature 2 has not yet been dated. Of the contexts where dates are available, carbonized bulb scale fragments are present only the younger deposits. Possibly, the earlier earth ovens were used to cook meat and items other than plant foods. Alternatively, later cooking events differentially destroyed the earlier bulb scale fragments, which are less common and more delicate than wood charcoal. SUMMARY Archaeological plant remains at Site 41DN612 were sparse (0.02 g/liter) and consisted primarily of wood charcoal and bulb scale fragments. Twenty-one bulb scale fragments, including some identifiable as camas and wild onion, were recovered from feature and unit level contexts. Thirteen types of wood charcoal were identified in the carbon and flotation samples. Wood is interpreted as fuel wood, probably associated with earth oven cooking of bulbs and other plant material. 8-7
